### ⭐ [Coherent room-temperature dipole synchronization in nanocavity sheets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06490v1)

**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **4/5** · 🔥 `driven-dissipative phase transition` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `superradiant laser` **2/5** · `Dicke superradiance` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

**Summary.** The research reports on achieving coherent dipole synchronization at room temperature using plasmonic nanocavity arrays. By combining experiments measuring spatial coherence with theoretical modeling of driven-dissipative dynamics, the authors demonstrate that phase locking occurs robustly despite high losses. This platform offers a novel route for studying quantum coherence in scalable, ambient photonic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it addresses driven-dissipative quantum phenomena (synchronization) in a solid-state, ambient environment. The distinction between this mechanism and traditional BEC/laser transitions provides new insights into open quantum systems dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research aims to demonstrate and characterize coherent dipole synchronization at room temperature within plasmonic nanocavity sheets, distinguishing this phenomenon from standard phase transitions.

**Main result.** The system achieves robust spatial coherence and synchronization despite high dissipation and incoherent pumping, exhibiting a unique crossover behavior rather than a sharp phase transition. The emission profile shows expansion into a 'halo' region at high pump powers.

**Method.** The study combines experimental measurements of spatial coherence ($g^{(1)}(R)$) using Michelson interferometry with theoretical modeling employing master equations and cumulant expansions.

**Model / system.** The platform is locally-ordered plasmonic nanogap 2D arrays containing organic dye emitters (Methylene blue). The dynamics are modeled using a tight-binding Hamiltonian coupled to the cavity modes, governed by a master equation incorporating pumping and decay rates.

**Key observables.** Spatial coherence $g^{(1)}(R)$, photon-photon correlations ($N_{nm}$), local emitter coherence ($\langle Z_n angle$), and emission intensity maps showing 'halo' formation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** High light-matter coupling ($g > 100 	ext{ meV}$), high photon loss ($\kappa > 100 	ext{ meV}$), and the critical pump power ($P_{th}$) marking regime changes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theoretical model relies on a second-order cumulant expansion, and the measurement of $g^{(1)}(R=0,	au=0)$ is noted to be an average over multiple nanogaps.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate setup details, intensity maps showing halo formation (Fig. 1), spatial coherence decay profiles using a Michelson interferometer (Fig. 2), nonlinear scaling of emission power vs. pump strength (Fig. 3), and theoretical correlation spreading ($N_{nnn}$, $C_{nn}$) (Fig. 4).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the physical system, detailing the governing master equation and measurement techniques, presenting results on coherence measurements and nonlinear scaling, and finally discussing advanced diagnostics like vortex detection to characterize the non-equilibrium nature of the synchronization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Plasmonic nanocavities enable the synchronization of spatially distant emissive dipoles through strong near-field coupling in sub-nm gaps. We report formation of a room-temperature synchronized dipole state in locally-ordered plasmonic nanogap 2D arrays under non-resonant continuous-wave pumping. Unlike lasers, photonic Bose-Einstein condensates, or exciton-polariton condensates, this system exhibits spatial coherence across the dipoles, while rapid radiative and non-radiative emission suppresses temporal photon coherence. A change of behaviour is observed with increasing pumping, marked by the spatial spread of g(1) coherence, but without spectral narrowing or directional emission. This driven-dissipative system exhibits fast temporal coherence decay and complex spatial correlations, offering a new platform for studying synchronization at room temperature. Combining ultralow mode volumes, high Purcell enhancement, and scalable ambient operation, it opens pathways for novel photonic and quantum technologies.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05837"></a>
### ⭐ [Odd-parity magnons in the Haldane-Hubbard model from topological exciton condensation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05837v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Johannes Knolle  
**Authors:** Rintaro Eto, Johannes Knolle  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05837v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work analyzes the Haldane-Hubbard model to show that topological excitons condense, driving the system into a novel odd-parity magnetic state. This resulting magnet exhibits characteristic f-wave magnon splitting, and the theory predicts how changes in electronic band topology govern the magnon's quantum properties.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit connection drawn between topological exciton condensation in an itinerant model and the emergence of topologically non-trivial magnons is highly relevant to understanding emergent quantum phenomena in correlated materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the collective excitations (excitons and magnons) and the mechanism driving the transition into an odd-parity magnet within the Haldane-Hubbard model.

**Main result.** The condensation of topological excitons drives the system into a collinear Néel state, realizing an odd-wave magnet characterized by f-wave magnon splitting. Furthermore, electron bandgap closing dictates changes in magnon topology and enhances spin splitting.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis using Time-Dependent Hartree–Fock (TDHF) and Random Phase Approximation (RPA) to calculate exciton and magnon spectra.

**Model / system.** The Haldane-Hubbard model is studied on a honeycomb lattice, incorporating nearest-neighbor ($t_1$) and next-nearest-neighbor hopping ($t_2$) with loop currents, alongside an onsite Hubbard repulsion ($U$).

**Key observables.** Dynamical spin structure factor $S(q, \omega)$, exciton Berry curvature ($	ext{BC}$), magnon dispersion relation, and topological invariants (Chern numbers).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Half-filled case; intermediate interaction regime ($U/t_1 = 4$); lattice geometry (honeycomb).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on mean-field descriptions (TDHF, RPA) to describe the excitonic condensation transition.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate $S(q, \omega)$ in paramagnetic and AFM phases, show exciton Berry curvature distribution, and map out electron band structures near phase boundaries.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops the theoretical framework by first establishing the model Hamiltonian, then using TDHF/RPA to calculate excitonic properties (including Chern numbers), demonstrating how exciton condensation drives the magnetic transition into the odd-parity magnon state, and finally analyzing the consequences of bandgap closing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Odd-wave magnets are the counterparts to even-wave altermagnets realizing odd-parity spin splitting. Normally discussed for noncollinear systems, they have recently been shown to appear in collinear magnetic states in the presence of loop currents. Here we study collective excitations of the paramagnetic and magnetic phase of the seminal Haldane-Hubbard model. We identify the existence topological excitons in the paramagnetic phase, and their condensation as the driving mechanism into the collinear Néel state. The latter realizes an odd-wave magnet with odd-parity magnons displaying a characteristic $f$-wave splitting. We further uncover that an electron bandgap closing ensures magnon bandgap closing causing a change in odd-parity magnon topology, as well as a drastically enlarged spin splitting. Our results establish the presence of topological excitons and odd-parity magnons in the Haldane-Hubbard, with potential realizations in Floquet-driven materials and cold atomic gases.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06259"></a>
### [Non-equilibrium thermodynamics of collapse models in the strongly non-Gaussian regime](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06259v1)

**Authors:** Pedro B. Melo, Pedro V. Paraguassú, Simone Artini, Gabriele Lo Monaco, Sandro Donadi, Mauro Paternostro  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06259v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **5/5** · 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium universality` **4/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper analyzes objective collapse models using Wigner phase-space methods to study their thermodynamics. It demonstrates that these systems reach a Non-Equilibrium Steady State rather than thermal equilibrium, and crucially shows that standard perturbative approximations fail under strong dissipation. The research confirms the thermodynamic consistency of the dissipative mechanism while quantifying non-Gaussian features.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly bridges quantum foundations (measurement problem/collapse models) with non-equilibrium statistical mechanics by providing rigorous quantitative tools to analyze irreversible dynamics far from equilibrium.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the non-equilibrium thermodynamics of objective quantum collapse models, specifically addressing how dissipative mechanisms affect system dynamics in strongly non-Gaussian regimes.

**Main result.** The system does not thermalize but settles into a Non-Equilibrium Steady State (NESS), and the asymptotic non-Gaussianity scales cubically with the dissipation parameter ($\delta \propto eta^3$).

**Method.** The analysis employs the Wigner phase-space formalism, utilizing advanced numerical techniques like pseudo-spectral simulation to overcome the limitations of perturbative expansions in highly dissipative regimes.

**Model / system.** The model concerns objective collapse mechanisms (like dDP and CSL) which modify standard quantum evolution via friction terms. The dynamics are tracked using the Wigner function evolution equation, moving beyond simple quadratic approximations.

**Key observables.** Wigner entropy production rate ($\Pi(t)$), non-Gaussianity measure ($\delta[ho]$), and time evolution of higher-order statistical moments (e.g., fourth moments).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dissipation parameter ($eta$), which dictates the strength of the friction mechanism, and the distinction between weak and strong non-Gaussian regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that standard collapse models predict unphysical energy heating; the analysis assumes the validity of thermodynamic consistency checks like positive entropy production.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the temporal evolution of marginal PDFs showing fat tails, compare simulation results against approximate methods (like Gram-Charlier expansion), and confirm the cubic scaling of non-Gaussianity with $eta$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the modified master equations in phase space to deriving moment equations. It then contrasts perturbative approximations (GC) with full numerical simulations, culminating in the determination of the NESS properties and thermodynamic consistency checks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Standard objective collapse models offer a unified approach to the quantum measurement problem but predict an unphysical, indefinite increase in the energy of the system. The dissipative Diósi-Penrose (dDP) model resolves this heating issue by introducing a linear friction mechanism. However, this modification induces complex, non-Gaussian phase-space dynamics. We rigorously establish the thermodynamic consistency of this friction mechanism -- extended to the CSL model -- across both weakly and strongly non-Gaussian regimes. Using the Wigner phase-space formalism, we go significantly beyond the quadratic approximation and, to bypass the failure of perturbative methods under strong dissipation, introduce a novel exact pseudo-spectral simulation approach. Our analysis reveals that the system subjected to the dDP mechanism does not thermalize, but rather settles into a non-equilibrium steady-state (NESS) where the asymptotic non-Gaussianity scales as the third power of the dissipation parameter $β$. By evaluating the Wigner entropy production, we confirm the thermodynamic validity of the model and demonstrate that highly sensitive information-theoretic quantities require exact numerical methods to accurately capture the key non-Gaussian tails of the distribution.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05767"></a>
### [Tight relation between the physical effects of a quantum measurement and the information gained about an observable](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05767v1)

**Authors:** Natsuki Ogo, Holger F. Hofmann  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05767v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **5/5** · 🔥 `measurement-induced transitions` **4/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

**Summary.** This paper develops a formal theory connecting information acquisition during quantum measurements to the physical disturbance experienced by the system. It derives that the probability of observable change (back action) is mathematically equivalent to $1$ minus the total update fidelity measure derived from the measured outcomes. This provides a tight, quantitative link between knowledge and physical effect in quantum measurement theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous theoretical framework for understanding the fundamental trade-off between measurement precision (information gain) and disturbance (back action) in quantum mechanics, which is central to quantum measurement theory and open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper establishes a precise, quantitative relationship between the information gained about one quantum observable ($\hat{A}$) during measurement and the resulting physical change (back action) on another observable ($\hat{B}$). It aims to resolve the conceptual difficulty of separating statistical knowledge updates from genuine dynamical state changes.

**Main result.** The probability of physical back action ($P(	ext{bout} 
eq \mathbf{b})$) is shown to be fundamentally linked to, and in some cases equal to, $1$ minus the total measurement update fidelity ($1 - F_{AM}$).

**Method.** The analysis employs Hilbert space formalism, utilizing Bayesian updates for information gain and Kraus operators/resolution matrices to quantify physical state evolution (back action).

**Model / system.** The study analyzes general quantum systems undergoing projective measurements of two observables ($\hat{A}$ and $\hat{B}$) starting from a pure input state $|\mathbf{b}angle$. The formalism is generalized to mixed states via purification.

**Key observables.** Back action probability ($P(	ext{bout} 
eq \mathbf{b})$), Update Fidelity ($F_{AM}$), Mutual Information ($I(A, M)$), and Intrinsic Robustness ($I_A(\mathbf{b})$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The relationship is governed by the structure of the resolution matrix $R(a, a')$, which quantifies correlations between measurement outcomes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis carefully distinguishes between statistical information updates (Bayesian) and physical state changes (back action), requiring careful handling within the Hilbert space framework.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the measurement process, deriving expressions for back action using Kraus operators, establishing identities linking back action to statistical fidelity measures ($P(	ext{bout} 
eq b) = 1 - F_{AM}$), and finally quantifying this relationship using resolution matrices.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The dynamics of quantum measurements defines a precise relation between the information gained about one physical property of a system and the observable changes in another physical property of the same system. Here, we express this relation in terms of the Hilbert space superpositions of the corresponding eigenstates and show how the probability of an observable physical change can be obtained from the Bayesian update of the probabilities associated with the information obtained in the measurement. Our analysis demonstrates that the superposition principle provides the tightest possible expression of the trade-off between information and back action in a quantum measurement.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06455"></a>
### [Breakeven demonstration of quantum low-density parity-check codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06455v1)

**Authors:** Edwin Tham, Michael L. Goldman, Shantanu Debnath, Ashay N. Patel, Jyothi Saraladevi, Jason Nguyen, Erik Nielsen, Neal Pisenti, Kenneth Wright, John Gamble, Nicolas Delfosse  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06455v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **5/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.06455_figures/2606.06455_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: A Ion trap depicted with six ions, with gates realized via counter-propagating Raman beams (green) steered from either side with acousto-optic deflectors (AOD). B An example syndrome circuit, measuring XXXX and ZZZZ stabilizers of the [[4, 2, 2]] code using two ancillae (top rails) prepared in |+⟩and measured in X. C Tanner graphs whose edges depict the highly non-trivial two-qubit gate connectivities required to implement syndrome circuits for a BB5 (left) and concatenated [[4, 2, 2]] (right) codes. D Sequence of physical operations implementing OMG-based mid-circuit measurements, with open (filled) circles representing the 0 (1) qubit state.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.06455_figures/2606.06455_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Survival probabilities 1 −εL for the BB5 and GB4 codes versus number of syndrome cycles, for X (left) and Z (right) eigenstate memory experiments. Circles and solid curves indicate the probability that no logical fault occurred in any of the encoded logical qubits. Diamonds and dashed curves indicate the corresponding probabilities without decoding. Error bars around each point depict the 95% Wilson confidence intervals for our estimate of εL, and shaded bands show 95% confidence bands around each fitted curve.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.06455_figures/2606.06455_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

Although not drawn on a toric surface, the boundaries of the solid and dashed squares are understood to wrap around along opposing edges to satisfy the toric boundary conditions.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.06455_figures/2606.06455_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Distribution of two-qubit (MS) gate noise as measured by DRB.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.06455_figures/2606.06455_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: Simultaneous two-qubit DRB results. Estimated MS gate error rate, aggregated over 36 runs, is shown as a function of qubit pair. In each run, simultaneous DRB is performed on the sets of 5, 4, 3, and then 2 disjoint pairs separated by the vertical dashed lines. An average error rate above the mean in Fig. 4 indicates the presence of crosstalk.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work demonstrates the feasibility of implementing advanced quantum error correction using qLDPC codes on a trapped-ion quantum computer. By leveraging a novel OMG architecture, the researchers achieved state-of-the-art logical error suppression and demonstrated breakeven performance for multiple code types. This marks a significant step toward building fault-tolerant quantum computers.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, high-fidelity demonstration of fault tolerance on trapped ions, directly addressing hardware limitations (like ion transport) that typically constrain QEC demonstrations in this modality.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Demonstrating high-rate quantum error correction (QEC) using qLDPC codes to achieve fault tolerance in a physical hardware platform.

**Main result.** The experiment successfully demonstrated nine different QEC codes, achieving logical error rates significantly lower than previous demonstrations and showing breakeven performance where logical lifetimes approach or exceed physical qubit lifetimes.

**Method.** Syndrome measurements are performed using controlled gates on ancilla qubits within a trapped-ion system, followed by decoding the syndrome data to estimate the logical error rate over multiple code cycles.

**Model / system.** The platform is a trapped-ion quantum computer utilizing $^{133}	ext{Ba}^+$ ions. The novel Optical-Metastable-Ground (OMG) architecture enables mid-circuit measurement and reset without ion transport, allowing for the implementation of various code families.

**Key observables.** Logical error rate ($\epsilon_L$), logical qubit lifetime ($T_{	ext{log}}$), physical coherence times ($T_1, T_2^*$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** High encoding rates (e.g., qLDPC codes), $9	imes$ improvement in error suppression compared to superconducting qubits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of post-selection is employed for error handling, and the analysis relies on modeling noise channels based on measured gate fidelities.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the physical setup (ion trap), the sequence of operations for mid-circuit measurement (MCM), and simulated performance curves comparing logical vs. physical lifetimes under varying noise conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the theoretical framework of qLDPC codes, describes the experimental implementation using the OMG architecture for MCM, presents measurements of error rates over multiple cycles, and concludes by analyzing lifetime scaling and fidelity improvements via remapping.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-rate quantum low-density parity-check (qLDPC) codes are a leading candidate for fault-tolerant quantum computing. They feature higher encoding rates than planar alternatives such as the surface code, but their implementation often entails significant hardware hurdles like the need for long-range couplers. We leverage the flexibility of a trapped-ion quantum computer to demonstrate nine quantum error-correcting codes with starkly different qubit connectivity requirements on a single device without any hardware reconfiguration. These experiments span three families of quantum error-correcting codes: qLDPC codes, topological codes, and concatenated codes. With a qLDPC code encoding 4 logical qubits into 18 physical qubits, we achieve a logical error rate up to $9\times$ better than a previous demonstration of a similar code on superconducting solid-state qubits. Moreover, our implementation exhibits breakeven performance, with some instances achieving qubit lifetimes comparable to or slightly exceeding that of our trapped-ion qubits. We use a novel implementation of the optical-metastable-ground (OMG) architecture for addressable mid-circuit measurement and reset, which enables us to perform these experiments without any ion transport or dedicated coolant ions, requirements that typically consume a large fraction of the runtime or ion count of trapped-ion quantum computers.

</details>

<sub>FIG. 4. Schematics and numerical results for the infinite-sized Dicke triangular lattice model are shown. Schematic illustrations show the Dicke triangular lattice model in (a) the normal phase and (b) the superradiant phase, with circle size and color indicating the amplitude and sign of the order parameter, respectively. In (a), the gray dashed line and black arrows represent the minimal unit cell and the lattice vector of the triangular lattice. In (b), the gray dashed line and black arrows denote the extended unit cell, which includes three sublattices, along with the lattice vector. The excitation energy band is shown relative to the critical coupling strength, using (c) the minimal...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.05278_figures/2606.05278_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Schematic and numerical results for the Dicke ladder model. (a) Three equivalent representations of the Dicke ladder model, where each gray circle corresponds to a Dicke model. The black arrow indicates the gauge field direction of the magnetic flux. (b) Excitation energy ϵk plotted in momentum space for various coupling strengths λ. (c) Schematic illustrations showing cases where the order parameter configuration is commensurate (top) and incommensurate (bottom) with the lattice structure.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper investigates superradiant phase transitions in frustrated Dicke lattice models. It demonstrates that geometric frustration leads to density-wave ordering and predicts the emergence of a Nambu-Goldstone mode near criticality by analyzing the excitation spectrum. This establishes a new theoretical framework for understanding symmetry breaking in coupled quantum emitter arrays.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges quantum optics (Dicke model) with fundamental condensed matter concepts like frustration and symmetry breaking, providing concrete theoretical predictions for emergent gapless modes in engineered light-matter arrays.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is understanding how geometric frustration and symmetry breaking govern collective behavior in quantum many-body systems, specifically focusing on superradiant phase transitions.

**Main result.** Frustration drives photonic density-wave ordering, leading to emergent Nambu-Goldstone modes near criticality, which can be predicted by analyzing the excitation spectrum rather than energy minimization.

**Method.** The work employs mean-field theory combined with a detailed analysis of quantum fluctuations around the mean-field solution, extending techniques from lattice Hamiltonians and Ginzburg-Landau frameworks.

**Model / system.** The primary model is arrays of coupled Dicke models on periodic lattices (1D and 2D). The Hamiltonian includes local light-matter coupling terms ($\lambda$) and hopping interactions ($J$), allowing for the study of superradiance in engineered quantum optical systems.

**Key observables.** Superradiant phase transition point, photonic density-wave ordering, Nambu-Goldstone mode emergence (gapless excitation), quasi-periodic order, and critical exponents ($\gamma_F$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strong light-matter coupling regime; lattice size parity ($N$ even vs. odd); synthetic magnetic flux ($\phi_{r,r'}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on approximating the system by analyzing the excitation spectrum of the symmetric phase to bypass computationally prohibitive energy minimization.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the model setup (Dicke lattice), show order parameter evolution across different coupling strengths ($\lambda$), and plot the excitation energy ($\epsilon$) revealing both standard mean-field scaling and anomalous, size-dependent critical exponents for odd $N$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the general Dicke lattice model to analyzing the 1D chain case by examining parity dependence (even vs. odd N), deriving the anomalous critical exponent, and finally generalizing the findings to show the emergence of a true Nambu-Goldstone mode in the thermodynamic limit.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding how frustration and symmetry breaking shape collective behavior is a central problem in quantum many-body systems. In this work, we investigate this problem in large one- and two-dimensional arrays of coupled Dicke models on a periodic lattice, where strong light-matter coupling gives rise to a superradiant phase and competition between neighboring order parameters induces spontaneous translational symmetry breaking. Such Dicke lattice models constitute a fundamentally new class of quantum many-body systems, as they simultaneously realize the thermodynamic limit associated with the lattice size and an intrinsic thermodynamic limit arising from collective on-site interactions with quantum emitters. We show that frustration drives photonic density-wave ordering, and that the resulting broken periodicity can be predicted from the excitation spectrum of the symmetric phase, without requiring computationally prohibitive thermodynamic energy minimization. Furthermore, we demonstrate that an emergent Nambu-Goldstone mode arises near the critical point in a one-dimensional chain despite the presence of only discrete symmetry, and uncover the mechanism that enables this otherwise forbidden gapless excitation. We also find quasi-periodic ordering in the superradiant phase, reminiscent of quasicrystals, and demonstrate that synthetic magnetic flux provides a powerful knob to control the nature of translational symmetry breaking. Our results establish a new direction in quantum many-body physics where the coexistence of local and global thermodynamic limits gives rise to unconventional symmetry breaking and emergent collective behavior.

</details>

### [Engineered dissipation for faster adiabatic state preparation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05815v1)

**Authors:** Yuanyang Zhou, Biao Wu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05815v1>

**Summary.** The paper introduces an engineered dissipative protocol that uses structured reservoirs to actively suppress nonadiabatic leakage during slow quantum state preparation. By continuously relaxing leaked population back to the ground state, it improves the required annealing time scaling from $O(\Delta^{-2})$ to $O(\Delta^{-1})$. This technique is shown to be robust and applicable to complex disordered systems like spin glasses.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses open quantum system dynamics in condensed matter contexts (like superconducting circuits or magnetic materials) by providing a concrete method to overcome fundamental limitations imposed by spectral gaps during adiabatic evolution.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Adiabatic state preparation is slowed by nonadiabatic leakage near small spectral gaps, especially in complex systems like spin glasses.

**Main result.** Engineered dissipation can improve the required annealing time scaling from $O(\Delta^{-2})$ to $O(\Delta^{-1})$, accelerating ground-state preparation significantly.

**Method.** The authors propose and analyze an engineered dissipative protocol using a filtered reservoir, modeled via the Lindblad master equation, which induces downward transitions while keeping the ground state dark.

**Model / system.** The analysis uses effective two-level models initially but generalizes to complex systems like the Transverse Field Ising Model (TFIM) and random spin glasses. The dynamics are governed by a master equation incorporating both unitary evolution and engineered dissipation terms.

**Key observables.** Final ground-state probability ($P_{GS}$), required annealing time threshold ($T_\epsilon$), excited-state population, and coherence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Minimum spectral gap ($\Delta$), engineered relaxation rate ($\Gamma$), thermal excitation ratio ($r = \gamma/\Gamma$), and system size ($N$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the Born-Markov approximation, secular approximations, and assumes that the engineered reservoir can effectively suppress nonadiabatic leakage.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare $P_{GS}$ vs. time/size for dissipative vs. closed-system protocols (TFIM); they also show robustness against finite temperature variations ($r$) and dependence on dissipation strength ($\Gamma$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from the general two-level model analysis to applying the technique to complex, disordered systems like spin glasses, systematically analyzing scaling laws and the impact of thermal noise.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Adiabatic state preparation is often slowed by nonadiabatic leakage near small spectral gaps. We propose an engineered dissipative protocol that uses a filtered reservoir to induce predominantly downward transitions in the instantaneous energy eigenbasis while leaving the instantaneous ground state dark. The leaked population generated by nonadiabatic driving is therefore continuously relaxed back toward the low-energy sector. An effective avoided-crossing analysis shows that in the regime where the engineered relaxation strength is much larger than the minimum gap, the runtime scaling can improve from the closed-system behavior $\mathcal{O}(Δ^{-2})$ to $\mathcal{O}(Δ^{-1})$ Finite-temperature upward transitions introduce a thermal error floor, but the enhancement survives when this heating rate remains below the target error tolerance. Numerical results show improved ground-state preparation over closed-system annealing. We also discuss a possible superconducting-circuit implementation using structured bosonic reservoirs.

</details>

### [Krylov Complexity: Flat bands and Carroll breaking deformations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05303v1)

**Summary.** The paper uses Krylov Complexity as a quantitative measure to probe the stability of Carrollian symmetries in flat band fermionic models. By analyzing quenches across different phases, it demonstrates that this complexity growth reveals how robustly protected these exotic symmetries are against external perturbations. This provides deep insight into non-equilibrium dynamics in strongly correlated materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses non-equilibrium dynamics and spectral properties in strongly correlated electron systems (flat band physics) using advanced quantum diagnostics like Krylov complexity.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how state dynamics in flat band systems, characterized by emergent Carrollian symmetries, evolve when subjected to perturbations that break these symmetries.

**Main result.** Krylov complexity proves to be a powerful diagnostic tool, quantitatively measuring the resilience and destruction of Carroll symmetry across different initial phases (Vanilla vs. Exotic).

**Method.** The analysis employs Krylov Complexity ($C_K(t)$) by projecting time evolution onto a dynamically generated orthonormal basis derived from the Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** The primary model is an interacting fermionic ladder Hamiltonian exhibiting flat bands, which are augmented by Carroll-breaking perturbations. A continuum scalar field theory with gradient deformation is also analyzed.

**Key observables.** Krylov Complexity ($C_K(t)$), Krylov Inverse Participation Ratio (KIPR), real-space spreading widths of excitons, and the initial growth rate coefficient $b_2^1$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling strength ($\lambda$), lattice parameters ($	au$, $V$), and the quench protocol defining the initial state (Vanilla vs. Exotic phase).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on short-time approximations for complexity growth, and in continuum limits, it assumes a double-scaling prescription to handle UV/IR mixing.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams defining different quantum phases (Vanilla/Exotic) and show the time evolution of Krylov complexity or real-space spreading profiles under perturbations.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the theoretical framework using flat bands and Carroll symmetry, then applying Krylov complexity to analyze quenches across critical lines in lattice models, and finally extending the diagnostic tool to continuum field theories exhibiting UV/IR mixing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Systems with flat band structures, when written in the language of Compact Localised States (CLS), have been shown to be explicitly invariant under supertranslation symmetries, making Carrollian symmetries inherently important for such systems. In this work, we explore the state dynamics of these systems, focusing on quenches induced by Carroll breaking perturbations, through the probe of Krylov (spread) Complexity. We specialise to Fermionic ladder Hamiltonians with all bands flat (ABF) scenario, augmented by a supertranslation preserving interaction, and discuss Krylov state complexity for quenches across critical lines. We further discuss how the growth of Krylov complexity sharply resolves the phase-dependent resilience of Carrollian sectors against delocalising perturbations. This is augmented by a complementary mechanism for Krylov growth in a continuum Carroll scalar field theory with a gradient deformation, which exhibits strong ultraviolet sensitivity (UV/IR mixing).

</details>

### [Energy-Modulated Time-Asymmetric Spontaneous Collapse: Forward-Backward Dynamics from Stochastic Ito Reversal and Bright Solitons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06452v1)

**Authors:** Ikechukwu C. Okoro, Mike O. Osiele, Godfrey E. Akpojotor  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06452v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `measurement-induced transitions` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper proposes an energy-modulated, time-asymmetric spontaneous collapse model for BECs by analyzing stochastic Ito reversal on the CQ-NLSE. It demonstrates that forward and backward dynamics yield fundamentally different nonlinear terms ($\gamma_b = 5\gamma_f$), confirming a structural quantum irreversibility quantified by a universal asymmetry parameter of $2/3$.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly tackles measurement problem phenomenology within a quantum gas context (BEC), providing quantitative predictions for irreversible collapse mechanisms distinct from standard symmetric models.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the fundamental problem of quantum irreversibility by developing a time-asymmetric spontaneous collapse framework derived from stochastic Ito field reversal.

**Main result.** It establishes that the forward and backward dynamics exhibit strong asymmetry, quantified by $\gamma_b = 5\gamma_f$ and a universal asymmetry parameter of $2/3$.

**Method.** The authors derive nonlinear stochastic differential equations (SDEs) using Ito calculus applied to the Cubic-Quintic Nonlinear Schrödinger Equation (CQ-NLSE) formalism.

**Model / system.** The model is applied to a quasi-one dimensional Bose-Einstein condensate (BEC) of attractive $^7	ext{Li}$ atoms, utilizing the CQ-NLSE as its mean-field description.

**Key observables.** Forward and backward soliton amplitudes ratio ($A_f/A_b \approx 1.870$), collapse operator growth ($\Gamma_f$ grows while $\Gamma_b$ decays), and the asymmetry parameter $\gamma_{ap} = 2/3$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Noise strength ($\eta$), local probability density ($|\Psi|^2$), excitation energy squared ($(\hbar\omega)^2$), and the universal asymmetry-coupling parameter (2/3).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the mean-field approximation, weak noise limits ($\eta \ll 1$), and an energy eigenvalue approximation for deriving the collapse operator $\Gamma$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate spatial profiles of forward/backward solitons (amplitude ratio), plot the distinct quintic coupling parameters ($\gamma_b = 5\gamma_f$), and show heat maps confirming temporal growth asymmetry in the collapse operator.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from deriving stochastic SDEs via Ito reversal, establishing the energy-driven collapse operator $\Gamma$, reducing to the CQ-NLSE mean-field limit, and finally analyzing the resulting time-asymmetric dynamics using soliton solutions and heat map diagnostics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a rigorous theoretical framework for symmetry breaking and quantum irreversibility arising from stochastic Ito field reversal within a cubic-quintic nonlinear Schrodinger equation (CQ-NLSE) formalism. Starting from three physically motivated considerations, forward and backward nonlinear stochastic differential equations are derived via the Ito calculus. Kinematic time-reversal is shown to be fundamentally incompatible with the Ito stochastic structure, yielding the universal asymmetry-coupling parameter of 2/3. An energy-driven collapse operator proportional to the product of noise strength, local probability density, and excitation energy squared is introduced, amplifying the collapse in high-density, high-excitation regions. Exactly bright soliton solutions are obtained for a quasi-one-dimensional BEC of attractive Li-7 atoms, with forward and backward amplitude ratio of 1.870. Heat map analysis of the parameter planes reveals that the forward collapse operator grows monotonically in time while the backward counterpart decays, achieving a ratio approximately 1030, sharply distinguishing this framework from conventional symmetric collapse models.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper analyzes non-equilibrium thermodynamics for a qubit coupled to a structured environment. It demonstrates that memory effects, stemming from the finite nature of the bath (harmonic oscillator), cause deviations from Markovian predictions. These deviations are quantified by analyzing the time evolution and potential sign changes in the entropy production rate.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it tackles the fundamental limitations of standard Markovian descriptions in open quantum systems, providing quantitative tools ($\sigma\Sigma(t)$) to detect and characterize non-Markovian energy flow.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates non-equilibrium quantum thermodynamics for open systems, specifically analyzing how memory effects influence work, heat, and entropy production beyond the standard Markovian approximation.

**Main result.** Non-Markovian dynamics, evidenced by backflow of information (e.g., negative entropy production rate $\sigma\Sigma(t)$), significantly shape the system's thermodynamic response compared to Markovian limits.

**Method.** The analysis employs quantum master equations and diagnostic tools like the BLP measure and the time evolution of entropy production ($\sigma\Sigma(t)$) to quantify non-Markovianity.

**Model / system.** The model is a two-level system (qubit) coupled to a composite environment consisting of a harmonic oscillator and a thermal bath, driven by an external field. The dynamics are governed by a Lindblad-like master equation.

**Key observables.** Work rate ($\dot{W}$), heat rate ($\dot{Q}$), entropy production rate ($\sigma\Sigma(t)$), and the trace distance $D(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strengths ($J$, $\Gamma$), driving frequency ($\omega_D$), and the regime comparison between Markovian ($J=0$) and non-Markovian ($J>0$) dynamics.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The weak-coupling regime is assumed, and the analysis often relies on deriving effective equations of motion by tracing out environmental degrees of freedom.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate time evolution plots showing population decay, coherence damping, and crucially, the non-monotonic or negative behavior of $\sigma\Sigma(t)$ when memory effects are present.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theoretical framework by defining thermodynamic rates (Work/Heat) and then analyzes the dynamics using both quantum information measures (BLP distance) and thermodynamic diagnostics ($\sigma\Sigma(t)$), comparing results across different coupling strengths to isolate the role of memory.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show the emergence of memory effects in the dynamics of a driven two-level system interacting with a composite environment, and analyze their influence on work, heat and entropy production. We further investigate how the interplay between driving, dissipation and memory effects, stemming from the finiteness of the environment, shapes the thermodynamic response of the system, thus providing insight into quantum thermodynamics beyond the Markovian approximation.

</details>

### [Broadband AC Magnetic Field Sensing via Continuous wave optically detected magnetic resonance with NV Centers in diamond](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05928v1)

**Authors:** Ryohei Dokai, Ryusei Okaniwa, Miku Ishizaki, Junko Ishi-Hayase, Yuichiro Matsuzaki

**Summary.** The authors propose a novel broadband AC magnetometry scheme utilizing NV centers in diamond and Continuous Wave Optically Detected Magnetic Resonance (CW-ODMR). By employing microwave-driven dressed states, they theoretically and numerically demonstrate the ability to detect magnetic fields up to 100 MHz. This overcomes the severe bandwidth restrictions of conventional CW-ODMR methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses open quantum systems dynamics in a solid-state platform (NV centers) by engineering the system Hamiltonian via microwave driving to overcome inherent physical bandwidth limitations, which is highly relevant for quantum sensing applications.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Conventional CW-ODMR AC magnetic field sensing suffers from a severely limited detection bandwidth, typically restricted to only a few MHz.

**Main result.** The proposed scheme using microwave-driven dressed states enables the detection of AC magnetic fields up to the order of 100 MHz, significantly expanding the operational range.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis and numerical simulations were used to model the system dynamics governed by a Lindblad master equation under microwave driving.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the Nitrogen-Vacancy (NV) center in diamond. The dynamics are modeled using a Hamiltonian ($\hat{H}$) and solved via the Lindblad master equation, incorporating optical pumping transitions.

**Key observables.** Fluorescence intensity drop (dip) measured as the time-averaged occupation probability of the $|0angle$ state ($P_0$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The achievable bandwidth is determined by tuning the Rabi frequency ($\lambda_D$), which can reach $\sim 100$ MHz.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Approximations include assuming ideal projective measurement initially, and later accounting for measurement noise; the Rotating-Wave Approximation (RWA) is used but verified up to high frequencies.

**Figures summary.** Figures demonstrate dips in $P_0$ at predicted resonance frequencies, show that increasing the control field strength ($\lambda_D$) broadens the dip spacing toward 100 MHz, and quantify the achievable sensitivity ($30 \mu	ext{T}/\sqrt{	ext{Hz}}$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying the bandwidth limitation of conventional CW-ODMR to proposing a microwave-driven dressed state scheme. It details the theoretical Hamiltonian and master equation, derives the resonance condition ($\omega_T = \sqrt{2E \pm \lambda_D^2}$), and validates the broadband capability through numerical simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The nitrogen-vacancy (NV) center in diamond has attracted considerable attention as a highly sensitive quantum sensor that can operate at room temperature. In particular, continuous-wave optically detected magnetic resonance (CW-ODMR) is promising for a wide range of applications because of its simplicity. However, conventional AC magnetic-field sensing schemes based on CW-ODMR suffer from a limited detection bandwidth: the detectable frequency is either fixed by intrinsic physical parameters of the NV center or, even when tunable, restricted to a narrow range of only a few MHz. Here, we propose a broadband AC magnetometry scheme based on CW-ODMR with NV centers using microwave-driven dressed states.Through theoretical analysis and numerical simulations, we show that the proposed scheme enables the detection of AC magnetic fields with frequencies up to the order of 100 MHz, which has been difficult to achieve using conventional CW-ODMR-based methods.

</details>

### [Emergent Self-Similar Quantum Revivals in Spiral Drives](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05288v1)

**Summary.** The paper introduces the concept of self-similar quantum revivals as a novel form of temporal order in non-equilibrium quantum matter. By analyzing systems driven by quasiperiodic spiral kicks, the authors prove that both fidelity and entanglement entropy exhibit hierarchical recurrence patterns. This work establishes a new theoretical framework for understanding memory retention and dynamics far from equilibrium.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides concrete theoretical predictions about emergent temporal order in driven many-body systems, offering testable signatures (self-similar revivals) that can be sought in current quantum simulators using superconducting qubits or trapped ions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To investigate whether self-similarity can emerge as a fundamental organizing principle of far-from-equilibrium quantum matter in the time domain.

**Main result.** The authors uncovered a distinct form of nonequilibrium temporal order: self-similar quantum revivals, which are robustly observed in both fidelity and entanglement entropy for systems driven by quasiperiodic spiral kicks.

**Method.** The analysis combines analytical proofs using quasiperiodic cocycles over SU(2) with numerical simulations tracking state fidelity and bipartite entanglement entropy across different driving parameters.

**Model / system.** The model is a many-body system (Ising chain) subjected to time evolution governed by quasiperiodic spiral kicks, which are constructed from an arbitrary non-diagonal SU(2) matrix combined with $\sigma_z$ gates. The dynamics are analyzed in momentum space.

**Key observables.** State fidelity $F(n)$, half-chain bipartite Entanglement Entropy $S_{L/2}(n)$, and the convergence of the product of transfer matrices to a period-6 cycle.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The quasiperiodic driving angle $\varphi$ (related to the golden ratio), perturbation strength ($	heta_z$), and entanglement thresholds ($S$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The mathematical derivations often rely on assuming that the underlying quasiperiodic cocycle is reducible, which simplifies the analysis significantly.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the revival structure in $F(n)$ and $S_{L/2}(n)$ at specific times ($n=F^{3m}$), the convergence of momentum mode trajectories to an attractor, and the scaling of prethermal lifetime $	au$ with perturbation strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the general concept of self-similar revivals in integrable settings, providing analytical justifications via cocycles, characterizing entanglement growth regimes (area/volume law), and finally demonstrating robustness against generic integrability-breaking perturbations to establish a new paradigm for non-equilibrium quantum matter.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We uncover a distinct form of nonequilibrium temporal order: self-similar quantum revivals in a many-body system driven by quasiperiodic spiral kicks, where the system recurrently returns close to its initial state at a hierarchically nested sequence of times. We demonstrate that both the fidelity and entanglement entropy exhibit this self-similar temporal structure. It originates from an emergent dynamical attractor, which we identify, such that all momentum modes eventually fall into the same closed orbits at self-similar times. We analytically justify this behavior and show that, for special momentum modes, this attractor arises as a consequence of a generalized spin echo process, and more generally we prove its existence using quasiperiodic SU(2) cocycles. Interestingly, the dynamics between consecutive revivals supports either volume- or area-law entanglement scaling, tunable via the driving parameters. In the presence of integrability-breaking perturbations, the system eventually heats up, but a long-lived prethermal regime with algebraically tunable lifetime occurs before heating sets in. Our results establish self-similar quantum revivals as a new paradigm for nonequilibrium quantum matter and provide a realistic route for its observation in current quantum simulators.

</details>

### [Enhancement of charge correlations and real-space topological marker on an interacting non-Hermitian Su-Schrieffer-Heeger model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06466v1)

**Summary.** This paper analyzes the interacting non-Hermitian SSH model to understand how charge ordering competes with topological phases. Using real-space markers and correlation functions, the authors show that interactions enhance charge correlations near exceptional points under open boundary conditions. The findings provide a detailed picture of topology breakdown driven by electronic instabilities.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges concepts from topology (non-Hermitian physics) with strong correlation effects (CDW formation) in a 1D lattice model, which is highly relevant for understanding correlated electronic states in synthetic quantum materials or open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the complex interplay between topological order, charge correlations, and interaction effects within a non-Hermitian quantum model.

**Main result.** Non-Hermiticity enhances interaction effects, leading to pronounced amplification of staggered charge correlations near exceptional points under open boundary conditions. The real-space topological marker remains robust in diagnosing phase transitions despite interactions.

**Method.** The authors employ Exact Diagonalization (ED) and mean-field treatments on the interacting non-Hermitian Su-Schrieffer-Heeger model to map out the phase diagram.

**Model / system.** The system is a one-dimensional, interacting non-Hermitian Su-Schrieffer-Heeger (SSH) model. The Hamiltonian includes nearest-neighbor hopping terms with non-reciprocity ($\delta$) and an interaction term ($V$).

**Key observables.** Real-space topological marker ($W$), charge structure factor ($S_{	ext{cdw}}$), many-body energy gaps ($	ext{Re}(E_{	ext{gap}}), 	ext{Im}(E_{	ext{gap}})$), and local density profiles ($\langle n_i angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength ($V$), non-reciprocity parameter ($\delta$), boundary conditions (Periodic vs. Open), and proximity to exceptional points.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on defining the ground state eigenpair using Krylov-Schur diagonalization, and careful consideration is given to eigenvector choices (left vs. right) to avoid artifacts.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams showing $W$, $S_{	ext{cdw}}$, and energy gaps as functions of interaction strength ($V$) and hopping parameters ($\delta$), comparing PBC and OBC results.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the model using multiple diagnostic tools: first establishing the topological marker's robustness, then mapping out phase transitions (Topo-I $\leftrightarrow$ CDW), and finally detailing how non-Hermiticity enhances correlations near exceptional points under OBC.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the interacting non-Hermitian Su-Schrieffer-Heeger (SSH) model, focusing on the interplay between topology and charge ordering. Using a real-space topological marker, charge correlations, and the complex many-body spectrum, we map out the phase diagram under periodic and open boundary conditions. We show that the topological marker remains a robust diagnostic of non-Hermitian topological phases in the presence of interactions and consistently signals their breakdown at the onset of a charge density wave (CDW). We further demonstrate that non-Hermiticity enhances interaction effects: While moderate changes occur under periodic boundary conditions, open boundary conditions lead to a pronounced amplification of staggered charge correlations near exceptional points. This enhancement arises from the accumulation of low-energy states near exceptional points, which promotes electronic instabilities and strengthens CDW tendencies.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05293"></a>
### [Breakdown of Thermalization from Real-Time Dynamics in the Two-Dimensional Hubbard Model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05293v1)

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper investigates how strongly correlated electrons in the 2D Hubbard model approach equilibrium after a sudden change in interaction strength. Using advanced Neural Network Quantum States within tVMC simulations, the authors find that while thermalization occurs at weak coupling, strong interactions cause the system dynamics to fundamentally deviate from expected thermal behavior, signaling a breakdown of ergodicity.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses fundamental questions in non-equilibrium quantum dynamics, which is crucial for understanding energy relaxation and transport properties in materials like cuprate superconductors.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether thermalization occurs during the real-time evolution of a strongly correlated fermionic system after being quenched out of equilibrium.

**Main result.** The dynamics exhibit two regimes: for weak interactions, the system thermalizes; however, beyond a critical interaction strength ($U_C$), the time evolution deviates significantly from thermal predictions, indicating a breakdown of thermalization.

**Method.** Time-dependent Variational Monte Carlo (tVMC) methods are employed, utilizing Neural-Network Quantum States (NQS) based on transformer architectures to simulate non-equilibrium dynamics. The infinite-time limit is extracted using the Linear Variational Method (LVM).

**Model / system.** The system modeled is the two-dimensional Hubbard model, a canonical framework for studying correlated electrons relevant to high-temperature superconductivity.

**Key observables.** Double occupancy ($\langle\hat{d}angle(t)$), comparison between long-time dynamic values ($\langle\hat{d}angle_\infty$) and thermal ensemble predictions ($\langle\hat{d}angle_{	ext{th}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength ($U$), critical interaction $U_C$, lattice size (2D square lattice), and simulation time/ramping protocol.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of tVMC with NQS provides an accurate description of the non-equilibrium dynamics, and LVM is used to extrapolate the long-time limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the time evolution of double occupancy ($\langle\hat{d}angle(t)$) for varying interaction strengths ($U$), contrasting tVMC results against thermal expectations and showing clear deviations above a critical $U$.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the physical problem (thermalization in Hubbard model), details the non-equilibrium protocol (interaction quench), presents advanced computational methods (tVMC/NQS/LVM), analyzes key observables ($\langle\hat{d}angle$), and concludes by mapping out the phase diagram regarding thermalization breakdown.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Thermalization in strongly correlated fermionic systems remains a central open problem in quantum many-body physics. In this work, we investigate the real-time dynamics and the approach to thermalization in the two-dimensional Hubbard model, a paradigmatic framework for correlated electrons, relevant to high-temperature superconductivity and ultracold quantum simulation. Focusing on the half-filled square lattice, we monitor the time evolution of the double occupancy following a quench in the on-site interaction $U$, and assess whether its long-time value is captured by a canonical thermal ensemble. We employ time-dependent variational Monte Carlo methods combined with transformer-based Neural-Network Quantum States to accurately describe the nonequilibrium dynamics of fermions, especially for the behavior at long times, thereby accessing regimes that were previously inaccessible to numerical simulations. Our results reveal two distinct dynamical behaviors: for weak to intermediate interactions, the long-time double occupancy agrees with the thermal prediction, consistent with ergodic relaxation; beyond a critical interaction $U_{C}$, the dynamics deviate markedly from the thermal expectation, revealing clear signatures of thermalization breaking. These results establish numerical simulation as a powerful tool to probe nonequilibrium quantum phenomena in correlated fermionic matter.

</details>

### [A closed system setting for quantum thermalisation in free fermions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06362v1)

**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper analyzes quantum thermalization in a closed system mimicking a temperature quench using free-fermion models. By calculating the Frobenius distance via Generalized Hydrodynamics, the authors rigorously prove that the Mpemba effect does not occur under these specific boundary-driven conditions. This provides an analytical characterization of relaxation dynamics for this class of integrable systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides rigorous analytical tools (GHD, correlation functions) to study non-equilibrium relaxation phenomena like the Mpemba effect in integrable quantum systems, which is central to modern statistical mechanics and open quantum systems theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates quantum thermalization dynamics, specifically testing for the occurrence of the Mpemba effect in a closed quantum system undergoing a temperature quench.

**Main result.** The analysis concludes that no Mpemba effect arises in this boundary-driven thermalization setting for free-fermion models. Relaxation is fully characterized analytically without anomalous acceleration.

**Method.** The authors use the Frobenius distance between the time-evolved reduced density matrix and its stationary state, employing Generalized Hydrodynamics (GHD) to derive analytical predictions in the hydrodynamic limit.

**Model / system.** The system is a tripartite geometry involving a finite central chain connected on both sides to two semi-infinite chains acting as thermal baths. Two free-fermion models are analyzed: the XX chain and the transverse-field Ising (TFIC) chain.

**Key observables.** Normalized Frobenius distance, two-point correlation functions, local energy density profile, and the condition for the Mpemba effect.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature difference ($eta_s$ vs $eta_b$), system size ($\ell$), and time ($t$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The dynamics are treated within the free-fermion approximation, keeping the density matrices Gaussian. The hydrodynamic limit is utilized for analytical tractability.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the tripartite quench setup and show the time evolution of the Frobenius distance, demonstrating how it approaches equilibrium without exhibiting anomalous acceleration.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically introduces the tripartite quench setup, applies the methodology to the XX chain, then extends the analysis to the TFIC chain, culminating in a general conclusion regarding the absence of the Mpemba effect for this class of protocols.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study thermalisation and the possible occurrence of the Mpemba effect in a closed quantum setting that mimics the interaction of a system with thermal reservoirs coupled only at its boundaries. Specifically, we consider a tripartite geometry in which a finite chain, initially prepared at a finite temperature, is suddenly connected on both sides to two semi-infinite chains of the same nature held at a different temperature. These outer chains act as thermal baths, while the full system evolves unitarily under a homogeneous Hamiltonian. This setup provides a simple quantum realisation of a temperature quench and closely resembles the original scenario in which the classical Mpemba effect was first observed. We focus on two paradigmatic free-fermion models, the XX chain and the transverse-field Ising chain, which respectively preserve and break the global $U(1)$ particle-number symmetry. As a probe of relaxation, we consider the Frobenius distance between the time-evolved reduced density matrix of the central subsystem and its stationary state, which is the thermal state at the bath temperature. Exploiting the free-fermionic structure of both models, the dynamics remains Gaussian and the Frobenius distance can be expressed exactly in terms of two-point correlation functions. Combining this representation with generalised hydrodynamics, we derive analytical predictions for the Frobenius distance in the hydrodynamic limit, providing a complete characterisation of the thermalisation process. Using these results, we investigate the possible occurrence of the Mpemba effect. We find that, despite the genuine non-equilibrium dynamics displayed by the system, no Mpemba effect arises in this setting. Our analysis identifies a broad class of boundary-driven thermalisation protocols in which relaxation is fully characterised analytically and exhibits no anomalous acceleration of equilibration.

</details>

### [A granular Büttiker-Landauer motor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05913v1)

**Authors:** Olivier Devauchelle, Predrag Popović, Piotr Szymczak, Anaïs Abramian, Arnaud Lazarus

**Summary.** The paper experimentally demonstrates a 'granular Büttiker-Landauer motor' using bouncing sand grains. It shows that particle accumulation deviates from classical diffusion laws when diffusivity varies spatially. By applying both a temperature gradient and an external force, the system generates a measurable, non-equilibrium steady current powered solely by noise.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides an accessible, macroscopic realization of fundamental non-equilibrium thermodynamic principles (like those governing Brownian motors), which are crucial concepts for understanding energy transduction far from equilibrium, relevant to open quantum systems dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research addresses discrepancies between classical diffusion theory (Fick's law) and experimental observations, aiming to realize a 'granular Büttiker-Landauer motor.' It seeks to show how non-uniform energy landscapes can drive particle currents powered only by noise.

**Main result.** The experiment confirms that grain density accumulates inversely proportional to local diffusivity ($ho \propto 1/D$), and the system generates a measurable steady current when both a temperature gradient and an external force are applied.

**Method.** The study combines macroscopic experiments using vibrating granular sand on patterned substrates with theoretical analysis based on modified flux equations derived from non-equilibrium statistical mechanics.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is quartz sand grains bouncing on a disk, where the substrate diffusivity ($D$) can be varied by embedding soft elastomer pads. The underlying physics models particle transport in non-uniform temperature fields, governed by generalized Smoluchowski or Fokker-Planck type equations.

**Key observables.** Grain density ($ho$), diffusion coefficients ($D_{	ext{hard}}, D_{	ext{soft}}$), and the resulting steady-state particle current ($j$) or angular momentum ($M$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The ratio of diffusivities ($D_{	ext{soft}}/D_{	ext{hard}}$) and the dimensionless parameter $eta_0 f R$ (related to temperature gradient and external force).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key assumptions include applying an alternative flux equation ($\mathbf{j} = -
abla(Dho)$) and assuming that particle interactions are negligible during the hopping process.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental setup, show steady-state density maps (Fig. 2a), plot variance vs. time to extract $D$ (Fig. 2b), and demonstrate current flow streamlines in simulations under external force (Fig. 3).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the failure of Fick's law using simple density accumulation experiments, to developing a more complex model incorporating external forces and temperature gradients, culminating in measurements of directed particle currents.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Random walkers usually diffuse according to Fick's law. On average, they move down the gradient of their concentration and, in the absence of external force, tend to distribute themselves uniformly. In some experiments, however, this familiar notion is at odds with observation. Sand grains, for instance, gather along the nodal lines of a vibrated elastic plate to form a Chladni figure, thus accumulating where fluctuations are weak -- a fact that escapes the reach of Fick's law. On theoretical grounds, Büttiker [Zeitschrift für Physik B, 68, 1987] and Landauer [J Stat Phys, 53, 1988] proposed that particles submitted to a non-uniform temperature field would indeed gather where the temperature is low. They also predicted that, in the presence of a potential force, a non-uniform temperature could drive a steady current of particles, powered only by noise. Here, we present an experimental realization of these phenomena in a macroscopic system, which confirms the quantitative predictions of Büttiker.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** The paper establishes that topological features within bipartite fermionic lattices can induce intrinsic Charge-Conjugation Violation (CCV) without external symmetry breaking. This microscopic mechanism manifests macroscopically as a population imbalance between particle and anti-particle excitations. The findings are highly relevant for experimental realization using cold-atom quantum simulators.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses emergent symmetry breaking mechanisms in lattice models, which is highly relevant for understanding non-equilibrium phenomena in ultracold atomic quantum simulators used to model strongly correlated systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how intrinsic Charge-Conjugation Violation (CCV) can emerge in quantum many-body systems without relying on explicit symmetry breaking mechanisms.

**Main result.** Intrinsic CCV is shown to arise from the graph topology of bipartite fermionic lattices, leading macroscopically to a measurable population asymmetry between kink and anti-kink excitations.

**Method.** The authors use an effective Hamiltonian approach derived from mapping the system onto sublattice kinks (topological defects) and analyzing its spectral properties under quench dynamics.

**Model / system.** The model is based on bipartite fermionic lattices containing quasiparticles called 'sublattice kinks' (SKs), governed by a Fermi-Hubbard type Hamiltonian restricted to specific Hilbert subspaces.

**Key observables.** Population asymmetry ($\delta n = n_K - n_{ar{K}}$), hidden leaf-like structure in the eigenenergy spectrum, and imbalanced sublattice-kink production during quench dynamics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Chemical potentials ($\mu_K$, $\mu_{ar{K}}$) and external boundary tilts ($h$) used to tune the asymmetry; lattice geometry (bipartite vs. ladder).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on mapping the system dynamics to an effective kink picture, sometimes requiring truncation of the Hilbert space or assuming non-interacting spinless fermions in certain limits.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematics of CCV in SKs (Fig. 1), show the leaf-like structure in the eigenenergy spectrum related to asymmetry (Fig. 2), and demonstrate control over K-$ar{K}$ imbalance via bi-quench protocols (Fig. 3).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the general problem of intrinsic CCV, developing the model using kink excitations on bipartite lattices, demonstrating measurable asymmetry in static spectra and quench dynamics, and finally extending the analysis to include doublon/holon physics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Charge conjugation violation (CCV) is a central concept in particle physics and appears also for quasiparticles in quantum many-body systems, which typically relies on an embedded external symmetry breaking to the underlying system. An open question is how an intrinsic CCV mechanism could emerge and what its macroscopic consequences would be. We establish sublattice kinks in bipartite fermionic lattices as a concrete setup showing intrinsic CCV. The intrinsic CCV of the sublattice kink is based on the graph-topological nature of the underlying Hamiltonian, with no explicit symmetry breaking taking place. It leads to a population asymmetry of different configurations and imprints a hidden leaf-like structure in the eigenenergy spectrum. The population asymmetry also leads to an imbalanced sublattice-kink production triggered by the vacuum-instability in the quench dynamics. Our work demonstrates the graph topology as the microscopic origin of intrinsic CCV, with the population asymmetry as the macroscopic consequence, of which the proposed setup is highly amenable to experimental implementation via cold-atom quantum simulators.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.05930"></a>
### [Thermodynamics of bouncing grains](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05930v1)
