**Summary.** This research uses bouncing sand grains on vibrating plates as a model system to study non-equilibrium diffusion. The authors demonstrate that when temperature gradients are present, the standard Fick's law fails, requiring an advanced stochastic calculus approach (the Itô process) for accurate description. They successfully measure and model the heat flux associated with this unique steady state.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides a tangible, mesoscopic physical realization of complex statistical mechanics concepts—like distinguishing between different types of random walks (Itô vs. Fickian)—using simple mechanical systems, which has parallels to understanding energy dissipation and transport in open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates diffusion dynamics of bouncing sand grains on a vibrating plate, specifically testing whether classical Fick's law or alternative stochastic models (like the Itô process) correctly describe non-equilibrium transport.

**Main result.** When temperature gradients are present, the system deviates from Fick's law; experiments support a new transport relation derived from an Itô random walk model. The heat flux associated with this non-equilibrium steady state is successfully measured and modeled.

**Method.** The authors combine experimental tracking of individual grain trajectories on vibrating substrates with advanced stochastic calculus (Ito vs. anti-Ito integrals) to derive macroscopic transport equations.

**Model / system.** The system consists of small particles bouncing on a vibrating, potentially heterogeneous substrate. The motion is modeled as a random walk process whose statistics are analyzed using concepts from non-equilibrium thermodynamics and stochastic calculus.

**Key observables.** Diffusivity (D), Root Mean Square velocity ($	ext{v}_{	ext{rms}}$), Heat Flux ($\dot{Q}$), and the relaxation parameter ($\gamma$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Vibration frequency ($
u$), tilt angle ($\phi$), substrate heterogeneity, and temperature gradient ($\Delta T/T$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the dynamics can be accurately described by specific stochastic integrals (favoring Itô over anti-Ito) and that the system reaches a non-equilibrium steady state.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate experimental setups, grain trajectories showing random walk behavior, velocity distributions fitting Maxwell-Boltzmann statistics, and plots comparing measured heat flux against theoretical predictions based on temperature gradients.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from basic observation of diffusion (comparable to molecular diffusion) to identifying the failure point of Fick's law under non-uniform conditions. It then rigorously compares stochastic calculus formulations (Ito vs. anti-Ito) and concludes by experimentally measuring and modeling the associated heat flux in the resulting non-equilibrium steady state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

When a horizontal plate vibrates strongly enough, it causes small particles such as sand grains to continually bounce on it and, over time, to diffuse across its surface. This phenomenon is the cause of the well-known Chladni figure, which is drawn by a higher density of grains gathering along the nodal lines of a resonating elastic plate. Using a heterogeneous, non-resonating plate, we investigate experimentally this type of diffusion. We find that, for the most part, is it comparable to classical molecular diffusion. We can define a temperature for the bouncing grains, and the system then obeys the fluctuation-dissipation theorem. We also recover Maxwell-Boltzmann statistics at equilibrium, when temperature is uniform. However, when temperature varies across the vibrating plate, the microscopic details of the grains' dynamics affect their macroscopic behavior: Fick's law, for instance, no longer applies. Instead, our experiments support a new transport relation that was recently proposed to represent diffusion in Chladni's experiment. Finally, we propose an expression for the heat flux associated to the non-equilibrium steady state predicted by this new relation, and test it against observations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper develops the Dual Model of Liquids (DML) to bridge macroscopic non-equilibrium thermodynamics with mesoscopic physics in liquids. It models energy transport via wave-packet interactions between solid-like aggregates and lattice excitations. The key finding is that this duality introduces a necessary asymmetry at the mesoscopic scale, providing a physical basis for the arrow of time.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, physically motivated mechanism—wave-packet interaction and structural relaxation—to derive irreversible thermodynamics from time-reversible microscopic dynamics, which is central to open quantum systems theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To establish a physical link between macroscopic non-equilibrium thermodynamics (NET) and mesoscopic microscopic processes in liquids, while simultaneously identifying the origin of the arrow of time.

**Main result.** The Dual Model of Liquids successfully models transport coefficients and suggests that the inherent duality introduces a mesoscopic asymmetry responsible for the thermodynamic arrow of time, even though elementary interactions are reversible.

**Method.** Theoretical modeling using the Dual Model of Liquids (DML), which treats energy/momentum exchange between solid-like aggregates ('icebergs') and lattice excitations via quasi-elastic wave-packet dynamics.

**Model / system.** The system is liquid matter, modeled by the DML. This model posits that liquids exhibit dualism, sharing energy transmission modes with solids through collective excitations (wave-packets) interacting with metastable solid-like clusters.

**Key observables.** Macroscopic transport properties like thermal conductivity, viscosity, and Soret coefficient; mesoscopic observables include propagating transverse currents and relaxation times ($	au$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mesoscopic scale (intermediate between atomic quantum mechanics and fluid dynamics); characteristic time scales ($	au$); energy/momentum exchange parameters ($\Delta E$, $\Delta p$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The DML assumes that solid-like aggregates maintain a quasi-harmonic structure over relaxation timescales, allowing wave propagation; NET is treated as phenomenological.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the elementary energy/momentum exchange between lattice and liquid particles (collision dynamics), the mechanism for viscosity via wave-packets mediating layer friction, and the comparison of time evolution under equilibrium versus irreversible processes.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the need to bridge NET with microscopic physics. It then details the DML framework, analyzing energy/momentum exchange mechanisms (collisions, tunneling) that yield macroscopic transport laws, culminating in an explanation for thermodynamic irreversibility at the mesoscopic level.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This manuscripts has two goals. The first goal is to show that the interaction in the Dual Model of Liquids between the solidlike molecule aggregates and the lattice excitations is appropriate to represent the link between the behaviour at macroscopic scale of normal liquids and the physical processes characterizing those systems at mesoscopic scale. The second goal is to show that the duality allows identifying a time arrow on the mesoscopic scale in liquids. The interaction of quanta of elastic energy with the molecular clusters introduces a privileged direction, which is relevant in time dependent and dissipative macroscopic processes, although the interaction remains temporally reversible

</details>

Top row: fast and weak driving regime (Ω/ωd = 0.1, ϵd = 0.1Ω) where our error measure is small for all master equations ε &lt; 0.05. Bottom row: slow and strong driving regime (Ω/ωd = 1.3, ϵd = 4Ω) where our error measure of the master equations is large ε &gt; 0.2, with an exception of the time-independent Floquet-Redfield for which ε &lt; 0.05. </details>

<sub>FIG. 3. Error measure (53) for the Magnus-Redfield and Magnus-Lindblad master equations as a function of the driv- ing frequency ωd and the driving amplitude ϵd for resonant (ωA = ωB = Ω, top row) and detuned (ωA = 2ωB = 2Ω, bottom row) spins. **Summary.** This paper rigorously benchmarks several analytical master equations designed for periodically driven open quantum systems. By comparing their predictions against numerically exact simulations using a two-spin model, the authors map out the precise parameter regimes where each equation remains accurate. The findings guide practitioners toward selecting robust methods, particularly favoring time-independent Floquet-Redfield approaches over those relying on approximations like the secular limit.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it provides a crucial diagnostic tool for researchers developing models for open quantum dynamics under time-periodic driving, helping them select the most reliable theoretical framework.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is benchmarking various Floquet master equations used to describe open quantum systems subjected to periodic driving, aiming to pinpoint the limits of their theoretical applicability.

**Main result.** The accuracy of any given master equation strongly reflects the underlying physical assumptions; specifically, errors are amplified near resonances where approximations like the secular approximation break down.

**Method.** The authors compare predictions from multiple analytical master equations (e.g., Floquet-Lindblad, Redfield) against numerically exact non-Markovian simulations using advanced techniques like uniTEMPO.

**Model / system.** The study uses a model of two locally driven spins coupled to a shared Ohmic reservoir at finite temperature, governed by a time-periodic Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Accuracy of the predicted dynamics (e.g., $\langle\sigma_z \otimes\sigma_zangle$ evolution) compared against exact simulations; error measure $\varepsilon(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Driving frequency ($\omega_d$), driving amplitude ($\epsilon_d$), and temperature/bath spectral density parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumptions tested are weak system-bath coupling, the validity of the secular approximation, and the limitations imposed by perturbative expansions (like Magnus series).

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the error measure ($\varepsilon$) across different master equations as a function of driving frequency and amplitude, generally showing that time-independent Floquet-Redfield performs best.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically reviews the derivation of various master equation formalisms (Magnus, Redfield, Lindblad) applicable to periodically driven systems, followed by rigorous benchmarking against exact non-Markovian simulations across varying parameter regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The dynamics of open quantum systems is commonly described by quantum master equations derived under the assumption of weak system-bath coupling and a separation of timescales between system and bath. When the system is additionally subjected to a periodic driving, the validity of the resulting Floquet master equations is further restricted to regimes of weak or high-frequency driving. Here, we benchmark a set of commonly used Floquet master equations for a model of two locally driven spins coupled to a shared Ohmic reservoir at finite temperature. We systematically probe the accuracy of the equations as a function of the driving parameters, thus identifying limits of their applicability. Dynamical maps predicted by each master equation are compared against numerically exact non-Markovian simulations, tracking the full relaxation dynamics. We find that the accuracy of each master equation closely reflects the assumptions underlying its derivation. For the Floquet-Lindblad equation, errors can be strongly amplified near resonances where the secular approximation breaks down, while approaches that avoid the secular approximation perform better and exhibit a more systematic dependence of the error on driving frequency and amplitude.

</details>

### [Isospectrality and Operator Complexity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05294v1)

**Authors:** Pradip Kattel, Yicheng Tang, Natan Andrei  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05294v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05294_figures/2606.05294_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Many-body spectra of the quadratic Hamiltonian Hff(λ) (blue circles) and the interacting Hamiltonian Hint(λ) (red crosses) for a chain of length N = 12 at λ = 0.5, 1, 1.5. The complete level-by-level agreement demonstrates the exact unitary equivalence between the interacting nearest-neighbor fermion chain Eq. (1) and the quadratic nearest-neighbor Hamiltonian Eq. (3). Inset: spectral differences ∆Ek = E(int) k −E(ff) k vanish to numerical precision.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05294_figures/2606.05294_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Ground-state density correlations Cij =   ni −1</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.05294_figures/2606.05294_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Infinite-temperature OTOCs of centered density op- erators Wj = nj −1</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.05294_figures/2606.05294_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Lanczos coefficients bn from the Liouvillian Krylov recursion with a local Majorana seed, computed numerically for a chain of length N = 100 up to n = 65 for various values of λ. To suppress short-range numerical fluctuations, the raw sequence is smoothed using a Savitzky–Golay filter (window size 11, quadratic polynomial) [24]. The resulting coefficients are consistent with bn ∝√n over the accessible Krylov depths, demonstrating distinct operator complexity despite exact isospectrality.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work explores whether two different interacting quantum models can be distinguished if they share the exact same many-body spectrum. By comparing an interacting chain to its quadratic dual, the authors show that despite spectral identity, the models exhibit distinct phase structures and fundamentally different operator growth rates. This proves that energy eigenvalues alone are insufficient to characterize all aspects of a complex quantum system.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful theoretical tool—the concept of 'operator complexity' beyond mere energy eigenvalues—to distinguish between physically distinct quantum phases even when the underlying Hamiltonians share identical ground state energies.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To investigate whether identical many-body spectra (isospectrality) are sufficient to determine all physical properties, such as phase structure or operator complexity.

**Main result.** The study shows that two Hamiltonians sharing the same spectrum can exhibit distinct phases and vastly different operator dynamics, demonstrating that spectral equivalence does not constrain all physical observables.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis involving nonlocal unitary transformations to relate an interacting Hamiltonian to a quadratic one, followed by calculating diagnostics like Out-of-Time-Order Correlators (OTOCs) and Krylov complexity growth rates.

**Model / system.** A pair of exactly solvable fermion chains: one strongly interacting ($	ext{H}_{	ext{int}}$) and its dual, quadratic/free-fermion counterpart ($	ext{H}_{	ext{ff}}(\lambda)$). The models are related by a nonlocal unitary transformation.

**Key observables.** Phase structure (e.g., CDW order), operator growth rates (Lanczos coefficients $b_n \propto \sqrt{n}$ vs. bounded), and OTOCs.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength $\lambda$, which controls the specific phase realized in the system.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on mapping local operators to extended Majorana strings via a nonlocal unitary transformation, allowing for exact computation of correlators using Pfaffians.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare spectra (confirming equivalence), density correlations diagnosing different phases ($\lambda$ dependence), OTOCs showing shared light cones but differing internal structure, and Lanczos coefficients demonstrating $\sqrt{n}$ growth in the interacting model.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the existence of two isospectral models, analyzes their spectral agreement, then contrasts their phase structures and operator dynamics using advanced diagnostics like Krylov complexity to prove that spectra alone are insufficient for full physical characterization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a pair of exactly solvable, isospectral fermion chains, one strongly interacting and one quadratic, that nevertheless display remarkably different phase structures and operator dynamics. A nonlocal nonlinear unitary transformation maps one onto the other while preserving the entire many-body spectrum and converting local fermion operators into extended many-body strings. Thus, operators that evolve within a closed linear subspace in the quadratic model become interacting operators that generate increasingly higher-body terms and exhibit asymptotic Lanczos growth $b_n\propto\sqrt n$. Despite their identical spectra, the two models realize distinct phases and sharply different notions of operator complexity. Our results demonstrate that free many-body spectra and interacting operator dynamics are fundamentally compatible.

</details>

**Authors:** Xiantong Chen, Xuanting Ji, Wen-Peng Li, Ya-Wen Sun

<sub>Figure 2 shows the representative behavior of the holographic c-function. At small strip width, the curves are close to one another, reflecting the common UV asymptotics of the backgrounds. At larger strip width, the nontrivial and trivial phases approach finite plateaus in both directions. The critical solution instead remains running over the accessible large width range: it grows in the transverse x direction and is suppressed in the z direction.</sub>

**Summary.** This work investigates using advanced multipartite entanglement measures within a holographic framework to study Weyl semimetals. By analyzing how these entanglement quantities scale with strip width and direction, the authors demonstrate that they can uniquely diagnose both topological phase transitions and inherent system anisotropy. This establishes multipartite entanglement as a powerful, non-local diagnostic tool for strongly correlated quantum matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter topology, the use of complex multipartite entanglement diagnostics provides a powerful theoretical tool for characterizing quantum phases in strongly interacting systems, relevant to understanding correlated electron behavior.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To determine if multipartite entanglement structures can diagnose topological quantum phase transitions and probe the anisotropy of holographic Weyl semimetals.

**Main result.** Multipartite holographic entanglement measures (like $\kappa$, Markov gap, multi-EWCS) show clear features near the critical point, confirming their role as sensitive probes for both topological transitions and anisotropic IR physics.

**Method.** The study uses the Ryu–Takayanagi prescription to calculate various multipartite entanglement quantities (CMI, EWCS, etc.) in a holographic bulk dual to the Weyl semimetal.

**Model / system.** The system is the zero-temperature holographic Weyl semimetal, which exhibits three phases—nontrivial, critical, and trivial—controlled by tuning the ratio $M/b$. The model involves gravity coupled to vector and axial gauge fields in the bulk.

**Key observables.** Conditional Mutual Information (CMI), Entanglement Wedge Cross Section (EWCS), tripartite measures ($\kappa$, Markov gap), multi-EWCS, and four-partite signals ($\Delta$ and $g$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strip width ($l$), tuning parameter across the topological transition ($M/b$), and angular direction ($	heta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on studying the large-$l$ behavior, which is governed by the deep IR geometry of the system.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of various entanglement measures (CMI, EWCS, $\kappa$, etc.) on strip width ($l$) and angular direction ($	heta$), comparing results across the three distinct topological phases.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically reviews the model and entanglement tools, analyzes the large-$l$ scaling to identify the transition point, and finally probes anisotropy by examining how these measures depend on the angle $	heta$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study multipartite entanglement structures in the zero-temperature holographic Weyl semimetal, focusing on tripartite and four-partite structures. For strip regions, we compute the conditional mutual information, the entanglement wedge cross section, tripartite measures $κ$ and the Markov gap, multi-EWCS, and two multi-EWCS based four-partite signals $Δ$ and $g$. These quantities are studied as functions of the strip width $l$ and the tuning parameter across the topological transition. At large $l$, their $l$ dependence takes a power-law form governed by the IR scaling of the system. At fixed large $l$, all these entanglement quantities develop clear features near the critical point, showing that tripartite and four-partite entanglement structures can diagnose the topological quantum phase transition. We further study strips pointing in different directions to probe the anisotropy of the system. The anisotropic large l behavior distinguishes the nontrivial phase from the trivial phase. These results establish multipartite holographic entanglement as a sensitive, nonlocal probe of topological phase transitions and anisotropic IR physics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05884"></a>
### [No-go theorems on simulating uncertainty principle's signatures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05884v1)

**Authors:** Chung-Yun Hsieh, Minjeong Song, Shin-Liang Chen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05884v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05884_figures/2606.05884_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Main question. When can uncertainty principle’s signa- tures, as carried by a pair of instruments, be simulated by a single instrument? In the figure, the left box is an instrument with classical outcome 𝜆, and the right box implements the 𝑥-th instrument (with classical outcomes 𝑎) when we press the 𝑥-th button.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05884_figures/2606.05884_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Non-vanishing overlap. Equation (2) implies that (E𝑎|0 ⊗I𝐴)(𝜌) ≥(L ⊗I𝐴)(𝜌) and (E𝑏|1 ⊗I𝐴)(𝜌) ≥(L ⊗I𝐴)(𝜌) for every finite-dimensional auxiliary system 𝐴and joint state 𝜌 (here, I𝐴is the identity channel acting on 𝐴). Hence, both the classi- cal outcomes and quantum outputs of E𝑎|0 and E𝑏|1 can be estimated by applying a single instrument containing L as an element [50].</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper rigorously analyzes the uncertainty principle by introducing 'complementarity' as a concept stronger than simple incompatibility for quantum measurements. It proves that strong signatures of this principle cannot be simulated by single-measurement processes, thereby establishing fundamental no-go theorems regarding resource simulation in quantum information theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides fundamental theoretical constraints on what quantum measurements can achieve, directly impacting the understanding of resource theories in quantum information science and setting benchmarks for quantum advantage claims.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is determining the fundamental limits on simulating signatures of the uncertainty principle using only a single quantum measurement process (instrument). The authors aim to establish a hierarchy between different notions of quantum compatibility.

**Main result.** They prove that strong signatures of the uncertainty principle cannot be simulated by a single measurement, even when assisted by quantum pre- or post-processing. They introduce 'complementarity' as a resource strictly stronger than mere incompatibility.

**Method.** The work employs rigorous mathematical characterizations using Semidefinite Programming (SDP) to define and test concepts like complementarity and joint measurability for generalized quantum instruments. Operational tests involve analyzing the error probability in unambiguously sending classical information.

**Model / system.** The study models general measurement processes using 'instruments' $\{E_a\}_{a}$, which are generalizations of POVMs, applied to bipartite quantum systems often involving maximally entangled states $|\Phi^+angle$. The analysis extends to noisy versions of these instruments.

**Key observables.** Complementary overlap $C(a,b)(E)$, error probability in unambiguous classical communication ($P(	ilde{\eta})_{	ext{error}}$), and the joint measurability ($\mathcal{J}\mathcal{M}$) condition.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The noise parameter $\epsilon$ (for noisy Pauli instruments) and the fidelity/overlap parameters derived from SDP solutions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often focuses on rank-one projective instruments for establishing the strongest no-go theorems, and some results rely on assuming positive probabilities for input settings ($p_x > 0$).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the central question of simulating two-instrument signatures with one. Figure 2 helps define complementary instruments by showing non-vanishing overlap.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds logically by first defining incompatibility, then introducing the stronger concept of complementarity via vanishing overlap (characterized by SDP). It then proves that complementarity implies incompatibility and establishes operational equivalences: complementarity is equivalent to achieving unambiguous classical communication regardless of outcome relabeling. Finally, it uses these results to construct no-go theorems against simulation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Uncertainty principle, one of the most iconic features of quantum mechanics, was originally viewed as a fundamental limitation. Since the inception of quantum information science, researchers began to use it to achieve quantum advantages. To better understand the origin of these advantages, an essential question is: To what extent can the uncertainty principle's signatures be simulated by a single measurement? As a single measurement clearly cannot demonstrate the uncertainty principle, such a simulation, if exists, implies the claimed advantages may either stem from other quantum features, or just be reproducible in a less resourceful way. In this work, we report a series of noise-robust no-go theorems, showing that strong enough signatures of uncertainty principle cannot be simulated by a single measurement, even when assisted by quantum pre- or post-processing. This signature is modelled by complementary instruments. We completely characterise complementary instruments by a numerically feasible measure and show that they are necessary and sufficient resources for the advantage in an operational task that aims to unambiguously send classical information.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.05719"></a>
### [Symmetries and overparametrization properties of Hamiltonian variational ansatzes for the $(1+1)$d $\mathbb{Z}_2$ lattice gauge theory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05719v1)

**Authors:** Kanta Yamanaka, Takanori Daiza, Katsumi Imaizumi, Yutaro Iiyama, Lento Nagano, Ryu Sawada, Koji Terashi

**Summary.** The paper rigorously analyzes Hamiltonian Variational Ansatzes applied to a lattice gauge theory, focusing on how symmetries constrain the variational space. It establishes that overparametrization leads to the saturation of the QFIM rank, which is bounded by the system's underlying algebraic structure (DLA dimension). This provides theoretical guidelines for designing more efficient and robust quantum algorithms.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides crucial theoretical diagnostics for designing scalable quantum circuits by linking fundamental algebraic properties (Lie algebras) to practical computational metrics (QFIM rank and optimization convergence).

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the relationship between symmetries, overparametrization effects, and optimization dynamics when using Hamiltonian Variational Ansatzes (HVAs) to simulate quantum systems.

**Main result.** Overparametrization causes the QFIM rank to saturate at a value bounded by the dimension of the Dynamical Lie Algebra (DLA), while the VQE loss function decay rate scales linearly with the number of parameters.

**Method.** The authors analyze five different HVA families applied to the $\mathbb{Z}_2$ LGT, calculating the DLA and QFIM rank, and simulating the optimization dynamics using Gradient Descent on the VQE loss function.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the $(1+1)$d $\mathbb{Z}_2$ lattice gauge theory (LGT). The Hamiltonian is complex, involving Pauli operators, and the analysis focuses on restricting it to symmetry-invariant subspaces for variational simulation.

**Key observables.** Dimension of the Dynamical Lie Algebra (DLA), Rank of the Quantum Fisher Information Matrix (QFIM), VQE loss function decay rate ($\kappa$), and asymptotic loss values ($L_X$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of parameters ($M$), number of layers ($L$), lattice size ($N_s$), and the degree of overparametrization.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on comparing theoretical bounds (DLA dimension) with numerical diagnostics (QFIM rank saturation) and assumes certain approximations regarding circuit design (e.g., 2-designs for decay rate scaling).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of DLA dimensions and saturated QFIM ranks on lattice size, show the convergence/saturation of QFIM rank versus ansatz layers, and plot the linear scaling of the loss function decay rate with the number of parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes five HVA families by calculating their symmetry-constrained subspaces (DLA) and then uses these results to predict how the QFIM rank saturates as the circuit depth increases, correlating this saturation with the observed behavior of the VQE loss function during optimization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We perform detailed studies of five Hamiltonian variational ansatzes (HVA) based on the Hamiltonian of the $(1+1)$d $\mathbb{Z}_2$ lattice gauge theory. The ansatzes are designed to respect local and global symmetries of the original Hamiltonian and therefore act on a finely segmented state Hilbert space. Following Larocca et al. (2023), we numerically study the dimension of the dynamical Lie algebra (DLA) and the rank of the quantum Fisher information matrix (QFIM) of the ansatzes within specific invariant subspaces. The ansatzes all involve sums of weight-three Paulis in their generators, which is a feature that have so far been underexplored in this context. We also perform numerical experiments to determine the ground state energy of the original Hamiltonian via variational quantum eigensolver (VQE), and observe that overparametrization of the ansatzes coincides with the apparent disappearance of local minima in the loss function, in line with the finding in the reference. Finally, the decay rate of the VQE loss function under gradient descent optimization is revealed to scale linearly with the number of parameters in the ansatz. These results help to enrich the theory of overparameterization of quantum circuits and inform the design of scalable variational ansatzes.

</details>

### [A microscopic design rule for spin supersolids in triangular-lattice magnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06111v1)

**Authors:** Ryota Ono, Jun'ichi Ieda, Michiyasu Mori, Sadamichi Maekawa  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06111v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This theoretical study develops a microscopic design rule for spin supersolids in triangular-lattice magnets by analyzing cobaltate compounds. It shows that the crucial exchange anisotropy parameter ($\Delta$) is controlled by the ratio of crystal field splitting to spin-orbit coupling strength. This framework allows researchers to predict and engineer materials exhibiting exotic quantum magnetic states.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides a quantitative, first-principles pathway (crystal field engineering) to tune quantum magnetic phases like supersolidity in solid-state materials, bridging orbital physics with frustrated magnetism.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To establish a microscopic design rule for realizing and tuning spin supersolidity in triangular-lattice magnets by understanding the origin of exchange anisotropy ($\Delta$).

**Main result.** The exchange anisotropy $\Delta$ is determined by the ratio of the trigonal crystal field strength to the spin–orbit coupling strength, allowing systematic prediction across related material families.

**Method.** Calculations combine first-principles electronic structure methods (DFT/cRPA) with strong-coupling superexchange theory and effective low-energy Hubbard model construction.

**Model / system.** The system involves $	ext{Co}^{2+}$ ions forming a quasi-two-dimensional triangular lattice in cobaltate families ($	ext{X}_2	ext{Y}	ext{Co}(	ext{PO}_4)_2$ and $	ext{X}_2	ext{Co}(	ext{SeO}_3)_2$). The physics is mapped onto an effective pseudospin-$1/2$ XXZ model.

**Key observables.** Exchange anisotropy parameter ($\Delta$), single-ion anisotropy factor ($\gamma$), nearest-neighbor exchange couplings ($J_{ab}^{ij}$), and crystal field parameters ($A_{20}, A_{40}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ratio of trigonal crystal field to SOC strength ($\omega_{	ext{trig}}/\lambda_{	ext{eff}}$); $\Delta > 1$ for supersolid; $\Delta < 1$ for superfluid.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The low-energy physics can be accurately described by projecting the full Hamiltonian onto a Kramers doublet (pseudospin-$1/2$) manifold, and that superexchange theory is applicable.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate crystal structures, energy level schemes for $	ext{Co}^{2+}$, and plots mapping calculated $\Delta$ values across various cation substitutions to identify supersolid candidates.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically moves from analyzing the local electronic structure (DFT/cRPA) to deriving an effective low-energy Hubbard model. It then uses superexchange theory to calculate magnetic couplings, finally relating these couplings to structural parameters ($\omega_{	ext{trig}}/\lambda_{	ext{eff}}$) that govern $\Delta$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Spin supersolids emerge as a central topic in frustrated magnetism, motivating the search for realization in quantum materials. To this end, we study the origin of exchange anisotropy, $Δ$, in triangular-lattice cobaltate families $X_2$$Y$Co(PO$_4$)$_2$ and $X_2$Co(SeO$_3$)$_2$ ($X$ = Na, K, Rb, Cs; $Y$ = Mg, Ca, Sr, Ba) by tailoring realistic spin models. We show that $Δ$ is determined by the ratio of trigonal crystal field to spin-orbit coupling strength. This framework explains contrasting anisotropies in these families, predicts systematic trends in $Δ$ across $X/Y$-substitutions, and identifies candidate materials for spin supersolids. Our results establish trigonal field engineering as a microscopic route toward the design of spin supersolids.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper presents a universal framework utilizing fermionic ultracold atoms to simulate complex many-body Hamiltonians beyond the hardware's native capabilities. By employing a hybrid Variational Quantum Eigensolver (fVQE), it successfully models systems exhibiting pairing and topological order. This demonstrates an exponential quantum advantage over classical simulation techniques for characterizing strongly correlated electronic matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges cutting-edge AMO physics platforms with quantum computation theory by providing a concrete, scalable pathway to simulate strongly correlated electron models (like those found in high-Tc superconductors) using neutral atoms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a universal and efficient hybrid digital-analog framework using fermionic ultracold atom platforms to simulate ground-state properties of many-body systems beyond the hardware's native Hamiltonian.

**Main result.** The proposed fVQE approach achieves an exponential speedup over classical methods for simulating complex physics, successfully capturing phenomena like pairing and topological order in models not natively implemented on the platform.

**Method.** A hybrid Variational Quantum Eigensolver (fVQE) algorithm is employed, where variational parameters are optimized classically based on measurements of non-native Hamiltonian terms derived from local observables.

**Model / system.** The primary system is fermionic ultracold atoms in optical lattices/tweezers arrays. Models studied include the repulsive Hubbard model, an extended version with nearest-neighbor attraction (EFHM), and the Hofstadter-Hubbard model (HHM) incorporating gauge fields.

**Key observables.** Energy density, density-density correlations ($C_n(r)$), spin-spin correlations ($|C_{Sz}(r)|$), and topological order parameters like the Girvin-MacDonald (GMD) order parameter.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The simulation time $T$ scales polynomially with the inverse relative error ($\epsilon^{-1}$), demonstrating an exponential speedup over classical methods; finite-size effects ($L$) are crucial for determining optimal simulation time $T^*$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the variational principle and assumes that the total error is dominated by a balance between quantum simulation error ($\epsilon_Q$) and finite-size error ($\epsilon_L$).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the programmable simulator framework, showing VQE feedback loops; comparisons are made between VQE energy error decay versus classical ED scaling for various models (FHM, EFHM).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the fVQE framework by detailing its application to specific Hamiltonians (FHM, EFHM, HHM), demonstrating how non-native terms are measured and incorporated into the variational optimization loop. It then analyzes the scaling of errors and measures complex correlations characteristic of different physical phases.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a universal framework to harness fermionic ultracold atom platforms for quantum simulation, showing how variational algorithms on existing hardware can simulate many-body systems well beyond the hardware's native Hamiltonian. Our analysis provides evidence that one can quantum simulate the ground-state properties of a broad class of gapless target Hamiltonians of local observables in a quantum evolution time that grows polynomially with the inverse relative error, $T\sim O(\mathrm{poly}(1/ε))$ up to logarithmic corrections, offering an exponential speedup over na{ï}ve classical algorithms such as exact diagonalization. We provide numerical evidence and theoretical argument that this holds for energy density, density-density, and spin-spin correlations in three qualitatively distinct models -- the repulsive Hubbard model; a Hubbard model augmented with nearest-neighbor attractive interactions, which introduces the phenomenon of pairing; and the Hofstadter-Hubbard model, which introduces a gauge field and fractional quantum Hall physics. This work demonstrates quantum simulation using current fermionic platforms far beyond the models natively implemented in the hardware.

</details>

### [Decoder-Consistent Hamiltonians for POVM-Based Quantum Relaxations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05604v1)

**Authors:** Takayuki Suzuki  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05604v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<img src="2606.05604_figures/2606.05604_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05604_figures/2606.05604_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>

<img src="2606.05604_figures/2606.05604_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.05604_figures/2606.05604_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.05604_figures/2606.05604_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

**Summary.** The paper introduces the concept of a 'decoder-consistent Hamiltonian' to rigorously model quantum relaxation methods solving combinatorial problems. By treating the measurement/decoding process as a POVM, it derives a unique Hamiltonian that accurately reflects the expected classical objective value after decoding. This framework yields quantifiable performance guarantees for optimization tasks like MaxCut.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a deep theoretical link between the classical structure of an optimization problem's decoder and the required quantum Hamiltonian, which is crucial for designing efficient variational quantum algorithms that move beyond simple approximations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a theoretically rigorous framework for quantum relaxation methods, such as QRAO, used to solve combinatorial optimization problems when direct qubit encoding is impractical.

**Main result.** The paper establishes the concept of a unique decoder-consistent Hamiltonian derived from the POVM representation of the decoder, providing quantifiable lower bounds for optimization objectives like MaxCut based on effective parity preservation rates.

**Method.** The core method involves representing the classical decoder as a Positive Operator-Valued Measure (POVM) and using the dual map to construct the decoder-consistent Hamiltonian. Performance guarantees are derived by analyzing how well this Hamiltonian preserves specific parity components.

**Model / system.** The model is based on quantum computation utilizing variational algorithms applied to combinatorial optimization problems (e.g., MaxCut). The system involves encoding classical variables into qubits and then decoding them via a POVM, which dictates the structure of the effective Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Parity Preservation Rate ($\kappa_A$), Effective Preservation Rate ($\alpha_A$), Expected Cut Value ($E[	ext{Cut}]$), and the lower bound on the objective function value $\lambda_{\max}(H_D[f])$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The structure of the decoder (e.g., Teramoto-type or QRAC type) dictates specific values for $\alpha_{in}$ and $\alpha_{out}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derived lower bounds assume that the system can be ideally maximized using the constructed Hamiltonian, which is a strong assumption regarding variational optimization success.

**Figures summary.** Not specified in the notes; however, the text references equations (Eq. 5, Eq. 9, Eq. 10) detailing the structure of the derived Hamiltonians and the final lower bound expressions.

**Paper structure.** The paper first formalizes the problem by defining the decoder-consistent Hamiltonian via POVMs. It then analyzes this framework for quadratic objectives (like MaxCut), deriving specific forms for different relaxation types (QRAO vs QRAC). Finally, it establishes performance guarantees based on inter- and intra-block effective preservation rates.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In compression-based quantum relaxations like QRAO, classical variables are encoded into qubits and decoded after optimization. We formalize that the choice of the quantum Hamiltonian is fundamentally determined by this decoder. By representing the decoder as a POVM, we define a unique decoder-consistent Hamiltonian via the pullback of the post-decoding expected objective value. Using this framework, we reveal that standard QRAO Hamiltonians are inconsistent for certain mixed-degree quadratic functions, and we provide new approximation guarantees for the MaxCut problem based directly on POVM decoder design.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05579"></a>
### [A Class of Multipartite Entangled States Based on State Transitions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05579v1)

**Authors:** Jehn-Ruey Jiang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05579v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05579_figures/2606.05579_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05579_figures/2606.05579_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.05579_figures/2606.05579_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.05579_figures/2606.05579_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.05579_figures/2606.05579_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces Transition states (T states), a novel class of multipartite entangled quantum states defined by the count of state transitions between adjacent qubits. The authors prove that these T states are mathematically equivalent to Dicke states, which are characterized by a fixed number of excitations. This establishes a powerful new structural link between two distinct ways of quantifying entanglement in multi-qubit systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on abstract quantum information theory, establishing structural equivalences between different physical constraints (like local transitions vs. global excitation numbers) provides powerful mathematical tools applicable to modeling complex many-body correlations in AMO systems or analyzing error correction codes.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper introduces a new class of multipartite entangled states, Transition states (T states), defined by the number of state transitions between adjacent qubits.

**Main result.** It proves that T states are unitarily equivalent to Dicke states, establishing a direct structural correspondence between transition-based and excitation-based entanglement representations.

**Method.** The characterization involves defining T states as equal-amplitude superpositions over basis states with a fixed number of adjacent transitions, followed by proving the unitary transformation linking them to Dicke states via CX operations.

**Model / system.** The system is an $n$-qubit quantum system. The analysis compares T states (defined by transition counts) against established classes like Bell, GHZ, W, and Dicke states (defined by fixed Hamming weight/excitations).

**Key observables.** Number of state transitions between adjacent qubits; total number of excitations ($k$); entanglement structure.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The number of qubits ($n$) and the specified count for either transitions or excitations ($k$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes an ordered sequence of qubits to define adjacency for transition counting, which distinguishes T states from permutation-invariant Dicke states.

**Paper structure.** The paper first defines the novel T states based on sequential transitions. It then compares these states mathematically and physically against existing entanglement measures (Bell, GHZ, W, Dicke). The core of the work is proving the unitary equivalence between the two classes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce Transition states (T states), denoted by $\ket{T_k^n}$, as a class of multipartite entangled states characterized by a fixed number of state transitions between adjacent qubits. These states form equal-amplitude superpositions over all states with a specified transition count. Unlike Bell states based on two-qubit correlations, GHZ states characterized by global correlations among all qubits, and W and Dicke states based on fixed numbers of qubit excitations, T states are defined by transition counts along an ordered sequence of qubits. We prove that T states are unitarily equivalent to Dicke states through a chain of CX (controlled-X) operations, thereby establishing a direct correspondence between transition-based and excitation-based representations of multipartite entanglement.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper establishes an information-geometric bound linking the robustness of entanglement generation in two qubits to the Quantum Fisher Information (QFI). By analyzing how concurrence degrades under fluctuating interactions, the authors show that this loss is bounded by a quantity derived from QFI. This result provides a fundamental measure for assessing the reliability of quantum operations against environmental or control imperfections.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful theoretical tool connecting quantum metrology (QFI) directly to resource quantification (entanglement robustness), which is crucial for designing reliable quantum communication protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research investigates how robust entanglement generation is when the underlying interaction strength fluctuates due to uncertainty.

**Main result.** A direct information-geometric bound is established, showing that the reduction in concurrence due to fluctuations is bounded by a quantity related to the Quantum Fisher Information (QFI).

**Method.** The authors use second-order Taylor expansions and weak-fluctuation approximations to relate the curvature of entanglement loss to the QFI.

**Model / system.** The system modeled consists of two interacting qubits whose dynamics are governed by a Hamiltonian $H(g) = g h$, where $g$ is the fluctuating interaction strength. The analysis uses Bell state expansions for diagnostics.

**Key observables.** Concurrence ($C$), Quantum Fisher Information (QFI, $F_Q$), and the Curvature of Entanglement ($	ext{CoE}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength fluctuation variance ($\sigma_g^2$) and time ($t$). The analysis focuses on the weak-fluctuation limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is operating in the weak-fluctuation regime ($\sigma_g 	o 0$), allowing for second-order Taylor expansion approximations.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by defining entanglement generation (concurrence) and parameter sensitivity (QFI), deriving an information-geometric bound linking them, and finally applying this to quantify the robustness of entanglement against noise sources.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement generation is a central resource for quantum information processing, quantum networking, and quantum sensing. In practical implementations, however, entangling interactions are inevitably subject to uncertainty and fluctuations in the interaction strength. We investigate the robustness of entanglement generation in the presence of such imperfections and establish a direct connection between the robustness of entanglement generation and quantum Fisher information (QFI). For two interacting qubits, we show that the reduction in concurrence caused by fluctuations in the interaction parameter is bounded by the QFI with respect to the interaction strength.

### [Robustness of Entanglement Manipulation for almost i.i.d. sources](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06392v1)

**Authors:** Nilanjana Datta  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06392v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.06392_figures/2606.06392_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.06392_figures/2606.06392_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.06392_figures/2606.06392_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.06392_figures/2606.06392_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.06392_figures/2606.06392_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proves that key entanglement manipulation rates—such as concentration and dilution costs—are robust to small, structured deviations from perfect independence in quantum sources. By analyzing MSR almost i.i.d. states, the authors show that these non-ideal sources maintain the same asymptotic performance bounds as ideal i.i.d. sources.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides rigorous mathematical proofs that the fundamental capacity theorems of quantum information theory hold even when physical sources exhibit controlled, non-ideal correlations, which is crucial for realistic quantum communication protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how robust asymptotic entanglement manipulation (concentration and dilution) remains when quantum sources deviate from the ideal independent and identically distributed (i.i.d.) regime.

**Main result.** For MSR almost i.i.d. sources, the achievable rates for both concentration and dilution are shown to be governed by the same asymptotic bounds as their perfectly i.i.d. reference states.

**Method.** The analysis employs advanced information-theoretic tools, including spectral entropy rates, Schur–Weyl duality, and structural characterizations of MSR defect spaces.

**Model / system.** The model involves bipartite quantum systems whose state sequences are described by Mazzola–Sutter–Renner (MSR) almost i.i.d. sources, allowing for controlled sublinear deviations from a tensor-power structure.

**Key observables.** Entanglement concentration rate ($S(\varphi_A)$), entanglement cost ($	ext{EC}(ho_{AB})$), and coherent information ($I(Aangle B)_ho$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The defect size sequence $r_n$ must satisfy $r_n = o(n)$, ensuring the deviation from i.i.d. is sublinear.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the source structure belongs to the MSR class, which mathematically controls the nature of the deviations from perfect independence.

**Figures summary.** Not specified in the provided notes; the analysis relies on mathematical proofs and structural theorems rather than figure descriptions.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds by first establishing structural and entropic rigidity properties for MSR sources (showing spectral entropy rates are preserved). It then applies these results sequentially to prove achievability bounds for entanglement concentration (pure states) and entanglement cost/dilution (mixed states).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the robustness of asymptotic entanglement manipulation beyond the exact i.i.d. regime, focusing on Mazzola--Sutter--Renner (MSR) almost i.i.d. sources, which allow a sublinear number of deviations from a tensor-power structure. For pure MSR sources along a bipartite reference state $|φ\rangle_{AB}$, we prove that the entanglement concentration rate is robust: every rate below the entropy of entanglement $S(φ_A)$ remains achievable. Moreover, this can be done by a single Schur--Weyl concentration protocol that is universal within the MSR class, depending only on the reference state and not on the particular source sequence. For mixed MSR sources along a reference state $ρ_{AB}$, we prove a source-dependent entanglement-distillation achievability result: every rate below the coherent information $I(A\rangle B)_ρ$ of the reference state is achievable, although the entanglement distillation protocol may depend on the particular MSR source sequence. For the reverse task of entanglement dilution, we prove a rate-robustness theorem: the asymptotic entanglement cost of any MSR target sequence along $ρ_{AB}$ is at most $E_F^\infty(ρ_{AB})$, the regularized entanglement of formation of the reference state. To establish these results, we prove structural and entropic properties of MSR almost i.i.d. sequences which may be useful in other information-theoretic settings. Thus, for the achievability statements considered here, MSR almost i.i.d. perturbations exhibit the same asymptotic behaviour as their i.i.d. reference states, despite allowing sublinear deviations from a tensor-power structure.

</details>

### [Forbidden transitions in superconducting artificial atoms](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06069v1)

**Authors:** Alberto Del Ángel Medina, Théo Sépulcre, Ricardo Gutiérrez-Jáuregui, Anton Frisk Kockum

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>Figure 1. Artificial atoms built from Josephson junctions cou- ple to the surrounding electromagnetic environment. The cou- pling depends on the polarization and spatial profiles of field and junction. (a) Sketch of a cross-strip junction [20]. **Summary.** This paper develops a sophisticated microscopic theory for Josephson junctions coupled to structured electromagnetic fields. It moves beyond simple lumped-element models by incorporating the spatial variation of $\mathbf{E}$ and $\mathbf{B}$ fields into the Hamiltonian. The key finding is that higher-order multipole transitions, such as quadrupole excitation, can be utilized to probe field gradients, enabling novel quantum control mechanisms in superconducting artificial atoms.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly bridges superconducting circuit QED with fundamental quantum electrodynamics concepts, providing tools to engineer non-linear responses sensitive to field gradients rather than just intensity, which is crucial for advanced quantum sensing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a microscopic theoretical framework for Josephson junctions embedded in structured electromagnetic fields, moving beyond simple circuit models to account for spatial field profiles.

**Main result.** The theory predicts that the junction can be excited via quadrupole transitions dependent on the electric field gradient, allowing excitation even where the electric field intensity vanishes.

**Method.** The analysis uses a microscopic Hamiltonian derived from minimal coupling theory and employs techniques like path integrals, normal-mode decomposition, and multipolar expansion to characterize the system's response.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a superconducting Josephson junction (artificial atom) modeled by an effective Hamiltonian incorporating charging energy ($E_C$), Josephson energy ($E_J$), and coupling terms derived from the spatial variation of external electromagnetic fields ($\mathbf{E}, \mathbf{B}$).

**Key observables.** Phase difference ($\varphi$), charge imbalance ($n(\mathbf{r}_s)$), electric dipole moment ($\hat{d}$), electric quadrupole moment ($\hat{q}$), and magnetic dipole moment ($\hat{\mu}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Josephson coupling energy ($E_J$), charging energy ($E_C$), field gradients, and the ratio of coupling strengths between different multipole moments.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on linearizing the sine-Gordon equation for small oscillations and expanding the electromagnetic interaction Hamiltonian in terms of multipolar operators (dipole, quadrupole).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the setup involving a cross-strip junction coupled to an electromagnetic environment, showing field dependence. Other figures likely detail mode coupling or energy level diagrams.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from deriving the microscopic Hamiltonian via minimal coupling (S1), integrating out fermionic fields to find effective action/Hamiltonian ($\hat{H}$) (S2-S4), and finally analyzing the low-energy dynamics using normal modes and multipolar expansions to predict specific excitation pathways (S5-S6).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Artificial atoms built from Josephson junctions have become a powerful tool to explore the limits of quantum optics due to their strong coupling to electromagnetic fields and their sensitivity to changes at the single-photon level. This sensitivity to quantum fluctuations complements their metrological and computational use, which are based on the precise oscillating frequency of the underlying supercurrents. We present here a theory for Josephson junctions immersed in electromagnetic fields where focus is shifted from temporal correlations and towards spatial ones. Unlike the commonly used circuit and black-box descriptions, our work is based on a microscopic model that enables systematically accounting for the effect of the spatial and vectorial profile of an electromagnetic field over a junction. As an example of the interactions that emerge in such a setup, we investigate the possibility of driving a junction via a quadrupole transition, using typical experimental parameters in existing devices. With the transition being dependent on the gradient of the electric field -- rather than its intensity -- the junction can be excited in a region where the electric field vanishes.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes particle movement on quasicrystalline surfaces driven by oscillating forces. It demonstrates that despite the aperiodicity of the surface potential, the mean direction and speed of the particles become locked to values determined by hidden periodic symmetries within the potential structure. The findings are significant because they reveal underlying deterministic order governing transport in complex quasiperiodic media.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete example of how symmetry principles (hidden periodicity) can govern macroscopic transport properties (locking) in complex, non-periodic media, which has implications for understanding energy dissipation and directed motion in engineered materials or biological systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates particle motion on quasicrystalline surfaces, aiming to predict directional and speed locking when subjected to time-periodic external driving forces.

**Main result.** Particle mean velocities can lock into specific directions dictated by the lattice vectors of underlying hidden periodic potentials, even though the overall surface potential lacks translational periodicity. Furthermore, this directional locking is often accompanied by a predictable locking of the mean particle speed.

**Method.** The analysis uses theoretical modeling based on Langevin dynamics for overdamped motion over a quasicrystalline potential derived from plane wave superpositions. Results are confirmed via numerical simulations and geometric analysis linking observed plateaus to underlying lattice symmetries.

**Model / system.** The system models particles moving across surfaces with potentials $U(\mathbf{r})$ exhibiting quasicrystalline order, generated by summing multiple plane waves. The dynamics are governed by a Langevin equation incorporating both DC and time-periodic AC driving forces.

**Key observables.** Mean particle velocity ($ar{\mathbf{v}}$), the angle of mean motion ($	heta_v$), and the mean speed ($|ar{\mathbf{v}}|$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The ratio of characteristic speeds to the inverse period ($1/	au$) of the driving force; noise strength ($D$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The particle dynamics are well-described by the overdamped Langevin equation, and the underlying potential structure is accurately captured by plane wave superpositions.

**Figures summary.** Figures demonstrate directional locking as plateaus in $	heta_v$ vs $	heta$, correlating these directions to lattice vectors $\mathbf{d}^{(ij)}_{m_1 m_2}$. Speed locking is shown via linear dependence of $ar{v}$ on $1/	au$.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the physical model and dynamics, analyzes directional locking by relating observed plateaus in mean velocity angle to lattice vectors derived from hidden periodic potentials. It then extends this analysis to show that speed locking is also predicted by these same underlying symmetries.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Motion of particles across quasicrystalline surfaces exhibits peculiar features due to the presence of long-range order without translational periodicity. Under time-periodic forcing, this motion can become locked in directions thatn deviate strongly from the mean driving direction. We show that for surface potentials with a quasicrystalline pattern of minima generated by a superposition of plane waves, particle trajectories are nonperiodic, yet their mean direction and speed are determined by hidden periodic potentials. The lattice vectors of these underlying potentials define characteristic velocities that dictate both directional and speed locking. The particle motion does not synchronize with the driving, and it is possible for the mean speed to remain nonlocked even in directionally locked states. These findings are demonstrated using a model directly amenable to experimental realization.

</details>

### [Breakdown of Fluctuational Electrodynamics in the Extreme Near Field](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05845v1)

**Authors:** Philippe Ben-Abdallah  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05845v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>FIG. 3. Spectral radiative heat flux carried by correlated fluc- tuations (full line) and predicted by conventional FE (dashed line) between two SiC half-spaces separated by a vacuum gap (a) d = 1 nm, (b) d = 10 nm and (c) d = 100 nm.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work shows that standard Fluctuational Electrodynamics fails near surfaces because it ignores correlated thermal fluctuations. By modeling coupled surface phonon modes, the authors derive a correction term to the heat flux due to hybridization resonance. This establishes a microscopic framework quantifying how strong coupling modifies radiative heat transfer in the extreme near-field.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete example of how non-equilibrium correlations arising from strong coupling (hybridization) can fundamentally alter predictions in thermal transport, which is relevant for open quantum systems interacting with structured environments.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the breakdown of Fluctuational Electrodynamics (FE) theory when calculating radiative heat transfer at subnanometric separations.

**Main result.** The failure stems from neglecting cross-correlations between thermal fluctuations in distinct bodies, which are significant due to phonon hybridization in the extreme near-field regime.

**Method.** A microscopic coupled-oscillator model is used alongside a Green-tensor formulation of the Poynting vector to derive the correlation-induced correction term ($\varphi_{cross}$).

**Model / system.** The system involves two closely spaced, polar interfaces (e.g., SiC) separated by a vacuum gap ($d$), supporting surface phonon-polaritons.

**Key observables.** The total spectral heat flux ($\varphi_{	ext{tot}}$), the correlation-induced correction term ($\varphi_{	ext{cross}}$), and the spectral ratio $R = \varphi_{	ext{tot}}/\varphi_{	ext{FE}}$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Subnanometric separations ($d$), hybridization energy, and intrinsic damping rates ($\gamma_i$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is most significant when the hybridization energy is comparable to the intrinsic damping rate; conventional FE assumes statistical independence of fluctuations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the resonance frequencies and coupling coefficients for hybridized modes (Fig 1), and show the total heat flux compared to FE predictions for varying gaps (Fig 3).

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes a microscopic framework by modeling coupled oscillators, deriving cross-correlations from hybridization, and quantifying the resulting correction to the radiative heat flux.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fluctuational electrodynamics relies on the assumption that thermal fluctuations in distinct bodies are statistically independent. We show that this approximation breaks down in the extreme near-field regime, where overlapping evanescent surface fields hybridize optical phonons across nanometric vacuum gaps and generate fluctuating-current cross correlations between opposite interfaces. Using a microscopic coupled-oscillator model combined with a Green-tensor formulation of the Poynting vector, we derive the resulting correlation-induced correction to the radiative heat flux. For polar materials supporting surface phonon-polaritons, these correlations become significant when the hybridization energy is comparable to the intrinsic damping rate and can substantially modify conventional fluctuational-electrodynamics predictions at subnanometric separations. Our results establish a microscopic framework for correlated thermal fluctuations in the extreme near-field regime and quantify their impact on radiative heat transfer.

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.06128_figures/2606.06128_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4e displays the calculated 𝑘-dependent transition dipole moment, which vanishes for</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The research demonstrates that interfacing monolayer $	ext{WSe}_2$ with a ferroelectric perovskite substrate allows for the electrical activation of spin-forbidden dark excitons. By leveraging the ferroelectric proximity effect, the system generates an effective in-plane SOC field, enabling magnetic-field-free control over exciton spin and valley polarization. This establishes a powerful, electrically tunable platform for quantum information processing in 2D materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses open quantum systems by demonstrating how an external, tunable electric field can engineer spin-orbit coupling to activate otherwise optically forbidden transitions in solid-state excitons, which is highly relevant for building quantum light sources or qubits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is achieving electrical reconfigurable, magnetic-field-free control over spin and valley information carried by long-lived dark excitons in 2D semiconductors.

**Main result.** The ferroelectric proximity effect successfully brightens the spin-forbidden dark exciton ($	ext{X}_{	ext{D}}$) in $	ext{WSe}_2$ at zero magnetic field, establishing a symmetry-tunable control mechanism.

**Method.** The study combines experimental characterization (PL, SHG, Raman) with theoretical modeling using a four-band tight-binding Hamiltonian to explain the underlying physics.

**Model / system.** The system is a $	ext{WSe}_2$/ferroelectric hybrid perovskite heterostructure. The mechanism relies on the ferroelectric proximity effect inducing an asymmetric intersublattice interaction, which generates an effective in-plane spin-orbit coupling (SOC) field.

**Key observables.** Brightening of dark excitons at zero magnetic field; polarization rotation/modulation proportional to the electric field orientation; reduction in exciton polarization phase difference indicating valley mixing.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ferroelectric field ($\mathbf{E}$); interlayer twist angle; applied magnetic fields ($B_{//}, B_{\perp}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The mechanism is modeled by assuming the ferroelectric proximity effect dictates an asymmetric hopping term in the tight-binding Hamiltonian.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate symmetry breaking via SHG polar maps, and PL spectra confirm new excitonic peaks emerging at zero magnetic field with characteristic magneto-optical responses.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the physical platform ($	ext{WSe}_2$/perovskite), presents experimental evidence of exciton brightening (PL/SHG), develops a theoretical model based on symmetry breaking and SOC, and finally demonstrates control via electric field tuning and twist angle dependence.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Long-lived dark excitons in monolayer WSe2 present promising candidates for carrying spin and valley information, but their optical access and spin manipulation have conventionally required the use of strong external magnetic fields. Here, using a ferroelectric hybrid perovskite heterostructure, we leverage the ferroelectric proximity effect to break the WSe2's in-plane rotational symmetry and brighten the spin-forbidden dark excitons under zero magnetic field conditions. Furthermore, we show that the twist angle between the WSe2 and perovskite crystals controls the ferroelectric coupling strength and valley-contrasting polarization. Our proposed mechanism, supported by a four-band tight-binding model, suggests that the ferroelectric proximity effect induces an asymmetric intersublattice interaction, generating an effective in-plane spin-orbit coupling (SOC) field that rotates spin/valley polarization and brightens dark excitons. Our work establishes ferroelectric proximity coupling as an electrically reconfigurable, magnetic-field-free strategy for spin exciton control in two-dimensional semiconductors.

</details>

### [Quantum Thermal Logic Gates](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06432v1)

**Authors:** Shuvadip Ghosh, Arnab Ghosh, Bivas Dutta, Papiya Maity
