**Summary.** The authors propose and theoretically analyze Quantum Thermal Logic Gates (QTLGs) implemented using coupled quantum dots connected to thermal reservoirs. By calculating steady-state heat currents, they demonstrate that the system's response exhibits a perfect mapping to classical logic functions like AND, OR, and NOT. This work suggests a pathway for ultra-low power computation by utilizing controlled heat flow in nanoscale electronic circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges quantum thermodynamics with computation, showing how energy dissipation (heat flow) can be harnessed as a controllable information carrier in solid-state quantum devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper proposes developing quantum thermal logic gates (QTLGs) that mimic classical electronic logic operations using heat current in nanoscale quantum systems.

**Main result.** A one-to-one correspondence is established between QTLG operations and classical logic gates (AND, OR, NOT, NAND), demonstrating the feasibility of energy-efficient computation via thermal effects.

**Method.** The analysis uses the Lindblad master equation to model time evolution, deriving steady-state heat and particle currents from transition rates governed by temperature gradients.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a coupled quantum-dot (CQD) structure tunnel-coupled to multiple metallic thermal reservoirs (leads). The dynamics are described by the Lindblad master equation acting on the reduced density matrix of the dots.

**Key observables.** Heat current ($J^Q$) and particle current ($J^N$), which are compared against predefined thresholds ($J_0^Q, J_1^Q$) to determine the logic output (logic-0 or logic-1).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Input logic states are defined by reservoir temperatures ($T_S \lesssim 50 	ext{ mK}$ for logic-0; $T_S \gtrsim 200 	ext{ mK}$ for logic-1); the system relies on temperature gradients to drive heat flow.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes weak coupling (Born-Markov approximation) and that inter-dot particle hopping is prohibited in certain analyses. The logic operation depends critically on the directionality of heat flow (thermal diode effect).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematics of the QTLG setups, showing operational cycles for Buffer, NOT, OR, AND, and NAND gates, with truth tables derived from measured heat current dependence on input temperatures.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops the theoretical framework by deriving steady-state currents using rate equations. It then applies this formalism to construct and analyze fundamental gates (Buffer, NOT) before extending the concept to complex double-input gates (OR, AND, NOR, NAND).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a new concept for quantum thermal logic gates -- analogous to classical electronic logic gates -- that exploit the heat current in a coupled quantum-dot system tunnel-coupled to metallic thermal reservoirs for logic operations in quantum circuits. We obtained a remarkable one-to-one correspondence with the structure of classical electronic logic gate circuits. An experimental setup is presented that demonstrates a realizable nano-electronic quantum circuit architecture for implementing such quantum thermal logic operations.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05498"></a>
### [Dissipation-coherence tradeoff for stochastic oscillations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05498v1)

**Authors:** Jie Gu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05498v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05498_figures/2606.05498_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Numerical verification of the theorem bound Y = σλR/λ2 I ≥η across model ensembles. Hollow markers are samples from the ensembles described in Sec. II B generated with k+ = 1 and k−= 0.5: translation-invariant rings with n ∈{10, 20, 50, 100}; heterogeneous rings with n = 50 and disorder strength s ∈{0.05, 0.10, 0.20, 0.30} (40 samples per s); and OBS-style fully connected models with n = 40, all- to-all link rate ε ∈{10−4, 3 × 10−4}, and outgoing disorder sout ∈{0.5, 1.0, 1.5} (25 samples per (ε, sout)). Only samples with coherence number N ≥0.05 are shown. The diagonal line shows Y = η.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper refines the Oberreiter-Barato-Seifert conjecture by introducing a mode-uniformity factor, $\eta$, into the lower bound on entropy production during stochastic oscillations. This factor quantifies how localized the dominant oscillatory eigenmode is within the system's state space. The work provides both theoretical derivations and practical, data-driven recipes to estimate this crucial parameter from limited experimental measurements.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a quantitative, state-dependent correction ($\eta$) to fundamental bounds in non-equilibrium statistical mechanics that govern energy dissipation during coherent oscillations, which is crucial for understanding open quantum systems dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Deriving a rigorous lower bound on the entropy produced per oscillation period for noisy, non-equilibrium systems that refines existing conjectures by accounting for mode localization.

**Main result.** The paper establishes $\Delta S \geq 4\pi^2 \eta N$, where $\eta$ is a mode-uniformity factor quantifying how evenly the oscillatory eigenmode is distributed across states. This shows that dissipation can be parametrically smaller if the dominant mode is localized ($\eta \ll 1$).

**Method.** The analysis uses Markov jump processes and spectral properties of the generator matrix, introducing $\eta$ to correct the standard eigenvalue-based bound.

**Model / system.** The study models stochastic oscillations using finite irreducible continuous-time Markov jump processes on discrete state spaces (e.g., a ring or general graph). The dynamics are governed by a Master Equation ($\dot{P}(t) = W P(t)$).

**Key observables.** Steady-state entropy production rate ($\sigma$), coherence number ($N$), and the mode-uniformity factor ($\eta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The ratio $\eta$ controls the sharpness of the dissipation-coherence tradeoff; the bound reduces to the original conjecture when $\eta=1$ (delocalized modes).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The rigorous derivation relies on algebraic inequalities related to the Dirichlet form, while estimation methods are heuristic and assume single-mode dominance.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 plots a tightness ratio $Y = \Delta S / (4\pi^2 N)$ versus $\eta$, demonstrating that the measured ratio satisfies $Y \geq \eta$ across various model ensembles.

**Paper structure.** The paper first derives the rigorous inequality incorporating $\eta$. It then outlines proof-of-principle routes for estimating $\eta$ from low-dimensional data (DMD/Koopman methods) and presents an eigenvector-free corollary based on the smallest stationary probability.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Autonomous noisy oscillations in biochemical and mesoscopic systems require nonequilibrium driving and therefore dissipation. A striking conjecture by Oberreiter, Barato, and Seifert (OBS) proposes a universal lower bound on the entropy produced per oscillation period in terms of the coherence number of the slowest oscillatory mode. Here we derive a weaker but rigorous lower bound that preserves the OBS structure while introducing a mode-uniformity factor that quantifies how evenly the oscillatory eigenmode is distributed across states in the steady-state inner product. The result makes explicit that an eigenvalue-only prefactor can fail when the dominant oscillatory mode is localized. We also outline a proof-of-principle route for estimating this factor from low-dimensional data under single-mode dominance and sufficiently informative measurements, and derive an eigenvector-free corollary using only the smallest stationary probability. Translation-invariant Markov jump processes on a ring provide a symmetry-protected class with $η=1$, so the refinement reduces to the OBS form; the drift--diffusion limit on a circle saturates the bound.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Gauging the Spacetime Code](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05664v1)

We insert spacetime locations, depicted by circles, at integer time-steps. (c) An example of a Clifford unitary: We allow the unitaries at each time-step to be arbitrarily complicated – in this case the unitary comprises two cNOT gates. We note that this circuit could also be differently discretized by separating the two cNOTs into...</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>Figure 3: Schematic illustrating examples of elementary circuit operators associated with circuit elements. (a) Propagators associated with the example depicted in Fig. 1c. Since the Clifford operation is purely unitary, there are no measurement slices or half- integer spacetime locations. <details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.05664_figures/2606.05664_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: A simple example of a detector formed by a ZZ measurement followed by a cNOT gate followed by a single qubit Z measurement, and several faults which violate the detector. </details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work develops a novel gauge-theoretic description by 'gauging' the spacetime code applied to quantum circuits. It successfully unifies fault tolerance analysis with topological physics by showing that circuit redundancies map directly onto measurable detectors within the resulting lattice gauge theory. This framework has broad implications for understanding measurement-based computation and noise in quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful mathematical formalism (gauge theory) to analyze robustness in quantum computation, connecting abstract concepts of symmetry breaking/restoration to physical error correction mechanisms relevant for AMO platforms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work aims to bridge the concepts of fault tolerance in quantum computation with the physics of dynamical phases of matter by constructing a gauge theory from the spacetime code.

**Main result.** A resulting lattice gauge theory is established that naturally incorporates fault tolerance elements, providing a unified language for describing measurement-based quantum computation and classical memory associated with topological states.

**Method.** The authors employ techniques from lattice gauge theory by 'gauging' the spacetime code. This involves defining elementary circuit operators (ECOs) to construct an Instantaneous Stabilizer Group (ISG) that characterizes the system's evolution under noise and measurement.

**Model / system.** The model centers on quantum circuits restricted to Clifford operations, which are discretized in time-spacetime. The framework uses concepts like spacetime errors, detectors (gauge invariant observables), and elementary circuit operators ($\mathcal{G}_C$).

**Key observables.** Gauge invariant observables (detectors), the Bare Effect $(o_f, p_f)$, and the structure of the Instantaneous Stabilizer Group $	ext{ISG}(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit depth ($T$), number of qubits ($N$), and the specific type/location of Pauli errors (data vs. measurement).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that fault tolerance can be captured by a gauge theory structure, and it relies on defining equivalence relations between spacetime faults using elementary circuit operators.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate conventions for Clifford operations across time-spacetime layers, showing the relationship between state evolution ($\psi_t$) and information geometry (ISG($t$)).

**Paper structure.** The paper builds logically by first defining the gauged spacetime code, then formalizing elementary circuit operators and the ISG structure. It proceeds to define fault equivalence via the 'Bare Effect' and finally proves that detectors correspond exactly to redundancies within this gauge framework.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In recent years, the spacetime code has arisen as a candidate for a unifying view of fault tolerance in space and time. On the other hand, the recent study of dynamical phases has increasingly turned its attention to fault tolerance as a notion of a dynamically stable process. In this work, I explore one pathway between the two, achieved by gauging the spacetime code. This gives rise to a lattice gauge theory that inherits the elements of fault tolerance associated with a circuit, with Gauss laws corresponding to equivalence relations between configurations of spacetime errors and Wilson loops corresponding to detectors. The obtained gauge theory finds a surprisingly wide array of applications, from quantum error correction to condensed matter physics, and even learning theory: (1) It contains in its description foliated computation, and hence gives rise to one version of a gauge theory for measurement-based quantum computation. (2) For a class of topologically ordered mixed states, it gives us a gauge-theoretic language to describe the classical memory associated with the state. (3) The gauge-invariant observables of the theory which describe detectors also coincide with the learnable degrees of freedom of circuit Pauli noise.

</details>

### [Generative Criticality in Large Language Model Temperature Scaling](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06238v1)

**Authors:** Huajian Ruan, Jinyang Li, Xingyu Guo, Lingxiao Wang  
**Type:** both · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06238v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

</details>

**Summary.** The authors apply statistical-field methods to analyze text generated by Large Language Models (LLMs), treating token embeddings as a physical system whose temperature is controlled by the softmax parameter $T$. They find evidence of a continuous phase transition at a critical temperature $T_c$, using diagnostics like susceptibility and intrinsic dimension. This suggests that LLM generation possesses an underlying collective statistical structure analogous to physical phase transitions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful analogy between complex machine learning outputs and established concepts from critical phenomena theory, suggesting that LLM behavior can be characterized using tools developed for condensed matter physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the underlying collective statistical structure of text generated by Large Language Models (LLMs) by treating token embeddings as a physical system.

**Main result.** The analysis reveals clear signatures consistent with a continuous phase transition near a critical temperature $T_c$, evidenced by sharp peaks in susceptibility, changes in an order parameter, and minima in intrinsic dimension.

**Method.** A statistical-field framework is employed, defining observables like susceptibility ($\chi$) and an order parameter ($\langle t angle$), alongside using the Two Nearest Neighbor (TwoNN) method to estimate the system's intrinsic dimension ($I_d$).

**Model / system.** The model treats LLM output as a one-dimensional chain of token embeddings, where the softmax temperature $T$ acts analogously to a thermodynamic temperature controlling sampling between deterministic and uniform regimes.

**Key observables.** Susceptibility ($\chi$), Order Parameter ($\langle t angle$), Intrinsic Dimension ($I_d$), and critical exponents ($\gamma$, $
u$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The softmax temperature $T$ is the primary control parameter, with a characteristic critical temperature $T_c$ identified across different model scales.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes that LLM generation can be mapped onto an effective statistical mechanics problem, though authors note the non-equilibrium nature of autoregressive generation requires further study.

**Figures summary.** Figures show $\chi(T)$ peaking near $T_c$, $\langle t angle$ changing sharply at $T_c$, and $I_d$ reaching a minimum near $T_c$ for various model sizes (Qwen3).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the statistical-field framework, applies diagnostics ($\chi, \langle t angle, I_d$) by varying temperature $T$, demonstrates consistent phase transition signatures across multiple models and scales, and concludes with implications for AI trustworthiness.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a statistical-field framework for text generated by large language models (LLMs), treating token embeddings as continuous spin variables on a one-dimensional chain. Defining a susceptibility from the connected two-point correlator and an order parameter from the ensemble-averaged embedding field, we vary the \texttt{softmax} temperature $T$ and observe a sharp susceptibility peak near a characteristic $T_c$ with power-law-like scaling, a concurrent rapid change in the order parameter, and a collapse onto a single semantic direction below $T_c$. The intrinsic dimension estimated by the two nearest neighbor (TwoNN) method independently corroborates these findings, reaching a minimum near $T_c$. Results are robust across model scales (Qwen3: 0.6B--32B) and prompt categories. While the phenomenology closely resembles a continuous phase transition, the non-equilibrium nature of autoregressive generation warrants further investigation. Our framework provides quantitative tools for probing the collective statistical structure of LLM outputs and suggests connections between decoding strategies and critical phenomena.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06412"></a>
### [Nonreversible Gauge Fields in Fokker--Planck Dynamics: Supersymmetric Hamiltonians and Learned Finite Forces](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06412v1)

**Authors:** Masayuki Ohzeki  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06412v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper unifies the study of stochastic processes by treating nonreversible perturbations as gauge fields within the Fokker-Planck framework. It introduces an optimal control method using Actor-Critic techniques to learn the best nonreversible force that accelerates sampling without altering the true equilibrium distribution. This has significant implications for understanding advanced MCMC and optimization algorithms.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful mathematical language (gauge theory) to analyze non-equilibrium dynamics in ML optimization, connecting concepts from quantum Hamiltonians/supersymmetry to practical stochastic gradient methods.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work develops an operator-theoretic framework for designing nonreversible perturbations to Fokker-Planck dynamics while ensuring the stationary probability density remains unchanged.

**Main result.** The authors show that optimal control of relaxation spectra can be achieved by learning a gauge field strength, which balances finite-time convergence speed against the cost of the applied nonreversible force.

**Method.** The problem is formulated as an optimal control problem solved using Actor-Critic methods, minimizing a regularized objective function derived from simulating forward Fokker-Planck dynamics under candidate gauge fields.

**Model / system.** The system models stochastic processes governed by Fokker-Planck equations, applied to optimization algorithms like Stochastic Gradient Langevin Dynamics (SGLD). The dynamics are analyzed using supersymmetric Hamiltonian formulations and nonreversible gauge potentials.

**Key observables.** Spectral gap ($\Delta$), stationary current ($J_{\pi}$), finite-time relaxation error ($\mathbb{E}[f_a(	heta)] - \mathbb{E}_{\pi}[f_a(	heta)]]$), and the learned gauge strength ($\gamma$ or $a^*_\lambda$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The regularization parameter $\lambda$, the time horizon $	au$, and the gauge field parameters ($\psi$ or $\gamma$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations like matching conditional moments to an Itô SDE, and the selection of a finite-time objective function is necessary because spectral gap alone is insufficient.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare trajectories in Gaussian benchmarks (showing chiral acceleration) and analyze relaxation in double-well potentials, contrasting reversible dynamics with those driven by learned nonreversible gauges.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from the general operator view of stationary-density-preserving perturbations as gauge fields, develops the optimal control objective using Actor-Critic methods, and validates this framework on anisotropic Gaussian and double-well benchmarks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We formulate stationary-density-preserving nonreversible perturbations of Fokker--Planck dynamics as gauge fields that deform relaxation spectra while leaving the invariant state fixed. When detailed balance holds, a similarity transformation maps the reversible Fokker--Planck operator to a Witten-Laplacian-type supersymmetric Hamiltonian; nonreversible gauges then appear as non-Hermitian perturbations that preserve the zero mode but modify the excited spectrum. This operator viewpoint gives a common language for relaxation gaps, circulating probability currents, hypocoercive acceleration, and finite control costs. We represent admissible gauge currents by antisymmetric tensor fields and identify the detailed-balance-violating Ohzeki--Ichiki force as a constant symplectic example whose infinite-strength limit is Hamiltonian dynamics. The continuous-time spectral gap alone does not select a finite gauge strength, so we introduce a finite-time regularized objective and an actor--critic procedure for learning the gauge. An exactly solvable anisotropic Gaussian Ornstein--Uhlenbeck benchmark separates the spectral transition from the finite-time optimum and shows that the learned gauge recovers the Lyapunov-equation optimum. A double-well benchmark then illustrates the same constrained selection in a nonconvex metastable landscape. Stochastic gradient methods enter this framework as physically relevant Fokker--Planck systems: mini-batch noise acts as an effective diffusion tensor, and adaptive methods such as Adam correspond to metric choices with possible nonequilibrium currents.

</details>

### [Complexity of the Laughlin wave function from the Dyson-orbital perspective](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05502v1)

**Authors:** J. M. Zhang, Y. Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05502v1>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper introduces the Dyson orbital as a diagnostic tool to measure the complexity of interacting fermionic wave functions. Applying this tool to the Laughlin state, the authors provide quantitative evidence that such strongly correlated states are fundamentally non-Fermi liquid, evidenced by specific power-law decays in particle addition overlaps.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter theory, the use of an orbital basis (Landau orbitals) and diagnostics based on single-particle addition/removal processes provides a generalizable mathematical framework for analyzing entanglement and correlations in complex quantum many-body systems relevant to AMO physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize the complexity of fermionic wave functions that deviate from simple Fermi sea descriptions, particularly in strongly correlated systems.

**Main result.** By analyzing the overlap using Dyson orbitals, the authors quantitatively demonstrate that the Laughlin wave function describes a non-Fermi liquid state, characterized by specific power-law decays.

**Method.** The core method involves defining and calculating an overlap functional related to the Dyson orbital ($\phi_D$), which measures how adding one particle changes the ground state wavefunction.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the Laughlin wave function, a model used to describe strongly correlated electron systems, specifically those relevant to the Fractional Quantum Hall Effect. It contrasts this with the simple noninteracting Fermi sea.

**Key observables.** The maximal overlap $O_{\max} = \|	ilde{\phi}_D\|^2$, which quantifies the connection between successive ground states; occupation numbers of Landau orbitals.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Filling factor ($m$), particle number ($N$); the decay exponent $eta = -(m-1)/2$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the ability to analytically determine or calculate the Dyson orbital for specific, highly structured wave functions like the Laughlin state.

**Figures summary.** Figures show $O_{\max}$ decaying with particle number $N$ following a power law (e.g., slopes $-1, -2, -3$), and illustrate that Landau orbitals serve as natural orbitals for the Laughlin states.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces Dyson orbitals as a diagnostic tool to measure wave function complexity, contrasting Fermi seas with correlated states. It then applies this formalism specifically to the Laughlin wave function, deriving quantitative evidence of its non-Fermi liquid nature through power-law decay analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Fermi sea is a simple and common concept in physics. However, a related and equally simple concept -- the Dyson orbital -- is far less discussed in physics, especially in textbooks. Yet, Dyson orbitals offer a valuable tool for characterizing the complexity of a fermionic wave functions, particularly in distinguishing between Fermi-sea-like and non-Fermi-sea-like states. As a preliminary application, we examine the Laughlin wave function and find the fortunate fact that the Dyson orbitals can be determined analytically. Further numerical data provides \emph{quantitative} evidence that the Laughlin wave function describes a strongly correlated, non-Fermi liquid state.

</details>

**Summary.** This work investigates the hidden-order state in the frustrated magnet Nd2Be2GeO7 using multiple advanced techniques like muSR and neutron diffraction. The key finding is that magnetic order appears only after field cycling, suggesting a unique ground state governed by spin-orbit coupling and frustration. This establishes the material as a distinct platform for studying quantum phase transitions.

**Why it may be interesting.** The history-dependent emergence of magnetic order upon field cycling is a hallmark of complex quantum phase transitions, providing experimental evidence for exotic correlated states near quantum criticality.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates a 'hidden order' state in the frustrated Shastry-Sutherland magnet Nd2Be2GeO7, characterized by thermodynamic anomalies without conventional magnetic ordering signatures.

**Main result.** The system exhibits a hidden-order phase whose magnetic signature only emerges after cycling an applied magnetic field, suggesting proximity to quantum spin liquid or long-range ordered states.

**Method.** A combination of techniques was used, including specific heat measurements, zero-field and longitudinal-field muon spin relaxation (muSR), neutron diffraction, and Electron Spin Resonance (ESR).

**Model / system.** The physical system is the Shastry-Sutherland magnet Nd2Be2GeO7. The magnetic behavior is modeled by considering the interplay of spin-orbit coupling, crystal field effects, and magnetic frustration.

**Key observables.** Sharp specific heat anomaly at 250 mK; absence of static magnetism in zero-field muSR/neutron diffraction; appearance of magnetic Bragg peaks only after field cycling; coherent spin fluctuations on the order of gigahertz.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The excitation gap ($\Delta$ or $\delta$) is consistently found around $8.5 - 8.7$ meV; fluctuation rates ($
u$) are in the GHz range.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the observed phenomena can be explained by a combination of CEF effects, magnetic frustration, and proximity to quantum critical points.

**Figures summary.** Figures show CEF excitations via INS, evolution of neutron diffraction intensities under field cycling (Fig. 2b), and temperature dependence of susceptibility/magnetization.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the hidden-order nature through multiple measurements (specific heat, muSR, NPD). It then uses detailed analysis of CEF excitations and field response to distinguish this state from known frustrated magnets, culminating in a discussion of its unique quantum ground state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hidden order typically manifests as a thermodynamic phase transition without a conventional order parameter, leaving its true nature concealed even at the lowest temperatures. In the frustrated Shastry-Sutherland magnet Nd$_2$Be$_2$GeO$_7$, we observe a related yet fundamentally distinct phenomenon. A sharp specific-heat anomaly appears at 250 mK, but zero-field neutron diffraction and muon spin relaxation detect no static magnetism down to 100 and 30 mK, respectively, pointing to a hidden-order state. Remarkably, this hidden order does not emerge under an applied magnetic field, but instead reveals itself only after the field is applied and subsequently removed where magnetic Bragg peaks appear, albeit with strongly suppressed moments. A glassy state is ruled out by ac susceptibility and specific heat measurements. Complementary $μ$SR measurements reveal coherent spin fluctuations at a rate on the order of gigahertz. Taken together, these results suggest that the system lies in close proximity to the quantum spin liquid and long-range magnetic order state such that a small perturbation can effectively drive the system towards distinct ground states. These findings also distinguish Nd$_2$Be$_2$GeO$_7$ from known frustrated systems, establishing it as a unique platform where the synergistic interplay among the spin-orbit coupling, crystal field, and magnetic frustration leads to unexpected quantum states.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05542"></a>
### [Thermalization with Gaussian Quantum Cellular Automata](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05542v1)

**Authors:** Roman Geiko, Jake Gerenraich  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05542v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05542_figures/2606.05542_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1 LH, UH, R, and E stand for the local and uniform hyperbolicity, regularity and everydayness, respectively. The shaded region LH ∩R contains the GQCAs for which we prove thermalization.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper rigorously proves that many-body bosonic lattice systems evolving under Gaussian Quantum Cellular Automata (GQCAs) thermalize to infinite temperature if the dynamics exhibit sufficient hyperbolic properties. The proof relies on advanced techniques from functional analysis, generalizing the Riemann–Lebesgue lemma to show that local observables lose memory of their initial state over time.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides rigorous, mathematically deep criteria for when quantum systems governed by simple, local rules will reach equilibrium, which is a cornerstone problem in non-equilibrium statistical mechanics and quantum information theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is rigorously establishing conditions on quantum dynamics (Gaussian Quantum Cellular Automata) that guarantee thermalization—the approach to infinite temperature equilibrium—for many-body bosonic lattice systems.

**Main result.** Thermalization is proven under two sufficient conditions: global hyperbolicity or local hyperbolicity combined with regularity, leading to the decay of expectation values of local Weyl operators towards the infinite-temperature state.

**Method.** The analysis uses techniques from functional analysis and operator theory, generalizing the Riemann–Lebesgue lemma to bound expectation values $\omega(W(\xi_k))$ by exploiting exponential growth in phase space combined with finite density moment conditions.

**Model / system.** The system consists of bosonic lattice systems on $\mathbb{Z}^d$ whose time evolution is governed by translation-invariant Gaussian Quantum Cellular Automata (GQCAs). The dynamics are analyzed via the action on local Weyl operators $W(\xi)$.

**Key observables.** Local Weyl operators $\omega(W(\xi))$, expectation values of local observables, and the particle density moment $\omega_{ho_\Gamma}(N_\Gamma)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The lattice dimension $d$, the hyperbolicity constants ($\gamma$), and the uniform bound on the particle density $
u$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The initial state must be locally normal, and the dynamics must satisfy specific hyperbolic conditions (global or local) related to the symplectic map $\Phi$ governing the evolution.

**Figures summary.** Not specified in the notes; the structure relies on theorems and lemmas establishing decay rates based on spectral properties of the underlying maps.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the necessary mathematical tools (Weyl algebra, local normality) to deriving key estimates (Riemann-Lebesgue generalization via commutator norms), culminating in proving thermalization under specific hyperbolic conditions for the GQCA dynamics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the long-time dynamics of many-body bosonic lattice systems under translation-invariant Gaussian quantum cellular automata. We formulate two sets of conditions on GQCAs which separately guarantee thermalization of any state on the local Weyl algebra to the infinite temperature state, whenever the state is locally normal and has uniformly bounded particle density. Our main intermediate result is a quantum many-body generalization of the classic Riemann-Lebesgue lemma which is a bound on expectation values of local Weyl operators involving their support and the state's particle density.

</details>

### [Opinion dynamics on social proximity disordered networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05349v1)

<sub>Fig. 3(c) shows the average clustering coefficient ⟨C⟩, which quanti- fies the tendency of neighboring agents to form locally interconnected groups. **Summary.** This paper models opinion dynamics on social networks whose connections are governed by physical proximity (Gabriel graphs). By introducing controlled spatial disorder, the authors study the resulting consensus-dissent phase transitions. They rigorously demonstrate that despite geometric heterogeneity, the critical behavior remains universal, matching the 2D Ising model predictions.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides a concrete example of how geometric embedding and local interaction rules can constrain the critical exponents of complex systems, suggesting that dimensionality is dictated by topology rather than just connectivity structure.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how local social pressure, mediated by geometric proximity constraints, shapes collective opinion formation dynamics.

**Main result.** The model exhibits second-order phase transitions whose critical behavior remains compatible with the two-dimensional Ising universality class, regardless of moderate levels of spatial disorder.

**Method.** Analysis involves simulating majority-vote opinion dynamics on Gabriel graphs and characterizing criticality using finite-size scaling techniques (magnetization, susceptibility, Binder's cumulant).

**Model / system.** The system models social opinion dynamics ($\sigma_i = \pm 1$) where interactions are restricted by the geometric proximity rule of Gabriel graphs. Disorder is introduced via random displacement parameters ($s$), and consensus breakdown is controlled by a nonconformity parameter ($q$).

**Key observables.** Magnetization, susceptibility, Binder's fourth-order cumulant, critical exponents ($eta/
u$, $\gamma/
u$, $1/
u$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Geometric disorder strength ($s$), Nonconformity level ($q$), System size ($L$ or $N$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the system's critical behavior can be accurately described by either volumetric or standard hyperscaling relations, and that planar, spatially constrained networks retain dimensionality $d=2$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate Gabriel graph construction (showing proximity rules), structural evolution under disorder ($s$), and data collapse plots for finite-size scaling analysis using both linear and volumetric methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the structure of Gabriel graphs as a function of disorder ($s$) to determine network metrics ($\langle k angle, \langle C angle, \langle \ell angle$). It then links these structural changes to the critical behavior of opinion dynamics by varying nonconformity ($q$), culminating in rigorous finite-size scaling tests confirming Ising universality.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding how local social pressure shapes collective opinion formation is essential in modern society, with implications in sociology, politics, finance, and technology. Complex networks are a powerful framework for investigating these processes and for representing social interactions in emergent phenomena, and canonical models such as random, scale-free, and small-world networks remain most relevant when Euclidean geometric proximity and contiguous interactions are not the dominant organizing factors. Our investigation explores the effects of Gabriel graphs, a spatially embedded network that utilizes proximity-based interactions, on social opinion dynamics. Using a geometric disorder parameter that randomly displaces agent coordinates, we transition from square lattices of social interactions to spatial random networks that preserve local constraints while introducing strong topological heterogeneity. Opinion dynamics operate through social pressure, with a level of nonconformity that affects dissent among opinions. We analyze magnetization, susceptibility, and Binder's fourth-order cumulant to characterize the emergence and breakdown of global social consensus. The model exhibits second-order phase transitions whose behavior depends on both geometric disorder and nonconformity levels. We use volumetric scaling and provide a reference frame appropriate for characterizing criticality in regular and heterogeneous interaction topologies. We find that the standard critical exponents remain compatible with the two-dimensional Ising universality class. Furthermore, the results suggest that spatially constrained, planar, and globally connected interaction topologies preserve the dimensionality of space in critical dynamics analysis.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05822"></a>
### [Study on the validity of IPT+parquet method as an impurity solver in DMFT focusing on orbital fluctuations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05822v1)

**Authors:** Aira Yamada, Ryota Mizuno, Masayuki Ochi, Kazuhiko Kuroki, Takuma Ohashi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05822v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

**Summary.** This paper rigorously validates the IPT+parquet method as an advanced impurity solver for DMFT in multi-orbital systems. It demonstrates that this combined technique accurately captures subtle physics, particularly orbital fluctuations, which simpler methods fail to resolve. The work provides a reliable computational tool for studying strongly correlated materials beyond simple Hubbard models.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter strongly correlated physics, the development of robust, scalable numerical solvers like IPT+parquet is a general computational tool relevant to any field requiring complex many-body simulations (e.g., quantum chemistry or AMO systems with strong interactions).

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to validate the IPT+parquet method as a computationally inexpensive yet accurate impurity solver for Dynamical Mean Field Theory (DMFT), specifically focusing on its ability to capture orbital fluctuations in multiband systems.

**Main result.** IPT+parquet successfully captures competition between orbital fluctuation channels, which conventional IPT fails to describe. The inclusion of the parquet contribution is crucial for accurately modeling these multi-orbital Mott physics effects.

**Method.** The authors compare results from IPT+parquet against numerically exact solvers (like CT-QMC) and simpler approximations by analyzing the self-energy calculation within DMFT.

**Model / system.** The research uses a multiband Hubbard model, applied to two-orbital systems on both square lattices and Bethe lattices. The Hamiltonian includes hopping integrals and various Coulomb repulsion terms ($U, U', J, J'$).

**Key observables.** Quasiparticle weight ($Z_\alpha$), which probes correlation effects; band-filling ($n_\alpha$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strengths ($U, U'$) and the ratio between them relative to Hund coupling ($J$); the system is studied near half-filling.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method relies on combining Iterative Perturbation Theory (IPT) with the parquet equation. Modifications were necessary for stability when interaction parameters deviate from specific relationships (e.g., $U' = U -2J$).

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the quasiparticle weight ($Z$) as a function of filling or interaction strength across multiple methods (Exact, IPT+parquet, conventional IPT), demonstrating where agreement is achieved and where failures occur.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the theoretical framework (DMFT, Parquet, IPT) and then validates IPT+parquet by applying it to two-orbital models on both square and Bethe lattices. Subsequent sections detail necessary modifications to the parquet calculation for improved stability.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A breakdown of calculations with exact impurity solvers in the dynamical mean field theory in multiband systems easily occurs due to the expensive numerical cost. To overcome this practical difficulty, three of the present authors developed an inexpensive and reliable impurity solver by combining the iterative perturbation theory (IPT) and parquet equation, and named it IPT+parquet [R. Mizuno, et al., Phys. Rev. B 104, 035160 (2021).]. In this study, we validate IPT+parquet focusing on the orbital fluctuation by comparing the numerically exact impurity solvers. We confirm that IPT+parquet can capture competition between orbital fluctuation channels, which the conventional IPT cannot capture.

</details>

### [Early psychosis shows deviations in scaling behaviour within a critical regime](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06290v1)

**Summary.** This study analyzes resting-state fMRI data from early psychosis patients using advanced techniques rooted in statistical physics, including the Phenomenological Renormalization Group. By comparing scaling exponents across multiple observables, the authors argue that psychosis involves a reorganization of collective dynamics rather than a simple breakdown of critical organization. This provides a quantitative framework linking concepts from condensed matter physics to psychiatric neuroscience.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work applies concepts from critical phenomena and renormalization group theory—tools central to statistical mechanics—to analyze complex biological time series data (fMRI). The quantitative comparison of scaling exponents provides a rigorous physical framework for studying brain function.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how large-scale brain activity organizes across space and time, specifically looking for deviations from critical scaling behavior in early psychosis compared to healthy controls.

**Main result.** Early psychosis is characterized not by a simple loss of critical dynamics, but rather by a systematic reorganization of collective dynamics within a preserved scale-invariant regime. This reorganization involves differential shifts in various scaling exponents ($eta$, $\alpha$, $\mu$, $z$).

**Method.** The researchers combined the Phenomenological Renormalization Group (PRG) framework with temporal analyses like Power Spectral Density (PSD) and Detrended Fluctuation Analysis (DFA) on resting-state fMRI data.

**Model / system.** The physical system is human brain activity measured via functional Magnetic Resonance Imaging (fMRI) BOLD signals. The analysis treats the collective dynamics as a complex, multi-scale system exhibiting critical-like organization.

**Key observables.** Scaling exponents ($eta$, $\alpha$, $\mu$, $z$), DFA exponent ($\alpha_{DFA}$), PSD exponent ($eta_{PSD}$), and characteristic time scales ($	au_c$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis is performed across multiple spatial/temporal scales (coarse-graining levels $K$) and uses various binarization thresholds (1 to 2 SD).

**Assumptions / limitations.** It assumes that the PRG framework provides a principled way to characterize collective dynamics, and that the observed scaling changes reflect underlying functional reorganization rather than mere noise artifacts.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the progression of the PRG coarse-graining process, compare calculated exponents ($eta$, $\alpha$, etc.) between controls and patients across different thresholds, and show temporal scaling analyses (PSD/DFA) indicating altered long-range correlations in psychosis.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a structure moving from establishing the theoretical framework (PRG, DFA) to applying it rigorously to fMRI data. It details preprocessing, presents results comparing healthy vs. patient groups across multiple scaling metrics, and concludes by synthesizing these findings into a model of dynamic reorganization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Accumulating evidence suggests that large-scale brain activity exhibits scale-invariant dynamics consistent with operation in a near-critical regime. Such dynamics have been associated with long-range correlations, efficient information processing, and the emergence of collective organization. While altered criticality-related measures have been reported in psychiatric disorders, previous findings remain fragmented across observables and modalities, making it unclear whether different scaling measures capture a common alteration of large-scale brain dynamics. Here, we investigated scaling properties in resting-state fMRI data from individuals with early psychosis and healthy controls. We combined a phenomenological renormalization group (PRG) framework with power spectral density (PSD) and detrended fluctuation analysis (DFA) to characterize collective dynamics across scales. In healthy controls, resting-state activity exhibited non-trivial scaling behavior consistent with critical-like organization. Early psychosis participants showed the same overall phenomenology of scale-invariant organization, but with systematic shifts in scaling exponents across multiple observables. These findings indicate that early psychosis is not characterized by a simple loss of critical-like dynamics, but rather by a reorganization of collective dynamics within a preserved scaling regime. More broadly, our results suggest that combining coarse-graining approaches with temporal scaling analyses provides a principled framework for studying large-scale brain dynamics in psychiatric disorders.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06246"></a>
### [Multiple Quantum Hypothesis Testing: One-Shot Pairwise Bounds and Sharp Asymptotics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06246v1)

**Authors:** Hao-Chung Cheng, Po-Chieh Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06246v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.06246_figures/2606.06246_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Illustration of the multiple quantum hypothesis testing.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper rigorously analyzes the minimum error probability when distinguishing between multiple unknown quantum states. It establishes sharp bounds by connecting the problem to operator means (like the harmonic mean) and proves that these bounds are achievable asymptotically. The results settle long-standing conjectures regarding the performance limits of quantum hypothesis testing in infinite dimensions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides fundamental theoretical tools—sharp error exponents and generalized divergence measures—that govern the limits of quantum measurement capabilities, which is crucial for understanding resource theories in quantum information processing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is establishing rigorous, sharp error bounds for discriminating among multiple quantum states ($\{ho_i\}$) in both finite-shot and asymptotic (many-copy) regimes.

**Main result.** The authors establish a dimension-free one-shot upper bound on the minimum probability of error and prove that this bound is achievable asymptotically, settling open problems regarding the multiple quantum Chernoff distance in infinite dimensions.

**Method.** The analysis employs advanced tools from quantum information theory, including deriving bounds using operator means (like the harmonic mean), applying large deviation theory (Cramér's condition), and utilizing techniques like spectral decomposition and local limit theorems.

**Model / system.** The system is general quantum hypothesis testing involving density operators $ho_i$ on a separable Hilbert space. The test uses an optimal Positive Operator-Valued Measure (POVM) $\{\Lambda_i\}$ to minimize the error probability.

**Key observables.** Minimum probability of decision error ($	ext{Err}^\star$), Quantum Chernoff distance ($C(ho_1, \dots, ho_M)$), and harmonic mean quantities ($	ext{HMs}(A, B)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of hypotheses ($M$), prior probabilities ($p_i$), number of copies ($n$), and the asymptotic parameter $s$ in operator means.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes conditions required for large deviation theorems (e.g., non-orthogonality, finite moments) or relies on extending results from finite-rank approximations to general trace-class operators.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the setup of multiple quantum hypothesis testing using a POVM $\{\Lambda_i\}_{i=1}^M$. Equations detail asymptotic decay rates and harmonic mean relationships.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing general one-shot bounds for multiple hypotheses, to deriving sharp characterizations for the binary case using harmonic means, and finally extending these results to derive almost-exact asymptotics in the i.i.d. limit.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider Bayesian discrimination among multiple quantum states and establish a dimension-free one-shot upper bound on the minimum probability of error in terms of the sum of pairwise errors. This resolves a conjecture of Audenaert and Mosonyi [J. Math. Phys. 55 (2014)] and improves the multiple quantum Chernoff bound of Li [Ann. Statist. 44 (2016)] by removing its dimension-dependent prefactor. In the asymptotic many-copy regime, our bound proves the achievability of the multiple quantum Chernoff distance for arbitrary separable Hilbert spaces, thereby settling the previously open infinite-dimensional case, and further yields constant-factor sharp asymptotics for the optimal error probability.   In binary quantum hypothesis testing, we prove that the minimum error probability is characterized, up to universal constants, by a trace harmonic-mean quantity. Consequently, the optimal binary quantum error probability is within a factor of two of the optimal classical error probability for the associated Nussbaum-Szkoła distributions, complementing the lower bound of Nussbaum and Szkoła [Ann. Statist. 37 (2009)].

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Quantum-limited estimation of atmospheric turbulence via spatial mode decomposition](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06101v1)

**Authors:** A. Hrebeniuk, M. Klen, I. Karuseichyk, N. Treps, A. A. Semenov

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper theoretically investigates how precisely one can measure the spatial coherence radius of light passing through turbulent air. It compares two detection methods—Direct Imaging and Spatial Mode Decomposition—using quantum metrology tools like Fisher Information. The key finding is that SpaDe provides a substantially more precise estimation than DI, especially when the turbulence effects are weak.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges advanced topics in quantum estimation theory with applied physics challenges like atmospheric sensing, providing concrete metrics (FI/QFI) to quantify measurement improvement beyond classical limits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is establishing the ultimate quantum precision limit for estimating the optical spatial coherence radius (Fried parameter, $r_0$) of atmospheric turbulence.

**Main result.** Spatial Mode Decomposition (SpaDe) significantly outperforms conventional Direct Imaging (DI) in the weak-scintillation regime, approaching the theoretical quantum limit when properly implemented.

**Method.** The analysis uses quantum metrology principles, comparing the Fisher Information (FI) derived from DI versus SpaDe measurements against the Quantum Fisher Information (QFI).

**Model / system.** The system involves light propagating through a turbulent atmosphere, modeled using single-photon density operators and characterized by field correlation functions ($\Gamma$). The analysis compares two measurement schemes: Direct Imaging (DI) and Spatial Mode Decomposition (SpaDe).

**Key observables.** Fisher Information (FI), Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), spatial coherence radius ($r_0$), and the ratio FI/QFI.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Weak field regime ($\eta n_{th} \ll 1$); normalized parameters $	heta_2$ and $\sigma_2$; the aperture size $D$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the weak-field approximation and often employs a Gaussian approximation for the complex correlation functions to derive analytical results.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the ratio of FI to QFI for DI vs. SpaDe, showing SpaDe's superior performance in the weak-scintillation regime; other figures plot these metrics as functions of $r_0/D$.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops by first establishing the quantum estimation framework using CR bounds and QFI. It then analyzes two measurement techniques (DI vs. SpaDe) under Gaussian approximations, deriving explicit FI expressions for both. Finally, it compares these results against realistic models to demonstrate the advantage of SpaDe.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We establish the ultimate precision limit for estimating the optical spatial coherence radius (Fried parameter) within a quantum metrological framework. In the weak field regime, we show that spatial-mode decomposition -- originally introduced for superresolution imaging -- enables substantially more precise estimation than conventional direct imaging when the receiver aperture is smaller than the coherence radius.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05992"></a>
### [Rapid Gaussian Boson Sampling Circuit Screening for GKP States Creation via a Two-Stage Machine Learning Surrogate](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05992v1)

**Authors:** Mohammad Amin Khanpour, Hossein Davoodi Yeganeh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05992v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05992_figures/2606.05992_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. GBS circuit schematic for GKP state preparation. The circuit consists of (left to right): single-mode squeezing gates on all nmodes input modes, a rectangular Clements beamsplitter interferometer, photon-number-resolving (PNR) detectors post-selecting on herald pattern m = (m1, . . . , mnmodes−1) on the first nmodes −1 modes, and the undetected output mode which collapses into the heralded GKP state.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05992_figures/2606.05992_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Two-stage ML surrogate pipeline block diagram. Stage 1 (Pattern Classifier) takes the circuit parameter vector and configuration metadata as input and outputs the predicted herald pattern. Stage 2 (Fidelity and Probability Regressors) takes the same input augmented with the Stage 1 pattern statistics and outputs predicted fidelity F and log-probability y = log10(p). If F ≥0.90, the circuit is passed to exact quantum simulation (Strawberry Fields + thewalrus) for confirmed validation.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.05992_figures/2606.05992_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Scatter plot of ML-predicted vs. simulation-confirmed fidelity (left) and log10 post-selection probability (right) on the 170-sample holdout set. Points are coloured by circuit mode count (nmodes ∈{3, 4, 5}). The dashed diagonal line indicates perfect agreement. The shaded band indicates ±0.05 fidelity error. Points above or below the F = 0.90 threshold line that are misclassified by the surrogate are highlighted.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.05992_figures/2606.05992_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Representative Wigner functions W(q, p) from the exact simulation step, plotted over q, p ∈[−3, 3] (in units of √π). (a) Circuit C1 (nmax = 8, three modes, ∆= 10 dB, WLN = 0.700): characteristic GKP lattice structure with prominent negativity (red/blue regions) at phase-space lattice points. (b) Circuit C7 (nmax = 8, three modes, ∆= 5 dB, WLN = 0.022): the GKP pattern is visually present but with negligible negativity, consistent with near-classical behaviour. (c) Circuit F1 (nmax = 12, five modes, ∆= 11 dB, sign +): the Wigner function bears no resemblance to a GKP state, consistent with Fsim = 0.307, confirming the catastrophic surrogate failure.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces an ML-based surrogate pipeline to efficiently screen candidate circuits for generating GKP states using Gaussian Boson Sampling. By predicting performance metrics like fidelity and success probability without calculating computationally prohibitive hafnians, it reduces simulation time by 90%. This framework allows researchers to focus expensive quantum simulations only on the most promising circuit designs.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the practical scalability barrier in continuous variable quantum computing by replacing intractable exponential calculations with machine learning surrogates, a key theme for theoretical quantum information scientists.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is developing a scalable method to generate Gottesman-Kitaev-Preskill (GKP) states, which are crucial non-Gaussian resources for fault-tolerant photonic quantum computation.

**Main result.** A two-stage Machine Learning surrogate pipeline was developed that predicts GKP circuit viability with 90.0% accuracy, drastically reducing the computational load by approximately 90% compared to full hafnian calculations.

**Method.** The authors employed a cascade ML architecture (Stage 1 for pattern prediction, Stage 2 for fidelity/probability regression) acting as a gatekeeper before performing expensive, exact quantum simulations on promising candidates.

**Model / system.** The system is photonic quantum computation utilizing Gaussian Boson Sampling (GBS). GKP states are encoded by leveraging the periodicity of the wavefunction lattice in phase space, making them robust against small errors.

**Key observables.** GKP state fidelity ($F$), post-selection probability, Wigner function, and Wigner logarithmic negativity ($	ext{WLN}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of optical modes (3–5), squeezing amplitudes/strengths ($\Delta$), and circuit parameters defining the interferometer structure.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The surrogate is a computational filter, not a replacement for simulation; systematic failure modes exist when test circuits deviate from the training distribution's sign conventions for squeezing parameters.

**Figures summary.** Figures likely compare ML-predicted vs. simulated fidelity and probability on holdout sets, visually confirming GKP state characteristics (e.g., Wigner functions) for successful versus failed candidates.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the need for GKP states in photonic QC, identifies the computational bottleneck of hafnian evaluation in GBS, proposes the two-stage ML surrogate pipeline, details its training and application, and validates its efficiency by comparing predictions against full quantum simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Gottesman-Kitaev-Preskill (GKP) states are essential non-Gaussian resources for fault-tolerant photonic quantum computing, enabling logical qubit encoding with intrinsic robustness against errors. Several approaches to GKP state preparation have been explored, including measurement-based protocols in circuit QED and trapped-ion systems, cat-state breeding, and photon-subtraction schemes. However, these methods are either restricted to specific platforms or require deep non-Gaussian resource chains with exponentially low success probabilities. Gaussian Boson Sampling (GBS) offers a compelling all-photonic alternative by generating non-Gaussian states through measurement-induced nonlinearity, without the need for matter-based ancilla or active feedforward. Nevertheless, its practical implementation is limited by the exponential computational cost of evaluating matrix hafnians-#P-complete functions that govern photon-number probabilities. To address this challenge, we introduce a two-stage Histogram Gradient Boosting surrogate pipeline that predicts, without any hafnian computation, the optimal heralding pattern, circuit fidelity, and post-selection probability for candidate GBS circuits, while reserving exact quantum simulation exclusively for surrogate-selected candidates. Trained on circuit configurations across 3-5 optical modes, the surrogate achieves 90.0% GKP-detection accuracy on a held-out set, representing a 23.7 percentage-point improvement over the baseline, with a fidelity mean absolute error of 0.032 and a log-scale post-selection probability $R^2 = 0.837$, reducing the total simulation burden by approximately 90%.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.05297"></a>
### [Continuous-variable ADAPT-VQE for bosonic lattice models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05297v1)

**Authors:** Dimitrios Athanasakos, Gloria Tejedor-García, Jack Y. Araz, Mafalda Ramôa, Bharath Sambasivam, Sophia E. Economou, Felix Ringer  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05297v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** The paper presents Continuous-variable ADAPT-VQE to find ground states for bosonic lattice models like the Bose-Hubbard and Kitaev chains. It demonstrates that this continuous-variable approach generates significantly shallower quantum circuits than standard VQE methods. By incorporating symmetry constraints and advanced optimization techniques, it offers a powerful tool for simulating complex condensed matter physics problems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the practical implementation challenges of simulating interacting quantum many-body systems on near-term quantum hardware by optimizing the required circuit depth using adaptive techniques tailored for bosonic Hamiltonians.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to efficiently prepare the ground state of bosonic lattice models using quantum algorithms, specifically addressing circuit depth limitations.

**Main result.** The Continuous-variable ADAPT-VQE approach was shown to yield significantly shallower circuits compared to traditional Hamiltonian-based VQE methods for these systems.

**Method.** The authors employ CV-ADAPT-VQE, an adaptive variational quantum eigensolver that constructs the ansatz by selecting symmetry-preserving bosonic operators from a tailored pool. This is contrasted with HVA-CV-VQE, which uses unitaries derived directly from the Hamiltonian terms.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on bosonic lattice models, including the Bose-Hubbard (BH) model and the bosonic Kitaev chain (BKC), sometimes extended with an on-site Kerr interaction. These systems are modeled using continuous variables described by quadrature operators ($\hat{q}, \hat{p}$).

**Key observables.** Ground state energy expectation value ($E$), circuit depth, fidelity ($I$), and the total boson number $\langle \hat{N}_{tot} angle$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size ($N_S$), local Fock cutoff dimension ($D$), interaction strengths ($J, U, \Delta$), and chemical potential ($\mu$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Classical simulations require truncating the infinite Hilbert space to a finite dimension $D$. The ADAPT-VQE relies on constructing an ansatz from operators respecting known global symmetries.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate energy error ($\Delta E$) and infidelity ($I$) convergence versus gate count for BH and BKC models, comparing standard vs. tiled operator pools, and showing the impact of warm-up circuits and Hessian recycling on optimization steps.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces CV-ADAPT-VQE for bosonic systems (BH/BKC), details the mathematical framework using continuous variables, compares its performance against HVA-CV-VQE, and discusses advanced optimizations like operator tiling and Hessian recycling to improve resource scaling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a continuous-variable adaptive variational quantum eigensolver (CV-ADAPT-VQE). As concrete examples, we consider the ground-state preparation for (i) the Bose-Hubbard model and (ii) the bosonic Kitaev chain, including its extension with an on-site Kerr interaction. The former conserves the total boson number, while the latter conserves global parity. We construct symmetry-preserving operator pools tailored to each case and show, using GPU-based classical simulations, that CV-ADAPT-VQE results in significantly shallower circuits compared to Hamiltonian-based VQE approaches. Our results point toward direct applications in quantum simulations of condensed-matter systems, quantum chemistry, and high-energy physics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06204"></a>
### [Semidefinite-programming hierarchies for classically simulable state families](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06204v1)

**Authors:** Mengyan Li, Yanning Jia, Fenzhuo Guo, Haifeng Dong, Sujuan Qin, Fei Gao  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06204v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.06204_figures/2606.06204_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Geometric illustration of the hierarchy bounds for criti- cal visibility. The black line represents the depolarizing trajectory {Λv(ρ)}v∈[0,1], interpolating between the maximally mixed family {I/d} and the target family ρ. Intersections of this trajectory with differ- ent hierarchy levels determine the visibility bounds χℓ, shown as red points. As the hierarchy level increases, these bounds decrease monotonically and converge to the exact critical visibility χ, shown in blue. The dashed line illustrates an affine witness separating the target family from a relaxation level.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a complete Semidefinite Programming (SDP) hierarchy to rigorously characterize classically simulable families of quantum states. By reformulating classical simulability as a POVM feasibility problem, they provide computable upper bounds on quantum advantages like critical visibility. This offers a systematic convex-optimization framework for certifying the nonclassical nature of quantum resources.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, systematic convex-optimization tool for certifying when quantum correlations are genuinely nonclassical, which is fundamental for understanding the limits of quantum computation and communication protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is developing a complete mathematical characterization of quantum state families that are classically simulable, which signifies the absence of irreducible quantum advantage.

**Main result.** The authors establish a complete Semidefinite Programming (SDP) hierarchy that characterizes classical simulability by relating it to the projective simulability of auxiliary Positive-Operator-Valued Measures (POVMs).

**Method.** They reformulate classical simulability as a feasibility problem involving deterministic response functions and POVMs, solving this using an asymptotically complete SDP relaxation hierarchy.

**Model / system.** The study concerns quantum state families $\{ho_x\}_{x=1}^n$ on a $d$-dimensional Hilbert space. The simulation aspect is modeled by checking if the measurement statistics admit a local hidden-variable model, which is mathematically equivalent to POVM simulability.

**Key observables.** Critical classical visibility ($\chi(ho)$), and the feasibility of SDP constraints derived from the hierarchy levels $\ell$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension $d$ of the Hilbert space, the number of states $n$, and the relaxation level $\ell \ge 2$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on establishing asymptotic completeness for the SDP hierarchy, which is proven using techniques related to the quantum de Finetti theorem.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the geometric convergence of the SDP bounds ($\chi_\ell(ho)$) towards the true critical visibility $\chi$ for various symmetric state families.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by defining classical simulability, reducing it to POVM simulability, developing the SDP hierarchy (both canonical and aggregated lifts), proving its asymptotic completeness, and finally applying this framework to compute bounds on quantum advantage measures like critical visibility.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Identifying whether a state family admits an irreducible quantum advantage is a fundamental task in quantum resource theory and quantum information processing. Here we study classically simulable state families, namely those residing within the convex hull of pairwise commuting families and therefore admitting a classical explanation. We develop a complete semidefinite-programming (SDP) hierarchy characterizing the set of classically simulable state families in arbitrary finite dimension. The key step is to reformulate classical simulability as a feasibility problem over deterministic response functions and auxiliary positive-operator-valued measures (POVMs) simulable by rank-one projective measurements. We establish a complete SDP hierarchy for rank-one projectively simulable POVMs and transfer the resulting characterization to state families, yielding both primal feasibility tests and dual affine witnesses certifying failure of classical simulability. Applying the hierarchy to state families mixed with depolarizing noise gives computable upper bounds on the critical classical visibility, which are matched by explicit classical simulations in several symmetric examples. These results provide a systematic convex-optimization framework for certifying classical simulability of quantum state families.

</details>

**Summary.** This work investigates quantum geometry effects in magic angle twisted bilayer graphene by measuring the circular photogalvanic effect. By carefully separating this effect from thermal contributions, the authors map out how the CPGE vanishes at specific electron fillings. This vanishing signals a fundamental symmetry breaking mechanism strongly linked to the material's Berry curvature dipole structure.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter, the use of optical pumping to probe geometric phases (Berry curvature) in a correlated system like MATBG touches upon non-equilibrium quantum dynamics relevant to open quantum systems and topological physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to reveal quantum geometry effects, specifically those related to the Berry curvature dipole, in magic angle twisted bilayer graphene (MATBG) using the circular photogalvanic effect (CPGE).

**Main result.** A robust CPGE is observed over wide doping ranges, but it vanishes precisely in the filling range $-2.5 < 
u < -1.5$, suggesting a novel symmetry breaking mechanism linked to the Berry curvature dipole structure.

**Method.** The research combines photo-transport measurements using circularly polarized light with theoretical calculations based on the Berry curvature dipole tensor and mean-field approximations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is MATBG, encapsulated in a van der Waals stack. Theoretical analysis utilizes models incorporating strain and calculating electronic structure properties like the Berry curvature dipole within a Hartree formalism.

**Key observables.** Circular Photogalvanic Effect (CPGE) current ($\Delta I_{ph}$), longitudinal resistance ($R_{xx}$), and photovoltage/thermoelectric voltage measurements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Band filling ($
u$), temperature ($T$), incident light wavelength ($\lambda = 1550$ nm, $\hbar\omega \approx 0.8$ eV).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the observed zero-CPGE plateau correlates with the BCD mechanism and that photoexcited carriers cascade into the flat band in a manner describable by modified Shockley-Ramo theorems.

**Figures summary.** Figures show measured CPGE current vs. filling compared to conductivity tracking, alongside theoretical calculations of the Berry-Curvature Dipole (BCD) under strain.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically measures photocurrents using polarization dependence to isolate the CPGE from thermoelectric effects. It then compares these experimental results across various doping levels and temperatures against theoretical predictions derived from the BCD formalism, pinpointing specific regimes of symmetry breaking.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report a photocurrent studies of a magic angle twisted bilayer graphene device using near infrared light. Through photocurrent imaging and polarization dependence, we separate the photo-thermoelectric effect from the photogalvanic effect. We observe a circular photogalvanic effect (CPGE) over a wide range of doping and temperature. The CPGE at normal incidence constraints the symmetry of the system to C$_1$, and points to a Berry curvature dipole, in agreement with theoretical predictions for strained graphene. Remarkably, the CPGE vanishes for filling $-2.5 < ν< -1.5$, suggesting an additional symmetry breaking in that regime. Insight into this effect is obtained through Berry curvature dipole calculations, which emphasize a novel symmetry breaking effect near $ν=-2$.

</details>
