<a id="paper-2606.06318"></a>
### [Reliability of asymptotic work extraction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06318v1)

**Authors:** Kaito Watanabe, Bartosz Regula, Marco Tomamichel, Ryuji Takagi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06318v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work rigorously compares two models for free operations—Gibbs-preserving and thermal—in the context of quantum work extraction. It demonstrates that while both yield the same asymptotic work rate, their reliability (error exponent) differs significantly. This reveals a fundamental operational gap, suggesting that physically constrained processes are more limited than mathematically convenient ones.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a deep theoretical understanding of operational constraints in quantum thermodynamics, showing that mathematical convenience (GPO) can mask physical limitations (TO) when precision is required.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper challenges the assumption that the optimal asymptotic work extraction rate is universally characterized by the Helmholtz free energy when comparing different classes of allowed quantum operations.

**Main result.** A significant operational gap exists between Gibbs-preserving operations and physically realizable thermal operations, particularly concerning the reliability (error exponent) of the extraction process in the zero-rate limit.

**Method.** The analysis uses resource theory concepts applied to work extraction, comparing performance metrics derived from quantum hypothesis testing divergences (Petz vs. sandwiched Rényi entropies).

**Model / system.** The study models work extraction from a general quantum state ($ho$) coupled to an auxiliary 'work battery' ($X_m$), analyzed under two classes of free operations: Gibbs-preserving operations (GPO) and physically motivated thermal operations (TO).

**Key observables.** Optimal asymptotic error exponents ($B_{GPO,ext}, B_{TO,ext}$) and the zero-rate error exponent ($	ext{BZ.R.}$), quantified by various quantum relative entropies.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The inverse temperature $eta$, the rate of work extraction $r$, and the coherence/incoherence condition $[ho, 	au]$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on comparing asymptotic limits derived from generalized Rényi divergences and assumes specific covariance conditions for certain data-processing inequalities to hold.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the strict separation of reliability functions ($	ext{H}_r > e^{	ext{H}_r}$) when quantum states exhibit coherence, quantifying the operational gap between GPO and TO.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the equivalence of work definitions at the asymptotic rate (first order), then rigorously comparing error exponents in fixed-rate regimes, and finally focusing on the zero-rate limit to quantify the fundamental difference between operation classes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Extracting work from quantum states is a fundamental task in quantum thermodynamics. Previous studies have primarily focused on determining the best achievable rate of work extraction, and remarkably, this characterization appeared to remain unchanged regardless of the choice of allowed processes: whether one considers the operationally motivated class of energy-conserving thermal operations, or the axiomatic class of Gibbs-preserving operations, the optimal extractable work is given by the Helmholtz free energy. Here, we challenge this perspective, showing that a more refined analysis of the asymptotic performance of work extraction reveals significant differences in the performance for the two different classes of free operations. Precisely, we focus on the trade-off between the extraction rate and its reliability, characterized by the optimal asymptotic speed at which the extraction error can be suppressed. We establish that the reliability of Gibbs-preserving operations and of thermal operations are respectively characterized by the Petz and the sandwiched Rényi relative entropies, demonstrating that the former in general strictly outperforms the latter, and providing new interpretations of several information-theoretic divergences. Our analysis reveals that operational constraints such as energy conservation impose stronger limitations on the achievable precision of quantum tasks than can be inferred from their asymptotic rates, thereby questioning the use of Gibbs-preserving operations as a mathematically convenient substitute for the physically realizable thermal processes.

</details>

### [Majorana-like fermion physics: Emergence of topologically protected vortical states in graphene interacting with an electromagnetic field](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05746v1)

**Summary.** This paper explores Majorana-like fermion physics in graphene interacting with magnetic fields, predicting the formation of topologically protected vortical states. The key finding is that these states manifest as three distinct Fano resonances in the optical conductivity, establishing a theoretical link between topological matter and neutrino oscillation phenomenology.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter topology, the explicit analogy drawn to three-flavour neutrino oscillations provides a rich theoretical framework for understanding how fundamental particle physics concepts can manifest as measurable electronic band structure features in solid-state systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the emergence of topologically protected vortical states in graphene when subjected to an external electromagnetic field, linking Majorana-like fermion physics to observable optical responses.

**Main result.** The model predicts that three distinct topological resonances appear in the optical conductivity, providing evidence for three non-trivial Majorana-like modes whose behavior mimics a toy model for neutrino oscillations.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis involving constructing and solving coupled Hamiltonian equations for Majorana-like fermions, followed by calculating the complex dielectric permittivity and analyzing resulting Fano resonances.

**Model / system.** The physical system is graphene, modeled using a quasi-relativistic framework that incorporates topological features via Majorana-like mass terms derived from coupling orbital and valley currents. The model is explicitly compared to neutrino oscillation physics.

**Key observables.** Fano resonances in the optical conductivity ($\sigma_{Ohm}$), non-zero Zak phases ($Q$), and the appearance of three distinct energy gaps/resonances corresponding to different topological charges (flavors).

**Important parameters / regimes.** External electromagnetic field strength, momentum/wavevector ($\vec{q}$), and parameters governing the Majorana mass terms ($\Sigma_{BA}\Sigma_{AB}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on treating the system as a toy model for neutrino oscillations and assumes specific symmetries (like electron-hole symmetry) to derive topological constraints.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the graphene Brillouin zone, band structures showing Landau quantization effects, and complex conductivity plots demonstrating Fano resonances corresponding to multiple Majorana modes.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the quasi-relativistic Hamiltonian for Majorana fermions in an EM field, deriving coupling terms between orbital and valley currents, calculating topological invariants (Zak phases), and finally predicting observable signatures like Fano resonances that confirm the multi-flavor nature of the topological states.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Within the framework of a quasi-relativistic model of graphene that admits topologically nontrivial Majorana-like quasiparticle excitations, appearance of such vortex states in the frequency dependencies of the complex dielectric permittivity of the system subjected to an external electromagnetic field has been examined. The vortex graphene states possess topological charges (flavours ) being nonzero Zak phases. Interaction effects of Majorana-like modes have been qualitatively related to the formation of Fano resonances in the optical response. The constructed topological model of graphene may be considered as a toy model of three-flavour mass-neutrino oscillations.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05866"></a>
### [Symmetry-Selective Stabilization of Charge-Density Wave in ScV$_6$Sn$_6$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05866v1)

**Authors:** A. Korshunov, C. -Y. Lim, J. Corral-Sertal, G. Garbarino, D. Chernyshov, A. Rajapitamahuni, C. Yi, S. Roychowdhury, C. Shekhar, C. Felser, V. Pardo, Ella M. Schmidt, S. Blanco-Canosa

**Summary.** This work demonstrates that applying controlled uniaxial strain can selectively stabilize and enhance Charge-Density Wave order in $	ext{ScV}_6	ext{Sn}_6$. By inducing a symmetry-lowering orthorhombic distortion, the researchers show that the CDW transition temperature increases significantly. The findings combine advanced X-ray diffraction measurements with sophisticated Potts model simulations to provide a powerful tuning mechanism for frustrated quantum materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on CDW physics, the use of symmetry breaking via external fields (strain) to tune an intrinsic many-body instability is highly relevant to controlling quantum phases in condensed matter systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how anisotropic lattice deformation can selectively stabilize and enhance Charge-Density Wave (CDW) order in frustrated kagome metals, contrasting with general suppression mechanisms.

**Main result.** Uniaxial strain acts as a powerful symmetry-selective tuning parameter, stabilizing the CDW state by inducing an orthorhombic distortion that lifts domain degeneracy. The enhancement is attributed to the ordering of rattling chains within the lattice structure.

**Method.** The research combines high-resolution X-ray diffraction under controlled uniaxial strain (experiment) with phenomenological modeling using a three-state Potts framework and Monte Carlo simulations (theory).

**Model / system.** The physical system is $	ext{ScV}_6	ext{Sn}_6$, a kagome metal exhibiting CDW order. The underlying physics is modeled by mapping the low-temperature state onto an effective three-state Potts model incorporating strain-dependent Ising couplings.

**Key observables.** CDW transition temperature ($T_{	ext{CDW}}$), lattice parameters ($	ext{a}_o, 	ext{b}_o, 	ext{c}_o$), diffuse scattering intensity, and the evolution of structural symmetry from hexagonal to orthorhombic.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Uniaxial strain magnitude (e.g., $-0.6\%$ compression along $[	ext{HH}0]$), temperature ($T_{	ext{CDW}}$), and coupling constants within the Potts model.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The CDW physics can be effectively mapped onto a three-state Potts model, and the enhancement mechanism is primarily driven by anisotropic lattice deformation breaking rotational symmetry.

**Figures summary.** Figures detail structural transformations from hexagonal to orthorhombic phases under strain; simulations show $T_{	ext{CDW}}$ increasing monotonically with compressive strain; ARPES confirms electronic renormalization localized at specific terminations ($	ext{V}_3	ext{Sn}$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the structural consequences of uniaxial strain (XRD/structural analysis), then modeling the resulting order-disorder transition using a Potts model (MC simulations), and finally confirming the electronic signature via ARPES.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Charge-density-wave (CDW) order in kagome metals is highly sensitive to external tuning parameters such as chemical substitution and hydrostatic pressure, which generally suppress long-range order. Here, using high-resolution X-ray diffraction under controlled uniaxial strain, we show that anisotropic lattice deformation instead stabilizes and enhances the CDW state in ScV$_6$Sn$_6$. Compression along the [H00] and [HH0] directions lowers the crystal symmetry from hexagonal to orthorhombic, lifts the degeneracy between symmetry-equivalent in-plane CDW domains, and promotes long-range order while preserving the underlying trimer instability. Phonon calculations indicate only a moderate stabilization of the imaginary flat phonon mode, demonstrating that the increase in T$_\mathrm{CDW}$ is primarily driven by the in-plane ordering of the Sn$^\mathrm{T}$--Sc--Sn$^\mathrm{T}$ \textit{rattling} chains within the frustrated kagome lattice. A phenomenological model incorporating strain-dependent Ising couplings within a three-state Potts framework successfully reproduces the evolution of T$_\mathrm{CDW}$ under compression and captures the continuous nature of the transition. Our results establish uniaxial strain as a powerful symmetry-selective tuning parameter for order-disorder transformations in frustrated lattices.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05690"></a>
### [Learning Hamiltonians at Long Times](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05690v1)

**Authors:** Constantin Cedillo Vayson de Pradenne, Jordan Cotler, Hsin-Yuan Huang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05690v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05690_figures/2606.05690_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Hamiltonian learning from weak equilibration. (a) Schematic of the mech- anism. Under long-time evolution, local observables in the search space generically lose their infinite-temperature autocorrelation, while the Hamiltonian direction is exactly conserved. Classi- cal shadow measurements on random product-state probes produce a data matrix whose approxi- mate null vector recovers H. (b) Weak-equilibration diagnostic for random local Hamiltonians. For each system size n, we track how strongly the most slowly decorrelating normalized local observable orthogonal to H retains its initial value under the dynamics. This quantity starts at 1 and rapidly settles to a plateau below 1....</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper develops the theoretical tools to uniquely identify an unknown local Hamiltonian $H$ governing a quantum system by analyzing its time evolution $U(t)$. By proving that the commutator gap must be non-zero, it establishes that all other local observables lose their correlations (weak equilibration) over time. This provides a powerful diagnostic tool for characterizing the dynamics of complex many-body systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous theoretical framework connecting fundamental concepts in quantum dynamics—namely, spectral gaps and conservation laws—to practical tasks like quantum state tomography and Hamiltonian reconstruction from limited measurements. It bridges condensed matter theory with quantum information theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is determining an unknown local Hamiltonian $H$ governing a quantum system from observing only the time evolution unitary $U(t) = e^{-iHt}$ at a single, potentially very long, time $t$. This is framed as an identifiability problem.

**Main result.** For broad ensembles of Hamiltonians, a single long-time measurement suffices to uniquely determine the Hamiltonian direction up to scale by showing that all other local observables lose their autocorrelation exponentially or polynomially fast.

**Method.** The analysis relies on establishing and bounding the commutator gap—both static ($\lVert [A, H] Vert_F$) and dynamic ($\lVert [U(t), A] Vert_F$)—which quantifies how strongly the dynamics rotate an observable $A$. This gap is then used to bound the decay of local autocorrelations.

**Model / system.** The system consists of quantum many-body systems (e.g., TFIM, general 2-local models) evolving unitarily in a Hilbert space spanned by Pauli strings. The Hamiltonian $H$ and observables $A$ are restricted to be sums of local Pauli operators.

**Key observables.** The primary diagnostics are the dynamic commutator $\lVert [U(t), A] Vert_F$, the static commutator gap, and the normalized autocorrelation function $C_A(t) = rac{1}{d}	ext{tr}(A(t)A)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis depends on the system size ($n$), the sampling time ($t$ or $T$), and the existence of a non-zero commutator gap, which must be inverse-polynomial in $n$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results are proven for 'broad ensembles' of random Hamiltonians; worst-case integrable systems where many observables commute with $H$ remain outside the scope. The learning guarantee is an average-case result.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the decay of local autocorrelations (dropping below 1) and show that the dynamical commutator gap scales predictably with system size, supporting the theoretical bounds derived from static gaps.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops theory by first relating dynamic to static commutator gaps using time-evolution identities. It then proves lower bounds on these gaps for specific local structures (Pauli-injective/geometrically local regimes). Finally, it applies these gap estimates to derive weak equilibration statements and the final Hamiltonian learning guarantee.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the problem of learning an unknown $n$-qubit Hamiltonian $H$ from $U = e^{-iHt}$ for a single time $t$, where $t$ may be arbitrarily large. For broad families of local Hamiltonians, we prove that, with high probability over $H$ and $t$, any sum of local observables $A$ that is normalized and orthogonal to $H$ satisfies $\tfrac{1}{2^n}\|[U(t),A]\|_F^2 \geq 1/\text{poly}(n)$. The Hamiltonian is therefore the unique approximately conserved local observable, and we can efficiently recover $H$, up to scale, as the approximate null vector of a data matrix built from random product-state inputs and classical shadows. As a corollary, we obtain a weak equilibration statement: the infinite-temperature autocorrelation of every sum of local observables orthogonal to $H$ decays by at least an inverse-polynomial amount.

</details>

### [Realistic quantum device data synthesized by consumer AI and how to identify it](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05472v1)

**Summary.** This paper investigates the ability of consumer generative AI to synthesize highly convincing data for quantum electronic devices. By simulating measurements like qubit spectroscopy and Majorana signatures, the authors show that plausible fake datasets can be generated using basic physics knowledge. The key takeaway is a call for mandatory sharing of large volumes of raw primary data to authenticate real scientific findings.

**Why it may be interesting.** It highlights a critical methodological challenge in modern experimental physics: distinguishing genuine discovery from sophisticated AI fabrication, which impacts how theoretical predictions are validated against empirical evidence.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The increasing capability of generative AI to synthesize highly realistic numerical data for quantum physics, raising concerns about misrepresenting synthetic outputs as genuine experimental results.

**Main result.** AI can generate convincing datasets mimicking iconic quantum effects (e.g., Majorana modes, Josephson junctions) using basic physical models, but the defense against this is sharing large volumes of raw primary data.

**Method.** The authors demonstrate AI synthesis by prompting LLMs to write and execute Python code that simulates known physics equations and adds realistic noise/artifacts to generate synthetic measurements.

**Model / system.** The focus is on quantum electronic devices, including superconducting transmon qubits, Majorana nanowires, and Quantum Point Contacts (QPCs). The underlying physics relies on established models like the qubit Hamiltonian or tunneling conductance equations.

**Key observables.** Spectroscopy measurements ($S_{21}$ vs. gate voltage/power), Rabi oscillations, zero-bias anomalies in tunneling spectroscopy, and quantized conductance plateaus.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gate voltage, microwave power, temperature, magnetic field (implied for Majorana studies).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary defense against AI fabrication is the mandatory sharing of large volumes of raw, unprocessed primary data files and metadata.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate synthetic transmon qubit characterization (single/two-tone spectroscopy, Rabi oscillations) and augmented Majorana nanowire tunneling spectra to suggest topological superconductivity.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the problem (AI synthesis capability), demonstrates its power across multiple quantum systems using specific examples (transmons, QPCs), and concludes by recommending data sharing as the necessary authentication standard.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

With the advance of generative artificial intelligence (AI) synthetic texts and images have become commonplace. These capabilities offer clear benefits, but have also raised a number of ethical concerns that often have to do with misrepresenting AI outputs as genuine material. A lesser known capability of generative AI is to perform the basic analysis, processing and even synthesis of numerical data. This raises the question of whether AI can be used to imitate experimental data that an expert would consider scientifically meaningful and on par with data in the figures of peer-reviewed manuscripts? In this paper, we focus on synthesizing data inspired by well-known experiments done frequently on quantum electronic devices. This field is related to information technologies such as spintronics and quantum computing, and is considered data-rich and data-driven. We demonstrate that it is possible to generate dramatic signals associated with iconic effects such as quantum bit control, Majorana fermions, Josephson effects, quantum dots and wires using widely available ChatGPT. We find that because some of the clearest data from quantum devices can be expressed in terms of relatively basic mathematical models, AI does not need to learn on the specialized body of data. Instead, knowledge of the physics equations and of the basic features of experimental signals can go a long way towards building a realistic dataset. We also demonstrate that real data can be augmented by AI, and that AI can mimic the noise of common scientific instruments. To help assure that published data come from experiments and are not synthesized by AI, we recommend sharing large volumes of the primary data. While it is straightforward for AI to mimic a few sets of data, consistently generating long measured sequences poses sufficient barriers to the proliferation of undisclosed synthetic data.

</details>

### [Fermiology and the Candidate Chiral Superconductor in Rhombohedral Tetralayer Graphene](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05356v1)

**Summary.** This research investigates the superconducting mechanism in rhombohedral tetralayer graphene by correlating transport measurements with theoretical calculations. The key finding is that the normal state supporting superconductivity is far more complex than previously assumed, exhibiting a 'multitone' signature incompatible with simple Fermi surface models. This forces a re-evaluation of how pairing and topological properties emerge from correlated electron systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The investigation into exotic electronic phases like chiral superconductivity and the failure of simple models to explain measured quantum oscillation patterns provides a rich playground for understanding strong correlations in 2D materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to characterize the normal state feriology of rhombohedral tetralayer graphene (R4G) to understand the pairing mechanism and potential chirality in a candidate chiral superconductor.

**Main result.** Experimental quantum oscillation measurements reveal a complex 'multitone' normal state that is inconsistent with simple models, suggesting the superconducting phase emerges from a highly intricate electronic structure.

**Method.** The work combines experimental magneto-transport measurements (Shubnikov-de Haas oscillations) with extensive theoretical modeling using Self-consistent Hartree-Fock (SCHF) calculations to map out competing correlated electron phases.

**Model / system.** The physical system is rhombohedral tetralayer graphene, modeled using continuum Hamiltonians that incorporate spin and valley polarization effects. Theoretical analysis explores various correlated states like quarter metals, nematic, annular, and intervalley coherent (IVC) phases.

**Key observables.** Shubnikov-de Haas oscillation frequencies/tones, superconducting critical temperature ($T_c$), anomalous Hall response, and flavor polarization ratios ($n_{\max}/n$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Carrier density ($n$), displacement field ($D/\epsilon_0$), dielectric constant ($\epsilon$), and magnetic field ($B$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on semiclassical Onsager quantization for quantum oscillations, but the observed multitone state challenges simple single-band models. Theoretical calculations often neglect spin-orbit coupling or assume specific symmetries.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams showing transitions between different metallic states (QM $	o$ MT), show $R_{xx}$ mapping out superconducting domes and polarized phases, and display FFTs confirming the transition from single to multi-tone quantum oscillation patterns.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing experimental evidence of superconductivity and complex normal state oscillations. It then uses detailed theoretical calculations (HF/SCHF) to map out possible correlated ground states (QM, IVC, nematic), ultimately concluding that the observed multitone state is beyond simple mean-field predictions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Chiral superconductivity, in which the phase of the superconducting order parameter winds in momentum space, has long been sought for its close link to topological superconductivity. Recent work reported a superconductor in rhombohedral multilayer graphene emerging from a time-reversal symmetry broken normal state, suggesting that it could be a chiral superconductor. However, the possibility of chirality depends on the symmetry and structure of the normal-state Fermi surface, which have not been directly measured. Here we measure quantum oscillations in rhombohedral tetralayer graphene over a broad range of the phase diagram, including the superconducting region. At densities well above the onset of superconductivity, we reproduce previously-reported oscillations consistent with a spin- and valley-polarized quarter metal with a single simply-connected Fermi pocket. As the carrier density is reduced, we find a transition to a complex "multitone" state that persists through the superconducting region. This state's spectrum of quantum oscillations is incompatible with a simply-connected quarter metal. The next-simplest candidate normal states suggested by our microscopic modeling (fully-polarized annular, nematic, and three-pocket states) are inconsistent with our measurements, albeit difficult to rule out entirely. The normal state is thus seen to be richer than previously envisaged, reshaping the search for the superconducting mechanism and the possible chirality of the pairing channel.

</details>

### [Ideal Quantum Geometry for Fractional Chern Insulators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05496v1)

**Authors:** Jennifer Cano, Jie Wang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05496v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05496_figures/2606.05496_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05496_figures/2606.05496_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.05496_figures/2606.05496_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper rigorously connects the concept of 'ideal quantum geometry'—where Bloch bands mimic Landau levels by saturating specific geometric bounds—to the realization of Fractional Chern Insulators (FCIs). While ideal band geometry is a necessary condition, the authors demonstrate that it is not sufficient to guarantee the FCI ground state stability, highlighting the critical role of electron-electron interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it bridges single-particle band theory (a common tool in condensed matter) with interacting many-body physics. The explicit failure of geometric criteria to predict interaction stability provides crucial physical insight for designing topological materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the role of quantum geometry (Berry curvature, metric) in single-particle Bloch bands to understand the conditions necessary for realizing Fractional Chern Insulators (FCIs). It specifically addresses whether geometric properties alone can predict the stability of interacting many-body topological phases.

**Main result.** While ideal band geometry (saturation of geometric bounds) is a strong indicator, it proves insufficient on its own to guarantee the FCI ground state; the robustness of the Laughlin state against changes in single-particle band geometry demonstrates this limitation.

**Method.** The analysis combines theoretical derivations involving quantum geometric tensors (QGT), topological invariants like the Chern number, and comparisons between ideal Bloch bands and established models like Landau levels. It uses analytical techniques to establish mathematical equivalences between different geometric conditions.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on single-band systems in two dimensions, including lattice analogs of Quantum Hall physics (e.g., the Kapit-Mueller model) and moiré materials. The underlying framework involves analyzing Bloch states $\vert u_k angle$ whose geometry is characterized by the Berry connection and curvature.

**Key observables.** Berry Curvature ($\Omega_k$), Chern Number ($C$), Quantum Metric, Trace Bound ($	ext{Tr} g_k$), and the QGT ($\mathcal{Q}_{ab}^k$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Filling factor ($
u$), magnetic flux ($\phi$), lattice parameters (e.g., moiré periodicity), and interaction strength (Hubbard repulsion).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that ideal bands are characterized by the saturation of geometric bounds, which implies momentum-space holomorphicity of cell-periodic wavefunctions.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the deviation from the trace condition and the many-body spectrum when changing orbital embeddings in the Kapit-Mueller model, showing spectral robustness despite geometric violation.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by defining quantum geometry concepts (Berry curvature/metric), establishing criteria for 'ideal bands' (saturation of bounds $	ext{Tr} g_k = |\Omega_k|$), demonstrating these conditions are equivalent to momentum-space holomorphicity, and finally testing this criterion against the known robustness of FCI ground states in lattice models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum geometry plays a fundamental role in many aspects of condensed matter physics. Among its central objects are the Berry curvature and the quantum metric -- quantities that, while distinct, are intertwined through geometric constraints. In this article, we survey recent progress in understanding when and how this bound is saturated, with particular emphasis on the emergence of momentum-space holomorphicity of Bloch states. These developments highlight a profound connection between certain ideal Bloch bands and the Hilbert space structure of the lowest Landau level. We elucidate this relationship through a review of quantum Hall physics in both homogeneous and spatially varying magnetic fields, and conclude by exploring its implications for the search for fractionalized phases in emerging platforms, including moiré materials.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05856"></a>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.05856_figures/2606.05856_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Graphical representation of the F-symbol. The red, black, and light-blue lines denote the bZσ 2, D, and bZτ 2 defects, respectively. The F-symbol compares two fusion paths, (a ⊗D) ⊗b and a ⊗(D ⊗b), corresponding to opposite orders of fusing the invertible defects a and b into the non-invertible defect D. In the lattice realization, the relative sign is determined by the commutation relation between the corresponding fusion operators.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper develops an advanced theoretical framework to understand Deconfined Quantum Critical Points (DQCPs) that evade conventional Landau theory. It achieves this by showing that these critical points can be mapped onto transitions governed by novel 'crystalline categorical symmetries' derived through a process called gauging anomalous onsite symmetries. The distinction between different symmetry types is rigorously established using the F-symbol of the underlying fusion category.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, mathematically rigorous framework for classifying quantum critical points in strongly correlated systems by extending symmetry concepts from standard group theory to full fusion categories. This has implications for understanding exotic phases beyond simple mean-field descriptions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses Deconfined Quantum Critical Points (DQCPs) which defy conventional Landau theory due to competing orders breaking incompatible internal and crystalline symmetries.

**Main result.** A class of DQCPs can be reinterpreted as Landau-type transitions by gauging anomalous onsite symmetries, leading to descriptions governed by crystalline categorical symmetries like Rep(D8)-type or Rep(H8)-type.

**Method.** The core method involves 'gauging' the system's anomalous symmetries and analyzing the resulting effective lattice Hamiltonians in a dual description. The distinction between candidate symmetries is made by calculating the F-symbol of the underlying fusion category.

**Model / system.** The analysis uses microscopic lattice models, specifically focusing on transitions between magnetic (FM) and Valence Bond Solid (VBS) orders in spin chains, exemplified by the FM–VBS and yAFM–VBS DQCPs.

**Key observables.** The F-symbol of the crystalline fusion category is the key diagnostic tool used to distinguish between different symmetry structures ($	ext{Rep}(D_8)$ vs $	ext{Rep}(H_8)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The transition parameter $\lambda$ governing the mixing between competing phases (e.g., $bH = (1 - \lambda) bH_{	ext{FM}} + \lambda bH_{	ext{VBS}}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that LSM anomalies provide a microscopic mechanism for generating these crystalline symmetries via the gauging procedure.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the mapping from DQCPs to categorical Landau transitions and use the F-symbol calculation to distinguish between different symmetry types ($	ext{Rep}(D_8)$ vs $	ext{Rep}(H_8)$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first identifying the failure of the standard Landau paradigm, then introducing gauging as a resolution mechanism. It develops this using specific lattice models (FM-VBS and yAFM-VBS), deriving distinct categorical symmetries ($	ext{Rep}(D_8)$ vs $	ext{Rep}(H_8)$) based on detailed calculations involving non-invertible defects and their associated F-symbols.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Deconfined quantum critical points (DQCPs) involving lattice symmetries evade the conventional Landau paradigm because the competing orders break incompatible internal and crystalline symmetries. We show that a class of DQCPs can nevertheless be understood as Landau-type transitions after gauging anomalous onsite symmetries. For spin chains with Lieb-Schultz-Mattis (LSM) anomalies, gauging produces a noninvertible lattice translation whose fusion closes only up to ordinary translations, giving rise to a crystalline categorical symmetry. In the gauged description, the original DQCP becomes a transition between different symmetry breaking patterns of this categorical symmetry. We demonstrate this mechanism in microscopic lattice models; the magnetic-valence-bond-solid (VBS) DQCP realizes a Rep($D_8$)-type crystalline categorical Landau transition, whereas a y-antiferromagnetic-VBS DQCP realizes a Rep($H_8$)-type one. Although Rep($D_8$) and Rep($H_8$) share the same fusion rules, they have inequivalent $F$-symbols and therefore define distinct categorical descriptions. Our results show that the universal categorical structure underlying these DQCPs is encoded in the full fusion category, rather than in the fusion ring alone.

</details>

**Summary.** The research demonstrates a functional trusted-node QKD link over 300 km using deployed commercial hardware integrated with multi-core fibers. Key findings include the necessity of advanced detectors (SNSPDs) for high performance and showing that the achievable key rate directly limits the fidelity of subsequent quantum-secured applications, such as image encryption.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it bridges fundamental quantum communication theory with practical engineering challenges, specifically addressing the integration of quantum signals into complex, noisy, and dynamically reconfigurable classical fiber infrastructure.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Demonstrating trusted-node Quantum Key Distribution (QKD) over long distances ($\sim 300$ km) in realistic communication networks that include heterogeneous fiber infrastructure and coexisting classical traffic.

**Main result.** The setup successfully demonstrates QKD operation with active core switching and autonomous reinitialization in a dynamic, multi-core fiber environment, while also showing how image fidelity depends on the available key budget.

**Method.** The authors implemented a trusted-node QKD link using commercial systems interfaced with external superconducting nanowire single-photon detectors (SNSPDs) over deployed SMF and MCFs.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a deployed quantum communication network spanning $\sim 303$ km, utilizing two sub-links connected by a trusted node. The LiU end incorporates a $33 	ext{ km}$ Multi-Core Fiber (MCF) to simulate metropolitan access links coexisting with classical Ethernet traffic and optical noise.

**Key observables.** Secret Key Rate ($	ext{SKR}$), Quantum Bit Error Rate ($	ext{QBER}$), Image Fidelity (PSNR, LPIPS).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Total distance of $\sim 303 	ext{ km}$; SNSPD detection efficiency and dark count rates; key budget size in kbits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system relies on Key Management Systems ($	ext{KMSs}$) buffers to smooth out short-term fluctuations in end-to-end key generation rate, and the classical link routing does not require symmetry.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental setup map, $	ext{SKR}$ and $	ext{QBER}$ measurements under varying noise levels (including SNSPD cycle effects), and a comparison of image reconstruction fidelity using JPEG 2000 versus deep-learning compression (JPEG AI) as a function of available key budget.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from characterizing the physical link setup (long-distance trusted node over SMF/MCF) to demonstrating robust operation under dynamic noise conditions, culminating in an application study showing how limited QKD throughput constrains high-level tasks like image encryption.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum key distribution (QKD) is increasingly considered for deployment in realistic communication networks, where long distances, heterogeneous fiber infrastructure, and coexistence with classical traffic present substantial challenges. Here, we demonstrate trusted-node QKD between Linköping University and the Stockholm hub of the Swedish national quantum communication infrastructure over 270 km of deployed single-mode fiber, extended by a 33 km multi-core fiber (MCF) segment emulating a metropolitan access link, for a total distance of 303 km. The two sub-links use commercial QKD systems whose receivers are interfaced with external superconducting nanowire single-photon detectors, enabling operation at losses beyond those supported by standard internal gated-mode detectors. We operate the link while actively switching the QKD channel between two MCF cores, with co-propagating Ethernet traffic and injected broadband optical noise in the other cores. The results demonstrate the integration of commercial QKD into demanding, dynamically reconfigurable fiber infrastructure relevant to future hybrid quantum-classical networks. Finally, using the generated secret keys, we illustrate how limited and time-varying QKD throughput affects one-time-pad-protected image transmission: image fidelity depends strongly on the available QKD-generated key budget and the choice of compression algorithm, highlighting application-level challenges for QKD-based encryption in realistic scenarios.

</details>

### [On the Cryptographic Structure Required for Verifying Qubits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05527v1)

**Authors:** James Bartusek, Itay Shalit  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05527v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05527_figures/2606.05527_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Visualization of the parameter regimes captured by Theorem 1.1 and Theorem 1.2. The solid black line is 𝛿= 𝜖, and the dashed black line is 𝛿= 𝜖/2, so the region between is the region of parameters where (normal form) ToNC plausibly exists. The dark gray shade denotes the sub- region where we show that ToNC implies KA or OT (additionally assuming one-way functions for the latter). The blue curve is the key agreement threshold 𝛿= (5𝜖−1)/4, and the red curve is the oblivious transfer threshold 𝛿= (𝜖2 + 𝜖)/2. It remains open to show that ToNC in the white region below the solid black line implies KA or OT. However, we note that ToNC with robust completeness and any 𝜖/2 ≤𝛿&lt; 𝜖implies...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper rigorously connects the physical concept of testing for non-commuting observables on qubits to foundational cryptographic primitives like Key Agreement and Oblivious Transfer. By formalizing this Test of Non-Commutation (ToNC), the authors prove that satisfying quantum anti-commutation tests implies strong computational security guarantees, while also developing advanced post-quantum amplification tools for these protocols.

**Why it may be interesting.** For a theoretical physicist working on quantum information, this work provides deep connections between fundamental physical requirements (like anti-commutation in qubit verification) and the mathematical structure of modern cryptography. It suggests that verifying basic quantum properties might be as hard as achieving secure key exchange.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work aims to characterize the cryptographic structure required for verifying quantum computational processes, specifically by relating qubit tests to established primitives like Key Agreement (KA) and Oblivious Transfer (OT). It seeks to establish strong cryptography from classical tests of anti-commutation.

**Main result.** The authors prove that a Test of Non-Commutation (ToNC) implies classical-communication Key Agreement (KA), and ToNC combined with one-way functions implies Oblivious Transfer (OT). They also develop post-quantum amplification theorems for KA and OT.

**Method.** The methodology involves developing formal definitions for interactive quantum protocols (like $(\epsilon, \delta)$-ToNC) and using advanced cryptographic techniques such as hybrid arguments and sequential repetition to derive implications between these primitives.

**Model / system.** The primary model is the abstract concept of a qubit test, formalized as an interactive protocol involving two binary observables ($P_0, P_1$) that must anti-commute. The analysis focuses on the security parameters ($\epsilon, \delta$) governing the success and failure gaps of these protocols.

**Key observables.** The key observables are the advantage parameters $\epsilon$ (completeness/correctness) and $\delta$ (soundness/security gap) associated with the ToNC protocol. Other observables include quantum prediction advantages ($\gamma$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Critical parameter regimes include thresholds like $\delta < 5\epsilon^{-1}/4$ for KA implication, and the general security parameters $(\epsilon, \delta)$ that define the strength of the underlying protocols.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results rely on assumptions such as the existence of one-way functions (for OT) and the ability to perform post-quantum hardness amplification techniques, which are themselves based on established cryptographic theorems.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 visually summarizes the parameter regimes showing the thresholds ($\delta$ vs. $\epsilon$) under which ToNC implies KA or OT.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first defining the core primitive (ToNC), then establishing implications to classical cryptography (KA, OT) using specific parameter bounds, and finally developing advanced post-quantum amplification theorems for these cryptographic primitives.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Classically testing for the presence of anti-commuting operators on a quantum device is a critical tool underpinning recent progress in classical verification of quantum computation. While such tests can be based on cryptographic assumptions, known constructions rely on highly structured assumptions, e.g. trapdoor claw-free functions.   In this work, we seek to explain this state of affairs by constructing strong cryptography from (certain forms of) classical tests of anti-commutation. In particular, we formulate the notion of a test of non-commutation (ToNC), an interactive protocol between a quantum prover and classical verifier in which the prover's final-round response is obtained by measuring one of two binary observables $P_0,P_1$ depending on the verifier's challenge bit $c$. We prove that, for a broad range of parameters, ToNC implies classical-communication key agreement (KA), and ToNC combined with one-way functions implies oblivious transfer (OT).   Along the way, we develop tools for and provide the first known results on hardness amplification for post-quantum KA and OT, where communication is classical but adversaries may be quantum. In particular, we prove the following results of independent interest.   - Post-quantum hard-core measure theorem: For any efficiently sampleable high-min-entropy distribution $D$ over pairs $(x,b)$ such that quantum circuits have advantage at most $δ$ in predicting $b$ from $x$, there exists a sub-distribution $M\preceq D$ of density $(1-δ)$ on which $b$ is nearly optimally quantum-hard to predict.   - Post-quantum interactive XOR lemma: Given any classically-interactive protocol, if quantum adversaries have advantage at most $δ$ in guessing a private challenger bit $b$, then two sequential repetitions reduce the advantage for predicting the XOR of the challenger bits $b_1\oplus b_2$ to at most $δ^2+\rm{negl}(λ)$.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06017"></a>
### [Superconductivity beyond band geometry: emergence of pair quantum geometry](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06017v1)

**Authors:** Mehmet Akif Keskiner, Menderes Işkın  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06017v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.06017_figures/2606.06017_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Top panels: “Conventional” (band-dispersion and band-geometry) contribution (red open circles) and pair-geometry contribution (blue open triangles) from Eq. (4), together with their sum (black filled circles), as a function of interaction strength. Bottom panels: Exact bound-state dispersions (black filled circles) compared with the quadratic expansion from Eq. (4) (red open circles), showing excellent agreement in the small-q regime for representative parameters: U = 3t′ (sawtooth and SSH models), U = 5t (Hofstadter lattice), and U = t (fluorite lattice).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work develops a rigorous framework to quantify 'pair quantum geometry,' extending the concept of quantum geometry from single electrons to Cooper pairs in superconductors. By analyzing the effective mass tensor derived from Hubbard models on various lattices, the authors show that this geometric term provides a fundamental correction to superconductivity's properties beyond simple band theory. This advances our understanding of pairing physics by incorporating underlying lattice topology into superconducting phenomenology.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit derivation of a geometric contribution to superconductivity's effective mass provides a novel, purely theoretical lens through which to view pairing mechanisms beyond standard BCS theory, connecting topology/geometry directly to macroscopic superconducting properties.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research extends the concept of quantum geometry from single-particle Bloch states to two-body bound states and Cooper pairs in superconductors.

**Main result.** It establishes 'pair quantum geometry' as a fundamental, quantifiable contribution to the effective mass of superconducting pairs, distinct from conventional band structure effects.

**Method.** The authors derive exact effective-mass theorems by analyzing the secular equation governing two-body bound states and Cooper pair fluctuations using functional integrals and spectral decomposition techniques.

**Model / system.** The primary model is the multiband Hubbard Hamiltonian applied to various lattices (1D, 2D, 3D), allowing for analysis across different superconducting regimes (BCS vs. BEC).

**Key observables.** Inverse effective mass tensor ($M^{-1}$), pair quantum metric, complex Cooper-pair effective mass (whose imaginary part reflects Landau damping).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength ($U$), temperature relative to $T_c$, and lattice geometry (e.g., sawtooth, fluorite-like).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on assuming nondegenerate extrema for long-wavelength dispersion and using analytic continuation in the many-body setting.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the decomposition of the inverse effective mass into conventional and pair-geometry contributions across varying interaction strengths, alongside comparisons to exact bound-state dispersions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing single-particle geometry via quantum metrics, then extending this formalism to two-body bound states (two-body problem), and finally generalizing it to the many-body Cooper pair fluctuation propagator in the superconducting state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum geometry shapes the effective mass of Bloch particles through the geometric properties of single-particle states. Here we show that this principle extends to paired states. Starting from a generic multiband Hubbard model, we derive an exact effective-mass theorem for two-body bound states and its many-body counterpart for Cooper pairs near the critical temperature within Gaussian fluctuation theory. In both cases, the inverse effective mass separates into a ``conventional'' band-structure contribution and a new geometric contribution, pair quantum geometry, governed by quantum metrics on the pairing manifold, which becomes nontrivial when pairing is non-uniform across sublattices. In the many-body setting, analytic continuation renders the fluctuation kernel non-Hermitian, producing a biorthogonal pair geometry and a generally complex Cooper-pair effective mass whose imaginary part reflects Landau damping. Exact calculations on one-, two-, and three-dimensional lattice models show that pair quantum geometry can make quantitatively significant contributions to the effective mass. These results establish pair quantum geometry as a fundamental ingredient of superconductivity beyond conventional band geometry.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Barbell Codes: qLDPC Codes for Superconducting Quantum Hardware](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06062v1)

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes 'barbell codes,' a novel family of qLDPC codes designed specifically for superconducting quantum chips. It solves the major scaling hurdle in QEC by maintaining constant hardware complexity as code distance grows. Through detailed circuit analysis and simulation, it proves that these codes can achieve fault tolerance with significantly lower qubit overhead than established methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed analysis of stabilizer evolution ($L_X \in S'$, $L_Z \in S'$) provides rigorous mathematical proof that the proposed hardware structure can support fault-tolerant logical operations, which is crucial for any theoretical physicist designing quantum computation protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is implementing efficient Quantum Error Correction (QEC) codes, specifically qLDPC codes, on fixed-connectivity superconducting quantum chips without increasing hardware complexity for non-local interactions.

**Main result.** The authors introduce 'barbell' qLDPC codes, demonstrating that they maintain constant hardware complexity as code distance increases and achieve fault tolerance at physical noise strengths of $10^{-4}$ over trillions of cycles.

**Method.** The work involves constructing the barbell code structure based on tile codes, detailing a superdense syndrome extraction cycle using native two-qubit interactions, and simulating performance under circuit-level noise.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is superconducting qubits utilizing a Barbell architecture built upon a hexagonal tiling (6QSL+NLC). The mathematical model is the qLDPC code, specifically the barbell family, which offers low overhead compared to surface codes.

**Key observables.** Logical Error Rate (LER) per QEC round; Hardware Complexity ($	ext{Chw}$); Logical Pauli measurement fidelity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Physical noise strength ($p \approx 10^{-4}$); Code distance ($d$ up to 14); Overhead ($<30$ data qubits per logical qubit).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the ability to perform joint Pauli measurements and relies on the specific connectivity provided by the Barbell architecture for syndrome extraction.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the chip layout (Barbell architecture), compare LER vs. distance against surface codes, and detail the construction steps of tile/barbell codes using lattice surgery concepts.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the need for QEC on fixed-connectivity hardware to introducing the barbell code structure via tile code principles. It then details the syndrome extraction cycle circuit, proves that logical operators are preserved through this cycle (Theorem 1), and finally simulates performance metrics like LER and $	ext{Chw}$ to validate feasibility.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The major challenge on the way to fault-tolerant quantum computing comes from the insufficient quality of hardware components and the difficulty of scaling their number without further compromising fidelity. Quantum Low-Density Parity-Check (qLDPC) codes offer a promising solution by encoding logical qubits with low overhead and at a comparatively high code distance. However, it remains an open question how to scalably implement efficient qLDPC codes on fixed-connectivity quantum chips without increasing hardware complexity to enable the non-local interactions in their underlying QEC cycles. We resolve this challenge for the first time by introducing a family of qLDPC "barbell" codes accompanied by a realistic chip layout that natively supports all required two-qubit interactions. Crucially, the hardware complexity required to implement barbell codes remains constant as code distance increases. We provide a detailed investigation into the feasibility of all required hardware components and simulate a specific family of barbell codes against circuit-level noise. We find that, with a modest overhead of $<30$ data qubits per logical qubit, barbell codes can preserve information at a physical noise strength of $10^{-4}$ for several trillion QEC cycles. Simulations of logical multi-Pauli measurements, performed with circuits tailored to the chip, yield similar logical performance per QEC round, indicating that entangling gates between logical qubits in barbell codes can be realized fault-tolerantly.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05412"></a>
### [Unlocking Exponential and Unbounded Robust Gains in Shannon Capacity of Classical Multiple Access Channels with Causal CSIT via Quantum Entanglement Assistance](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05412v1)

**Authors:** Yuhang Yao, Syed A. Jafar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05412v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05412_figures/2606.05412_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Transmitter-side quantum entanglement-assisted classical MAC with causal CSIT.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05412_figures/2606.05412_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Additive MAC with state-dependent interference. Tx-k denotes the kth transmitter. PS1···SKZ denotes the distribution of (S1, . . . , SK, Z).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.05412_figures/2606.05412_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: The ratio of entanglement-assisted capacity to classical capacity, CEA/CC is illustrated for Channel B1, versus the number of users K.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proves that for certain complex Multiple Access Channels with state information and causal CSIT, using quantum entanglement assistance leads to capacity gains that grow exponentially or are unbounded compared to purely classical schemes. This demonstrates a significant advantage of quantum resources in enhancing communication throughput beyond what is classically possible.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a strong theoretical demonstration of how quantum resources can fundamentally alter the achievable communication rates in classical multi-user systems, offering insights into the limits of classical vs. quantum information processing in wireless channels.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work investigates whether quantum entanglement assistance can provide large (exponential or unbounded) multiplicative gains in the Shannon capacity of classical Multiple Access Channels (MACs). It specifically addresses this when causal Channel State Information at the Transmitters (CSIT) is available.

**Main result.** Quantum entanglement assistance provides a multiplicative capacity advantage that grows exponentially with the number of users K, and can be unbounded as the state alphabet size increases. These gains are robust even against significant quantum noise.

**Method.** The analysis uses information-theoretic techniques, relating MAC capacity to optimal winning probabilities in associated XOR games. Achievability and converse bounds are established using direct coding theorems and entropy calculations.

**Model / system.** The model is a classical K-user Multiple Access Channel (MAC) with state-dependent interference, often analyzed via an additive structure involving XOR operations over binary or larger alphabets. The assistance mechanism involves providing quantum entanglement only to the transmitters.

**Key observables.** Multiplicative capacity gain factor ($	ext{CEA}/	ext{CC}$), achievable rates ($R_k$), and error probabilities ($P_e$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of users (K), size of alphabet (m or $r$), degree of noise/depolarization ($\gamma$), and the availability of causal CSIT.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The gains are based on entanglement assistance provided *only* to the transmitters. The analysis assumes specific channel structures, such as additive MACs with state-dependent interference.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the setup (Transmitter-side quantum entanglement-assisted classical MAC) and plot the multiplicative capacity gains ($	ext{CEA}/	ext{CC}$) for varying numbers of users K across different channel classes.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds by first establishing the theoretical framework using XOR games to relate classical and EA capacities. It then applies this framework to specific, complex MAC models (e.g., state-dependent interference) to derive concrete bounds on exponential and unbounded capacity gains, concluding with robustness checks against noise.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum entanglement assistance is known to improve the Shannon capacity of classical communication networks but the largest gains noted thus far are rather modest (less than 6%), motivating the question: are large capacity gains ever possible? It is shown in this work that in the presence of causal channel state information at the transmitters, quantum entanglement assistance provides a multiplicative capacity advantage that grows exponentially with the number of users K for certain classical K-user multiple access channels with fixed size (binary) alphabet for inputs, outputs and states. Similarly, in the presence of causal channel state information at the transmitters, quantum entanglement assistance is shown to provide a multiplicative capacity advantage that is unbounded as the size of the state alphabet grows, while the number of users (K=3) and the input and output alphabet (binary) are held fixed. Even with only a few users and small alphabet sizes, substantial multiplicative gains in capacity are found, e.g., with binary inputs, outputs and states, multiplicative gains by factors exceeding 21 and 88 are noted with K=5 and K=7 users, respectively. The gains are robust in the sense that they persist even with noisy quantum resources, e.g., an exponential (in K) capacity advantage from quantum entanglement assistance remains available even if each entangled qubit independently depolarizes completely with probability $\approx$ 30%. The gains are based on quantum entanglement assistance provided only to the transmitters.

</details>

### [$E_\infty^{1,2}$-type Lieb-Schultz-Mattis anomalies, deconfined quantum critical points, and non-invertible symmetry breaking](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06343v1)

**Authors:** Hao-Ran Zhang, Hanlin Lin, Shuo Yang, Qing-Rui Wang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06343v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.06343_figures/2606.06343_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Effect of LHS anomaly components under gauging an Abelian normal subgroup A of G. The key result is that a nontrivial E1,2 ∞component produces projective A-sectors and hence a non-invertible dual symmetry, whereas E3,0 ∞and E2,1 ∞data keep the dual symmetry invertible.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.06343_figures/2606.06343_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Decorated-domain-wall representatives for a 2+1D bulk SPT with 1 →A →G →Q →1. Left: an E2,1 ∞ decoration puts A charges on Q-wall junctions, so gauging A keeps the dual symmetry invertible. Right: an E1,2 ∞ decoration puts a 1+1D A-SPT on each Q wall; its boundary endpoint carries a projective A representation, which becomes a non-invertible dual defect after gauging A.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.06343_figures/2606.06343_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: The peak of the MPS correlation length ξ(χ) grows with increasing bond dimension χ, indicating a continuous transition between the dimer phase near λ = 0 and the zFM phase at large positive λ. As shown in Fig. 4, the peak height follows a power-law scaling with χ.</sub>

</details>

<img src="2606.06343_figures/2606.06343_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: The MPS correlation length ξ(χ) exhibits power-law scaling with χ in the critical regime. In the insets, the peak value ξ[χ, λc(χ)] is plotted as a function of χ, from which the finite-entanglement exponent κ is extracted. The estimate from the dimer ansatz is shown in the left panel, while that from the zFM ansatz is shown in the right panel.</sub>

</details>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper establishes a general mechanism showing that specific types of Lieb-Schultz-Mattis anomalies ($E_{1,2}^{\infty}$) dictate that the resulting deconfined quantum critical points must be dual to the spontaneous breaking of a non-invertible symmetry. This is demonstrated using 1D spin chains and provides a powerful mathematical tool for classifying exotic quantum phase transitions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a deep mathematical framework connecting fundamental symmetry constraints (LSM) to the nature of quantum critical points via advanced concepts like non-invertible symmetries, which are crucial for understanding exotic topological phases in condensed matter systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study characterizes Deconfined Quantum Critical Points (DQCPs) associated with Lieb-Schultz-Mattis (LSM) anomalies in 1D spin chains, focusing on the implications of an $E_\infty^{1,2}$-type anomaly.

**Main result.** An $E_{1,2}^{\infty}$-type LSM anomaly necessarily leads to a non-invertible dual symmetry upon gauging the internal symmetry, defining 'type-II' DQCPs distinct from type-I transitions.

**Method.** The analysis uses advanced mathematical tools, including the Lyndon-Hochschild-Serre spectral sequence and category theory, to structurally characterize anomalies and gauge symmetries.

**Model / system.** The primary system is one-dimensional spin chains. A specific example analyzed is a dimer-to-ferromagnet DQCP candidate using a spin-1/2 chain with an anomalous $D_8$ LSM symmetry.

**Key observables.** The type of anomaly ($E_{1,2}^{\infty}$ vs $E_{2,1}^{\infty}$) and the nature of the dual symmetry (invertible group symmetry vs non-invertible fusion-category symmetry).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Central charge $c \approx 1$; the structure of the internal symmetry group ($D_8$); and the anomaly class $\omega_{LSM} \in E_\infty^{1,2}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on structural characterizations within the LHS spectral sequence framework and assumes Abelian normal subgroups for certain categorical simplifications.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 summarizes that a nontrivial $E_{1,2}^{\infty}$ component causes non-invertibility in the dual symmetry. Figure 2 illustrates how $E_{1,2}^{\infty}$ leads to an A-SPT decorating the wall, resulting in a non-invertible dual defect.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by mathematically characterizing the LSM anomaly using the LHS spectral sequence, establishing the general link between $E_{1,2}^{\infty}$-anomalies and non-invertible symmetries, and then illustrating this mechanism with explicit lattice constructions and numerical evidence for a specific DQCP candidate.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study deconfined quantum critical points (DQCP) associated with Lieb-Schultz-Mattis (LSM) anomalies in one-dimensional spin chains. Our starting point is a structural characterization of the LSM anomaly in the Lyndon-Hochschild-Serre spectral sequence: $ω_{\mathrm{LSM}}\in E_\infty^{1,2}= H^1(\mathbb Z_{\mathrm{trans}},H^2(G_{\mathrm{int}},\mathrm{U}(1)))\subseteq H^3(G_{\mathrm{int}}\rtimes_ρ\mathbb Z_{\mathrm{trans}},\mathrm{U}(1))$. Physically, this class decorates a translation defect with a projective representation of the internal symmetry $G_\mathrm{int}$. We show that gauging the internal symmetry in the presence of an $E_\infty^{1,2}$-type anomaly necessarily produces a non-invertible dual symmetry. This gives a general mechanism for type-II DQCP: in contrast to type-I examples with $E_\infty^{2,1}$-type anomalies which are dual to ordinary group-like symmetry breaking, type-II transitions are dual to spontaneous breaking of a non-invertible symmetry. We illustrate the mechanism using a spin-$1/2$ chain with an anomalous $D_8$ LSM symmetry. We construct a dimer-to-ferromagnet DQCP candidate, provide numerical evidence for a critical theory with central charge $c\approx 1$, and show, using both category theory and explicit lattice constructions, that gauging the internal symmetry yields the non-invertible $\mathrm{Rep}(H_8)$ dual symmetry.

</details>

### [BCS-BEC crossover driven by small Fermi pockets of a high-Tc cuprate superconductor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05653v1)

**Authors:** Junhyeok Jeong, Yamato Enomoto, Yoshimitsu Kohama, Tomotaka Nakayama, Kotaro Ando, Kifu Kurokawa, Soonsang Huh, Zhuo Yang, Toshihiro Nomura, Matthew D. Watson, Timur K. <details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<sub>FIG. 5.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The study uses ARPES and quantum oscillations on cuprate superconductors to investigate the BCS-BEC crossover. By focusing on small Fermi pockets within the inner $	ext{CuO}_2$ layers, the authors demonstrate a large superconducting gap indicative of strong coupling. This work provides crucial evidence for the pairing mechanism in high-$T_c$ materials by showing that the pocket state dictates superconductivity.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides direct spectroscopic evidence of strong coupling physics in a highly correlated material, linking macroscopic superconducting properties ($T_c$) to microscopic electronic features (small pockets) near theoretical limits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to resolve the ambiguity regarding Fermi arcs in cuprates—whether they represent large Fermi surface segments or small Fermi pockets—and to establish a microscopic foundation for the BCS-BEC crossover mechanism.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrated the coexistence of a small Fermi pocket and a large superconducting gap, providing evidence for the BCS-BEC crossover. Crucially, this crossover emerges upon *increasing* carrier density in the inner $	ext{CuO}_2$ layers.

**Method.** The research combines Angle-Resolved Photoemission Spectroscopy (ARPES) to map band structures and measure gaps, with quantum oscillation measurements (dHvA effect) to determine Fermi surface geometry and effective masses.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the four-layer cuprate superconductor $	ext{Ba}_2	ext{Ca}_3	ext{Cu}_4	ext{O}_{8}(	ext{F},	ext{O})_2$. The focus is on distinguishing electronic behavior between the inner and outer $	ext{CuO}_2$ planes, which exhibit distinct superconducting properties.

**Key observables.** Superconducting gap ($\Delta$), Fermi pocket size/location, ratio $\Delta_{	ext{pocket}}/\epsilon_F$, $T_c/T_F$ ratio, and the presence of a Bogoliubov flat band.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Small doping range ($<1\%$) where crossover occurs; high ratios like $\Delta_{	ext{pocket}}/\epsilon_F \sim 0.6$; and comparisons to theoretical upper bounds for 2D superconductivity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The superconducting properties of the inner $	ext{CuO}_2$ layers (IPs) are assumed to dominate or determine the bulk critical temperature ($T_c$).

**Figures summary.** Figures show Fermi surface mappings, ARPES spectra illustrating gap openings and band dispersions, dHvA oscillation plots confirming pocket geometry, and comparisons of superconducting gaps between IP and OP.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically uses multiple experimental techniques (ARPES, quantum oscillations) on multilayer cuprates to characterize the electronic structure. It contrasts findings from different planes (IP vs. OP) and doping levels to build a cohesive argument supporting the BCS-BEC crossover driven by small Fermi pockets.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fermi arcs observed in underdoped cuprates have sparked debate over whether they represent segments of a large Fermi surface or small Fermi pockets. This ambiguity has long hindered their classification as either the conventional Bardeen-Cooper-Schrieffer (BCS) regime or the strongly coupled Bose-Einstein condensation (BEC) crossover limit. Here, using angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy and quantum oscillations, we demonstrate the coexistence of a small Fermi pocket and a large superconducting gap in the clean inner CuO2 layers of the four-layer cuprate Ba2Ca3Cu4O8(F,O)2. This coexistence constitutes a hallmark of the BCS-BEC crossover and has remained elusive for decades. Despite the presence of antiferromagnetic (AF) order, the superconducting gap in the small pocket is remarkably large, yielding a gap-to-Fermi-energy ratio (Delta_pocket/e_F ~ 0.6) and a critical-to-Fermi-temperature ratio (Tc/TF ~ 1.3) that reach the theoretical upper bound for two-dimensional superconductivity. Unexpectedly, this BCS-BEC crossover emerges not as the carrier density decreases but as it increases, abruptly within a narrow doping range of less than 1%. These results provide a long-sought microscopic foundation for the d-wave pairing mechanism in doped AF-Mott insulators.

</details>
